THE BED PAGE.
YON KOTZE supplies a text—
“ The Ballad of Reading Gaol,” first
stanza,
“ The poor dead woman whom he loved.
And murdered in her bed.”
What awful banality, these last three words!
They jar on one’s nerves, and prejudice at once
against the whole. Reminds me of Heine's col-
lapse when a friend remonstrated with him on
account of his studied use of the anti-climax, and
presented him with the following travesty of his
inaiinerisrn (freely translated) :
“ The night was coming. But ere she died
She gave me one of her locks.
It lies in the drawer, right-hand side,
Just under the razor box. ”
“Don’t publish that, or I’m undone!” quoth
Heine. Still, his effects were cunningly premedi-
tated, artistically arranged, intentionally effec-
tive. But Oscar Wilde—well, there are many
similar breaks : for instance, stanza 58 :
“ At six o'clock we cleaned our cells,
At xeven alt was stilt.”
Reads like an omnibus time-table set to music.
And so forth!
+
And sermons come from various preachers—
Arthur H. Adams : Seems to me that objection
to a poet stating that a woman was murdered
“ in her bed ” —provided that she was not actually
murdered in the kitchen copper—w<mid imply the
complete exclusion of certain words from the
poetic vocabulary— surely an impoverishing of the
poet’s resources ! Bacon made a tragedy turn on
a fictitious cold in the head and a handkerchief ;
Wilde should be allowed to mention such a hor-
ribly prosaic article of furniture as a bed. In
fact, “The Ballad of Reading G tol ” depends
largely for its eiiect upon its naive air of reality.
The contrast between Oscar’s former delicate
feminine style (in which his characters exploded
in a coruscation of brilliant epigrams) and bis
present virile realism shows the chastening effect
of gaol upon a genius. If Tennyson could only
have been given twelve months’ hard instead of
the Laureateship, he. might have cast off that
fatal gift of over-elaboration and written us some-
thing with more red corpuscles in it. It is
“The Ball id’s” air of “having been there”
which gives the poem its full impressiveness.
Such a line as “at six o’clock we denned our
cells,’’ interpolated as equally important as the
details of the h inging, grimly adds to its horror.
The poet naively contrives to show that the hang-
ing is almost part of the prison routine. “The
Ballad” is written inside the prison, by a convict;
unfortunately, critics, as a rule, are not sentenced
to imprisonment for life.
+
J. Le Gay Brereton : In “ The Ballad of Read-
ing Gaol” Oscar Wilde takes a subject which,
though tragic enough, is also commonplace and
sordid. In fact, much of the force of the tragedy
springs from the ugliness and workaday nature of
the environments. We are shown no heroic
Othello surrounded by the pomp and circumstance
of glorious war, but a winebibbing guardsman
who has to “ swing ” for a vulgar murder. The
protruding eyes of sudden Death—horrible, sug-
gestive of infinite mystery—glare at us from a
background of unbeautifnl prison life, mechanic-
ally regular. Throughout there is a powerful
contrast of habitual routine witli the disturbing
irruption of Death. There seems to be little
reason in Yon Kotze’s objection to the lines—
“ The poor dead ivomaa whom, he loved
A:.J murdered in her bed.”
Why are the italicised words labelled “ banality”?
Wilde deepens the gruesomenesß of his effects by
bare statement of commonplace fact, and makes
his picture the grimmer by an almost photo-
graphic reproduction of absurdly familiar details.
Thus there is an artistic motive in verses like
these from stanza ll.
“ He walked amongst the Trial Men
In a suit of shabby gray;
A cricket cap was on his head,
And his step seemed light and gay ; ”
though to such critics as Yon Kotze they may
appear fatally trivial and paltry.
Von Kotze objects also to the unimpassioned
timetable—
“ At six o'clock we cleaned our cells,
At seven all was still; ”
but it is ridiculous to detach it from its context.
Let us only complete the stanza —
“ But the sough and swing of a mighty wing
The prison seemed to fill,
For the Lord of Death with icy breath
Had entered in to kill."
The horror of the one unusual presence is brought
out by this very insistence upon the unceasing
clockwork regularity and monotony of prison-life.
The spectators of the tragedy, the wretched con-
victs, are deprived of everything in which they
can take an intelligent interest, so that their whole
being is saturated with the hideous Idea which
intrudes upon their solitude and silence.
By the way, the minute pedantry which hunts
and scratches for borrowed lines, phrases or
cadences, seems so far to have missed one obviously
“ conveyed ” simile. Says Wilde—
“ And each man’s heart beat thick and quick,
Like a madman on a drum.”
Here the quick thudding is the only point of com-
parison, and the simile is incomplete beoause of a
oonfusion between the transitive and intransitive
use of the word “ beat. ” But about 300 years ago
genial old Tom Heywood wrote (“ A Woman
Killed with Kindness,” Dent’s ed. iv. 4. 23 25) —
“ Astonishment,
Fear and amazement, beat upon my heart,
Even as a madman beats upon a drum. ”
♦
Victor J. Daley : Well ! What if this feeling of
repulsion were precisely that which the author
of the Ballad intends to produce in the minds of
his readers ! But why have done so by means of
Bad Art? says somebody. The answer is that no
Art is bad that achieves its advertised purpose.
And the evident purpose of “The Ballad of Read-
ing Gaol ” was not to tickle the fastidious palate
of the Man of Taste, but to picture the gloom and
horror of prison-life as felt by a Man of Imagina-
tion, who had become a mere number in the gaol
registry.
“ At six o’clock we cleaned our cells, •
At seven all was still, ”
“reads like an omnibus time-table set to music.”
Does it? It is worse than that. It is the time-
table of the monotonous, dreadful, mill-round of
misery in which; within grim walls, human souls
are condemned to tramp day after day, year after
year, while, beybnd the walls, the stream of ever-
changing opalescent life goes eddying by. It is
the hideous regularity of the thing that makes it
so appalling. And the sense of this seems, to the
present writer, most convincingly brought home
by plain, bare statement. The present writer
also fails to see the alleged “ awful banality ” in
the last three words of the lines—
“ The poor dead woman whom he loved
And murdered in her bed.”
Othello not only murdered Desdemona in her bed,
but committed the deed in the least romantic way
conceivable — viz., by smothering her with a com-
monplace conjugal pillow. Yet, so far as the
p.w. knows, not one of the legion of Shake-
spearian critics has seriously objected to either the
bed or the pillow. Moreover, the bed-detail actu-
ally intensifies the horror of the crime. If the
soldier had slaughtered his wife in the open air,
or in some vague, indefinite place, the crime
would impress one as being merely a tragic inci-
dent, within the circle of a great horizon wherein
other incidents as tragic were continually occur-
ring to divert the attention of the spectator from
any one in particular. But this tragedy was com-
pressed into the space of a single room, in which
Murder reigned alone. The eye sees into
that room at a glance and beholds the figure
of the dead woman lying on her bloodstained
bed. There is nothing else to distract the
mental vision. And she was murdered in her bed
—which should have been her sanctuary, her place
of refuge, her last retreat. Nowhere else is a
human being so helpless, so incapable of self-
defence. Finally, the p.w. considers that the
realistic details scoffed at by Von Kotze are just
the features of “The Ballad of Reading Gaol”
which make that production so horribly human.
Without them—and they abound—it might havo
been the description of a damned soul shut up in
some imaginary inferno.
♦
“ Ishmael Dare ” : I’ve always understood that
Heine was a mocker, and now I know it. Fancy
Von Kotze’s taking that badinage seriously ! If
The Bulletin’ still circulates in Heine’s present
abode, he will be enjoying himself immensely.
It’s very hard to get a satisfactory grip of V. K.
He doesn’t dare to attack the use of nude fact in
literature every poet sinoe Cowper could be
brought up in overwhelming witness against him.
But he tries to distinguish between the poet who
is “ intentionally effective ’’ in his use of the nude
and the poet who isn’t : which (as we know nothing
about either poet’s intentions) comes merely to
this, that V. K. has been impressed by Heine’s
trick and hasn’t been impressed by Wilde’s.
Which may possibly be V. K.’s own fault.
For my part, whether Wilde intended it or not,
that
“ murdered in her bed ”
gave me a fleeting, shivery remembranoe of the
great murder in “Macbeth.” There, in the
tragedy, the point is insisted on, reiterated,
clanged upon us like a struck bell—“ Macbeth
doth murder sleep—Glamis has murdered sleep.”
Here, in a ballad, the phrase is as simple as pos-
sible, but the same touch is there. [lt might be,
too, that “ in her bed ” is partly explained by the
line before : the woman lying dead in the place
that had known their love.]
V. K.’s second example seems even less for-
tunate. Between the dawn and the death Wilde has
set for us two emotions —the grimness of mechani-
cal routine and the more awful grimness of the
silent hour that succeeded it. The minutes have
been crawling all the night: in that last silent
hour they crawl more heavily still. The momen-
tary crackling of dry sticks deepens the gloom on
either side. Again one has a far-away vision of
“ Macbeth ’’ and the knocking at the gate.
The weakest points of the Ballad for me are a
line in part 11. :
“ To dance to flutes, to dance to lutes,”
where the “ lutes ” give one a mental jolt by their
absolute unreality; and the third verse of part
111. :
“ The Governor was strong upon
The Regulations Act,” &c.
Thinking this verseover, the chief faultseems to be
in the rhytlpn ; the first and fourth lines are short
and jerky compared wit i those of neighliering
verses, and the uhemphatic run-on endings
“upon” in line 1 and “ but” in line 3 savor too
much of “The Walrus and the Carpenter.” Put
t in side by side :
The Doctor said that death The Carpenter said
was but nothing but
A scientific fact. ‘ Out us another slice.'
There’s not much to choose between them. But
this is distinctly owing to the rhythm, not to the
matter: tlie last two lines, about the chaplain,
are grim enough when one forgets the rest. In
nearly all real tragedies the most tragic part is
the ghastly incongruity of fact and feeling : I can
imagine situations in which an omnibus time-table
could be made tragic beyond description.
-V
Ed ward Dyson : I think Von Kotze errs in the
excess of his depreciation. If tin r • is a fault of
simplicity, Wilde may have slid into it, but
“awfu! banality ” ! Surely this is the verdict of
one to whom abuse is second nature. The second
quotation looks like a deliberate effort to provide
an excuse for the first hasty judgment. Take
the whole stanza, or only four lines of it instead
of two.
“At six o’clock ive cleaned our cells,
At seven all teas still,
But-the sough and swing of a mighty wing
The prison seemed to Rll ”
and the commonplace feel is gone. With the
context it never existed. Picking out half-ideas,
half-effects, and inviting judgment whilst pass-
ing sentence is easy, but what poet would survive
the criticism of bits ? Take an impression of the
Ancient Mariner—
“ The Wedding-Guest here beat his breast,
For he heard the loud bassoon.”
An author, like a mesmerist, demands something
of his reader, who must be a willing subject, be
content *to identify himself in a measure witii the
man who plays upon him. If you go to gaol with
the author of the ballad, and hear the clank of
the condemned man’s chains, and feel the hushed
prison atmosphere, and have intuitive knowledge
of the souls that inhabit the cells, then the
trite touches help to conviction—you feel you
are not in a prison peopled only by gentlemanly
embezzlers. Afterwards go over the verses alone,
hunting for a grievance, and probably you will
find dozens, but it is like examining an oil paint-
ing with a microscope. Of course there are limits,
and I confess Coleridge’s bassoon gave me an
intellectual jolt which I did not experience in
reading Wilde’s first verse. Possibly the jolt was
due to my inability to accept the bassoon as a
serious instrument, perhaps to my failure to seize
the poet’s idea. Tennyson’s oft-quoted triviality
closing “ Enoch Arden ” is agross error, inasmuch
as it diverts attention from a fine idea to the
expensive frippery and theatricality of a respect-
able up-to-date funeral. Tennyson’s art lacked
Attic salt; a decent sense of humor would have
made that line impossible. I find in “ The Ballad
of Reading Gaol” no reason to doubt Wilde’s
humor—humor being invaluable to the artist for
what it represses. Wilde’s descent to simplicity
never excites the suggestion of a grin, and often
carries the story and helps impressions.
Francis Kenna : There is no banality in the first
quotation. Neither is it an anti-climax. To test
it, substitute “in the field,” “ in the wood,” “in
the street,” or any other phrase of locality, and
see what a loss of effect there is. “In her bed”
is pregnant with repressed suggestion. It is em-
ployed with intentional effect, with artistic ar-
rangement, with cunning premeditation. It is a
real climax. Consider the materials of the piece
—a convict of a certain temperament, and the
prison system. Consider also the pent-up indigna-
tion rankling in the heart of “ C. 3.3 ” for two long
years, and then judge of the admirable temper in
which the ballad is written. The repression of
Wilde is a thousand times more eloquent and
forceful than hysterical screeching. The cruelty,
the horror, and the torture of prison life are all
there, in the lines, and between them.
“At six o’clock we cleaned our cells,
At seven all ivas still,”
would have been part of a musical time-table if it
had to bear the burden of what they did at eight,
nine, and ten o’clock. But the selective instinct
of the artist prevented any such catastrophe.
These hours were evidently selected with a two-
fold motive —(1) to carry on the impressive realism
of the theme ; (2) as a preparation for, and to
heighten the effect of, the succeeding portion of
the verse. And so cunningly are the two motives
associated that it is almost impossible to disso-
ciate them.
“ At six o’clock we cleaned our cells,
At seven all was still,
(Now you are ready for it!)
“ But the sough and swing of a mighty wing
The prison seemed to fill,
For the Lord of Death, with toy breath,
Had entered in to kill.”
A realistic poetic narrative of an experience
should never be more than an expression of the
impression produced, plus a degree of individual
interpretation. In the highest artistic produc-
tions the artist should retain his own personality,
or interweave it into his work indiscoverably.
When his own prejudices or idiosyncrasies obtrude
his work suffers. This is just the weakness of
“ Reading Gaol.” And yet the critic aware of
Wilde’s temperament and history isn’t in a posi-
tion to arrive at an altogether just estimate of
this ballad. -To judge it properly, you want
someone who has never heard of him.
“Q.” : Von Kotze’s objection is the essence of
exquisite egotism. The words “in her bed” are
not banal to anyone who can read in them a sug-
gestion of the passionate motif of the squalid
crime and the atmosphere of sexuality in which it
was committed. If such a trifle as that is to be
allowed “to jar upon our nerves and prejudice us
against the whole’’ of such a Soul-holt as Wilde s
Ballad, a holocaust of ballads is inevitable. Begin
with “The Ancient Ml urine?” for ttxe bathos of
the lines,
“ The Weddinn-Quest here heat hi* hr cant,
For he heard the loud bassoon.”
To please Von K. t he line should be altered to
And he cursed the old gossoon.
This amendment will make Coleridge’s poem
inhumanly perfect. The
‘ At six o'clock we cleaned our cells
At seven all was si hi
objection is equally paltry. Anybody who knows
a prison atrno-phere will testify to the importance
of certain tolled hours. Gaol in this respect is
like school : every item in the day’s time-table is
done to a prodigious hullabaloo of bells. These
two lines, simple as they are, suggest the picture
as I have vividly experienced it—the early bustle
of cleaning up anil then a sudden silence as all
ceases, and the prisoners, instead of marching out
to work, are locked up “ to sit in solemn silence ”
because a man is going to be hanged. But Von
K. has never been in gaol, and I decline to take
advantage of him. I’ll “ have at him ” with the
application of his objection to “Eugene Aram,”
which is given “higher rank ’’over Wilde’s ballad,
but which I venture to consider maudlin, melo-
dramatic, meretricious, and artificial in com-
parison. But to the objection : Stanza 1 —
“Four and twenty happy hoys
Came bounding out of school ”
Stanza 9 :
“ The usher took six hasty strides
.4.s- smit with sudden pain —
Six hasty strides beyond the place,
Then slowly back again ; ”
Stanza 19 :
“ A dozen times I groan'd ; the dead
Had never groan'd but twice !”
Stanza 32 :
“ Or land or sea, though he should be
Ten thousand fathoms deep.”
Why 24 happy boys ? why 6 hasty strides ? why
12 groans for the murderer and 2 for the mur-
dered ? why 10,000 fathoms ? It sounds like one
of Lewis Carroll’s mathematical problems in “ A
Tangled Tale.” Such carping is quite in the
Von. K. spirit. It could be done with almost
everything that has ever been written. This
supertine sensitiveness and magnified importance
of technique is not in the spirit of true criticism.

Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy ?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?

Various Verses.
Love.
From out the wonder-country
He rides whose name is Love ;
He little cares if sunshine flares.
Or shy stars show above.
From out the wonder-country
He travels far and fast,
And joy there is in hut or hall
When Love comes home at last.
His reins are Pride and Prudence,
His spur it is Desire,
His horse’s hoofs are shod with ! TopA
Well wrought in Passion’s fire !
No matter what the color be —
Or black, or white, or roan,
Or grey the steed that carries him,
For Love is lord alone !
There is no bolt can stay him,
He laughs at lock and bar ;
The winds are but bis messengers,
The waves his servants are.
He climbs the white Sierra,
He clears the convent-wall,
He clatters up the palace-steps,
For Love is lord of all!
Alas, alas ! the many
Who linger—even as I !
Who wait and watch the live-long night,
And hear Love gallop by !
And grey seems life for ever,
And sad the looking back ;
For come what may, by dark or day,
Love turns not on his track.
Ah, Love ! from out the distance,
I hear the ring of hoofs,
Now loud —now low—now fast—now slow —
And sure toward our roofs.
And comes he here to tarry ?
Or w ill he gallop past ?
0, loyal Love, my master,
Come home to me at last!
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.
At the Crossways.
After long delaying
(Go’den were the days)—
Days of sylvan straying,
A friend and I together,
Hand in hand together,
Faring through the weather
Came upon a lady
Standing at crossways.
A pale and marble lady she
With a slight smile gleaming :
Like lily ’neath a shady tree
In hottest noontide dreaming ;
And her smile was all for me,
Like a silver call for me.
Now, no more together
Go my friend and I
Through sad or sunny weather,
For our hands are parted,
And our lives are parted ;
And aweary-hearted,
The gainer and the loser
Am I,:and this is why:
The lady’s name is Duty, and
She gives infrequent posies ;
But, ah ! though fresh from beauty-land,
There's thorns among her roses :
And so you see wherefore and whence
I long for my friend, Indolence.
RODERIC QUINN
Aspiration.
Where upland rocks were bleak and bare
There 1 ose a sturdy tree ;
Its leaves in hue were fresh and fair,
Its branches strong and free.
Of other verdure all bereft
I saw the ridges roll :
How could thy crest the rock have clSfb,”
I cried, “ my l onny bole ? ”
It seemed,” an answ’ring whisper fell,
1 Beneath the stone I knew,
Though dark was all my tiny dell,
?et skies above were blue.
‘ And so through rending rift I drave
As ploughman drives a share ;
And, whensoe’er my heart misgave,
I murmured ‘ Heav’n is there ! ’
il And fired again with visions bright
I fought anew my way
For all my Being drew to Light
To drink of Life and Day ;
“ And so I burst n.y prison band,
My skyward path I made,
Till rugged rock and weary land
Might slumber in my shade.
“ And dost thou ask me did I quail
To rive my road to air?
\h, no ! How could I fear to fail —■
[ l-nrir that Heav’n was there ! ”

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in

SATURDAY, JULY 2, 1898.
Eight Shillings a Week.
A very small strike started lately in Sydney
under no particular circumstances, and pro-
duced no results worth mentioning, and
practically pegged-out almost as soon as it
was horn. The strikers were the “ deck-
hands ” of the North Shore Ferry Co. A
deck-hand is the unskilled— or not very much
skilled—toiler who heaves the rope, and
drags the weighty gangway from 4.30 a.m.
on cold winter mornings and up to midnight
on wet winter nights ; lie also scrubs decks
and helps round promiscuously. There
were 23 of them who rose in revolt against
their kind boss the co., and, as the manager
indignantly pointed out to the public, they
received the splendid remuneration of from
10s. to 17s. 6d. per week—all except one
Very Little Fellow. Th ■ Very Little Fel-
low did the same kind of work as the Big
Fellow, but the co. took a mean advantage
of his size and only paid him Bs. Apart
from the Very Little Fellow, the co.’s story
seems to have been quite true ; the aggre-
gate Big Fellow did get from 10s. to
17s. fid. per week. Seventeen of him got
10s. and one got 17s. 6d., but the
average of the whole crowd (includ-
ing the Very Little Fellow) was just
10s. 10id. per week. The average working
hours were 72 per week with no time for
meals. The men got their magnificent re-
muneration once a fortnight—that is to say,
it was due on Monday and was paid on
Wednesday, so that the wages were always
two days in arrear ; and they were sub-
ject to dismissal without notice. The whole
23, including the Very Little Fellow, after
vain efforts to obtain a rise, struck for the
very humble all-round wage of £1 per week,
and the infuriated management is reported"
as having described their conduct as “ das-
tardly.”
« * * •
The North Shore Ferry Co. doesn’t pay
these miserable wages because of its
poverty. It is a wealthy speculation which
regularly pays 15 per cent, dividends on its
original capital of £40,000. The nominal
capital is £60,000, and the nominal dividend
10 per cent., but £20,000 is really capitalised
profits. It has also a large reserve fund, and
the £1 per week which was asked for by the
employes would have made a very small
difference in the dividends. The co’s only
visible reason for paying such starvation
rates is that it can get men to work for
them, and if these men get tired of it
and quit—well, it can get other men. In the
Jolly and Fat land of New' South Wales there
are so many men—the strikers ranged from
very large-sized boys of 17 to full-grown and
legally-qualified men of 23—willing to make
an attempt at providing themselves with
board, lodging, clothes, boots, and some-
thing over wherewith to take their best girl
to Ckiogee at odd times, all out of 10s.
per week, that the co. really doesn’t need to
pay more. It is a solemn outlook for a pro-
vince which yells incessantly that it is the
richest province in the Australian group, and
a melancholy testimonial to the Jolly and
Fat policy of Freetrade, and to the artificial
prosperity that came of the great borrowing
dodge in bygone days.
* * *
Trades-unionism is no remedy for such a
case. Trades-unionism can do something in
skilled occupations employing a very large
number of workers, though its customary
w'eapon, the strike, is of little use even there.
The mining industry is the one where union-
ism is—or was—strongest ; it spreads all over
Australasia and employs more skilled labor
than any other occupation, and the miners
have probably more cohesion than the men
of other trades—though even that isn’t
saying any too much. And yet in every
mining strike for years past the miners have
gone down. But, as regards the small, casual
occupations, w'here little skill is required and
where the people employed are so few that
they can’t dislocate the country’s business by
leaving work, trades-unionism is just about
hopeless. These people can’t even strike in
the ordinary sense of the term ; it is im-
possible to create a strike-fund out of 10s.
101jd. per w'eek, so when they leave their
kind boss, the North Shore Ferry Co., in
disgust, they have to speedily go and
hustle for some other badly-paid employ-
ment elsewhere, and incidentally, by their
competition, make things worse for some
other poor wretches in other branches of in-
dustry. And while there are, as at pre-
sent, many hundreds of men willing to
take almost any wage because the poorest
w'age is better than none at all, the limit of
wage reduction is bounded by only one thing
the pay must be enough to exist
upon. That offers almost unbounded
scope for reduction, because ten adult
human beings can dwell in a very small
room—in fact, twenty can if necessary, and
they can refuse to pay the rent into the bar-
gain ; also the smallness of the sum for which
some kind of food can be purchased is really
incredible ; and fuel can be picked up ; and
the possibilities of endurance that lie in a
second-hand suit and a pair of hobnailed
boots w'hichare constantly repaired at home,
are almost beyond estimating.
* • * •
There is apparently no hope for the toiler
in casual occupations, and very little hope for
the toiler in any other occupations, save in
the enforcement by law of a minimum wage
for all classes of workers. In New South
Wales the trend of wages has been continu-
ously downwards for years past—within the
last four years the drop has been unusually
rapid. The New South Wales coal-miner
now averages about 225. fid. per week. The
list of wages paid in all manufacturing occu-
pations, published annually by the New
South Wales Statistical Department, shows
a further drop every year. It is not a ques-
tion, in New South Wales, of getting down to
the British level, but of getting below the
British level. Britain and New South Wales
are both run on Foreign-trade principles,
therefore they are both exposed to the com-
petition of the cheap laborers of all the
world. But New South Wales is close up
against the cheapest laborer in the world —
the Asiatic ; Britain isn’t. And while New
South Wales has to pay away about
£5,000,000 a year (nearly £4 per inhabitant
annually) to public and private foreign credi-
tors, Britain receives about £140,000,000 a
year (say £3 10s. per inhabitant annually)
from her innumerable foreign debtors. New
South Wales is the man who is in debt,
while Britain is the man who has a big
accumulation laid by and bringing in
interest, therefore the inevitable tendency
of New South Wales, other things being
reasonably equal, is to get below the British
standard. It is impossible to fix a decent
minimum wage which will be of any use
unless it is fixed for all occupations. And
in all the multitudinous occupations which
are subject to foreign competition, there
seem to be serious difficulties about fixing a
minimum of, say, 40s. per week, if there is
an expert Asiatic next door who is ready to
do the same work for 10s. and ship the
result to New South Wales per cheap
steamer at a freight charge which equals,
perhaps, another Is. The only visible re-
sult would be to kill these industries, and by
turning their employes adrift make the com-
petition in the businesses, which are neces-
sarily of a local character, more severe. The
minimum wage undoubtedly could be applied
to the Very Little Fellow who heaves the
rope on the North Shore Ferry Co. s boat at
Bs. per week, for that rope must be heaved
locally—to heave it at a cheap rate in Japan
wouldn’t serve the purpose in the least. But
if it is applied to the local boot and slop-
clothing industry, where wages are now
down to zero and sweating is rampant, the
most probable result is a diminution of local
manufacture and another big boom in im-
ports. Then the competition for the billet
of the Very Little Fellow would be all the
keener. And the trouble can’t be met for
ever by finding Government jobs for the
unemployed, and paying their wages with
borrowed money.
At all events, this paper votes for a re-
spectable minimum wage to be decreed by
law in all industries and in all the pro-
vinces. And out of respect for the Very
Little Fellow at Bs. per week it proposes that
New South Wales moves in the matter at
once. If that province is, as its politicians
allege, the richest of the Seven, it can afford
it better than Victoria, which is already
enforcing a minimum wage in all its leading
industries. If Foreign-trade creates more
wealth than Protection, as is so loudly
announced, thes .again it can afford it better
than Victoria, % South Wales finds it
can pay a decSfit mipimum wage under its
present fiscal system, then so much the bet-
ter for the system. It would be the greatest
and grandest advertisement Freetrade ever
had if it could stand the enforcement of a
respectable white man’s wage, and a white
woman's wage also, in all its industries,
whether they are of a kind affected by foreign
competition or not, and still show that its
manufactures were steadily progressing.
Anyhow-, the first duty of the State is to
keep its people from sinking into animalism
through sheer inability to maintain the
decencies of life. The only way to do that
is to fix a minimum wage. Whether a
decent, all-round mimimum wage wall square
with the political circumstances of the pro-
vince it is impossible to say till it is tried ;
so the only thing is to try it. And then, if
it doesn’t fit in with the political circum-
stances of the country, the next thing is to
find out exactly which circumstance it
doesn’t fit in with, and alter that circum-
stance forthwith.
The Demand for a Cast-Iron Constitution.
When the N.S. W. Premier, in the speech at
the opening of Parliament, started to define
the amendments which he and his anti-Billious
minority propose to “ cram down the throats ”
of the pro-Bill majority he included a perfectly
new complaint in his list of grievances. What
he desires in the matter of tnis new grievance
is that there shall be “ the same Protection for
the territorial rights of each State as there is
for the representation of each State in the
Federal Parliament.” It is already provided
that the limits of a State shall not be altered
without the consent of its Parliament, which is
a sufficiently cast-iron restriction ; but the
clause which provides this can be abolished,
like any other part of the Constitution, by a
majority of both Federal Houses and by a
majority of the people and the States. Premier
Reid’s further demand is that this clause shall
not be capable of abolition or alteration like
any other part of the Constitution, but that to
alter it there must be a majority of both
Houses of the Federal Parliament, a majority
of the people, a majority of the States, a
majority of the Parliament of the State which
it is proposed to cut up, and a majority of the
voters of that State. At least that is how it
pans out if it is proposed to make territorial
rights as inalienable as the right of representa-
tion in the Federal Parliament. The clause
dealing with this subject in the present Bill is
cast-iron ; the proposed amendment is thick
steel, backed with concrete and granite. Appa-
rently this new proposal is partly Reid’s
answer to the threatened demand of Riverina
for separation, and partly his bid for the
adhesion of the Brisbane Government. The
clause would assure Brisbane that all the
weight and power and military force of the
Commonwealth would be available to preserve
to Brisbane for ever, or as nearly so as possible,
its inalienable right to hold North and Central
Queensland against their will. And it would
make it absolutely certain that for any portion
of a province which has a genuine need for
autonomy there would be no way out
except by the bayonet. This is the phrase
which Reid’s own party applied to the present
Federation scheme and which is quoted as a
reason why that scheme should be rejected. It
applies to Reid’s own scheme even more for-
cibly.
J * * * *
When there is a demand for secession, as
there was many years ago in Queensland and
Victoria, and as there is now in North and
Central Queensland, and as there may be some
day in the Riverina and in many parts of Wes-
tralia and elsewhere, it is always the minority
which wants to break away on account of the
real or fancied injustice with which it is treated
by the majority. For obvious reasons it is
never the majority which wants to secede. The
dog which is on top is always satisfied with
things as they are, or if he desires a change it
is in the direction of getting the underneath
dog even more effectually down than he is at
present. The demand for a radical change of
circumstances always comes from the dog
underneath. No one can imagine Brisbane or
Sydney or Melbourne and the populations im-
mediately adjacent thereto, which populations
generally form a voting majority of the pro-
vince, ever willingly listening to the demand of
a remote part of the province for separation,
no matter how well-grounded that demand may
be. The size and weight and wealth and
consequence of these cities depend mostly on
the size of their attached territory. If it is
enacted that no part of any province can ever
get separation except with the consent of the
rest of the State and the State Parliament, that
means that no minority can get separation unless
it is a majority. And when it becomes a majority
it doesn’t need separation, for then it will be top
dog. North Queensland wouldn’t want to part
company with the arrogant South if it was a
majority ; it would simply shift the capital to
Townsville, and spend all the loan money in
the North, and have low railway rates in the
North and change high ones in the South lo
makegood the loss, and-let Brisbane lake a
turn at vainly howling for the right of seces-
sion. Therefore, Reid’s proposed amendment
practically means that there will never, while
the Constitution remains, be any readjustment
of State boundaries allowed, no matter what
the necessity may be. And that is too vast
and brutal and comprehensive, and too danger-
ous to boot.
* * * *
The only legitimate process of government is
undoubtedly by the will of the majority. But
even the majority may be curiously “ faked ”
by extraneous circumstances. If Austialia
were forcibly annexed to China, and then the
whole new Austral-Chow empire was blessed
with one man-one-vote, the four millions Aus-
tralians would be lost among 400 million
Chinese, and the blessings of majority-rule
wouldn’t appeal to them worth a straw. If
the handful of North Queenslanders, who never
had any voice as to whether they wanted to be
joined to South Queensland or not, are allowed
an equal vote, it doesn’t help them much
further forward. In the agitation for Victorian
autonomy, all the country south of the Mur-
rumbidgee was more-or-less favorable to sepa-
ration. But an outside authority fixed the
boundary at the Murray, and the Rivet ina was
thrown in with N.S.W. without its own people
having any voice in the matter. Similarly,
the people of far Western Victoria were never
asked whether they wanted to belong Lo Ade-
laide, which is near and convenient, or to Mel-
bourne, which is far off and inconvenient. If
any district deliberately becomes part of a
particular State then it is bound to let its des-
tinies be decided by the majority vole of that
State. If it is attached to a State without any
consent of its own, and is forced to let its des-
tinies be governed by the wrong majority, the
situation is wholly different. If a free and
independent community of three aboriginals
were to kidnap George Reid, and give him
the same franchise as themselves, it would
about express the situation. His very natural
reply would be that he believed in majority
rule, but he wanted to exercise his franchise in
a very different community, and to be ruled by
a different kind of majority, and to have a
voice in deciding which majority he would
attach himself to.
» * ♦ *
The proposed amendment would mean that
the welfare of Australia in general is to be sac-
rificed even more than it is sacrificed already
to provincial vanity and the interests of a few
big cities. It would mean that North and
Central Queensland were to be permanently
exploited for the benefit of Brisbane —unless
they grew strong enough some day to shift the
capital up North and take a turn at exploiting
the South instead. It would mean that Wes-
tralia was to remain a tremendous, unwieldy
province existing for the benefit of Perth and
Bunbury and Fremantle. It would mean that
N.S.W. would retain the power, by its railway
policy, to force the trade of the far South over
450 miles of line to Sydney and deny it access
to the port that is only one-third of the dis-
tance away; and to keep the far West a desert
because it couldn’t afford to furnish railway
communication with Sydney, which is far off,
and because it is contrary to provincial policy
to facilitate trade with Adelaide, which is close
at hand. And it means that when western
Victoria fills up it will be sacrificed for the sake
of Melbourne in exactly the same fashion.
These things may be good in a small way for
the present State capitals, and the exploiters
and financiers and traders who live there, but
they are very bad for Australia, which is of
vastly greater importance.
• • * *
The Bulletin is content to be Australian ;
so long as Australia is Australia, and is pro-
sperous and contented under a strong central
Government, the mere accident of .State boun-
daries matters nothing. It is of absolutely no
importance, provided the national interests are
served to the best possible purpose, w hether
N.S.W. consists of 300,000 or 159,000 square
miles, or whether what is now Queensland
is one State or three, or whether Westralia is
one State or six. If the division of a State
will hasten its development in any way, then
it ought to be divided ; the two States will be
Australia, and their people Australians, just
as much as the one was; and if our pride is
in our country and not in any accidental frag-
ment of it, then no one can be injured by the
transaction. Australia is a vast country with
few good sea-outlets. The natural tendency of
each part of it is to have its political and
trading centre at the nearest good outlet, and
if, in the utterly absurd drafting of the old
artificial boundaries, any portion of the country
is attached to a State which doesn’t supply it
with the best outlet, its right to secede should
be fully recognised. Also, if it w’ants to set up
a political and trading capital of its own be-
cause it is nominally attached to one which is
too remote to be useful, its right to secede
should be recognised in that case also.
* » * »
There is very little fear of the process of sub-
division going too far on the Australian main-
land, where sea-outlets are so few. No
district is likely to want to shake off its
allegiance to any existing capita! if that
capital is its shortest road to the sea, or its
nearest and most convenient commercial centre.
Provided the Commonwealth supervises the
apportionment of liabilities when a subdivision
takes place there is no possible objection to any
readjustment of boundaries which is geo-
graphically desirable, and about which the
people of the readjusted district are sufficiently
unanimous—none save parochial vanity, and
the desire of the newspapers and politicians and
statisticians of a few bloated cities to boast
about the size of “our” parochial territory
and the vastness of “our” provincial popul,.


tion For the sake of Australia in general it
would be better to see two prosperous capitals
grow up in Northern Queensland and two or
three or ten more in the North-west and North,
than that Brisbane and Perth should grow to
be two huge cities with a comparative desert
behind them. And the best thing of all would
be if the Commonwealth Government could
begin by wiping every existing boundary oti the
map, and peg out the provinces afresh on com-
mon-sense principles. But as that can twe >e
done the obvious course is to leave the question
as open as possible, and let it be settled the
best way it can when occasion arises, tor the
Commonwealth to begin by pledg-ng itself to
use all its power to for ever squash the demand
of North Queensland for autonomy, no matter
how good reason it may show for its demand,
and to squash all similar demands w hich may
arise anywhere, at any time, from any cause, is
too vast an undertaking. Also it is ghast y
foolishness to suggest that the parochial vanity
of this generation should prohibit the nee,< u
readjustment of provincial boundaries hun-
dreds of years hence, and that the dead barn
of the dead politicians who are buried and with
the worms should block the way for ever
But it is a huge and utterly unscrupulous bi
for the adhesion to the Commonwealth ot the
ruling gang in Brisbane, and the argument
about the rule of the majority, on which it is
based, looks specious. But, then, the mere
provincial majority isn’t everything. 1 iere
may be occasions when the Australian majority
requires to have a voice.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Mixed.
Sydney Telegraph’s latest cry of woe is that
the mission of Barton at the coming Js .S.v>.
election is to split the Foreign trade vote ar L < l
to let Lyne get into office by a side-wind. It
is a pretty crude lie, and shows that the D.I.
isn’t in good form. Barton goes to the N.S. W.
electors on the Federal ticket alone, and he
asks the support of the people as the most
trusted and most consistent leader of the
Federal party, as the man who has been a
Federalist all along—not a recent turn-coat
who took up the cause to save his billet, and as
the man who was definitely entrusted with the
job by the whole province when it put him at
the head of the Federal poll. If his party
comes into power lie promises to leave the
tariff entirely alone ; his whole object will be
to bring about the establishment of a fair and
workable Federation and to hand the fiscal
question over to it. He might have done
this already but for the things which Reid
helped to put, or absolutely insisted on put-
ting, into the present Bill. Reid goes
to the country also as the man who is
going to leave the taritf alone, except that
he is going to put a protective duty on
sugar. Therefore he has no more claim to
support on fiscal grounds than Barton. The
only difference between him and Barton is
that Reid asserts he is the better Federal
leader of the two. And in proof of that he
can proudly point to the fact that he
voted in the Convention for the three-fifths
majority, for the equal representation of the
States in the Senate, for the method of amend-
ing the Constitution —the one which he says
makes it a cast-iron Constitution and incapable
of amendment, for the arrangement which
leaves the division of the surplus after the first
five years to the Federal Parliament, for keep-
ing the debts in the hands of the States, and
for the railways settlement; and that he then
came back to N.S.W. and implored the people
to vote against the Bill because of all these
things which he had helped to put in it. In
fact, Reid’s claim is that he has been the one
consistent wobbler and unwavering turncoat in
the crowd. And Lyne goes to the country
on the ground that he wants two things—Pro-
vincial Protection, and a Federal system which
will abolish Provincial Protection ; also that
his first desire is to put up a fiscal barrier be-
tween N.S.W. and the other provinces, and his
other first desire is to pull it down ; and
he wants, above all things, both to get
the fiscal question settled in the local Par-
liament and to get it removed out of the
local Parliament altogether. As for the Labor-
par ty, it believes in Federation, and, so far as
the present session has gone, its members have
consistently cursed Barton, apparently because
he is a Federalist. Altogether, the election
promises to be a lovely four-cornered shindy,
and, when the four parties all come back from
the election, N.S.W. will need a ton of ice to
put on its head as it sorts things out.
♦
Want of Confidence.
As a howling farce, and a pathetic sham, and a
feeble slab of imbecility, Lyne’s motion of cen-
sure on Reid in N.S.W.’s worried and anxious
Parliament occupies a high place. It almost
looked like a pre-arranged affair between Reid
and Lyne —each of them knowing that a large
portion of his followers were inclined to break
away, and feeling, therefore, that it was advis-
able'to raise up the old party light, and get
them once more into the habit of voting on the
old party lines. Reid and Lyne were on the
same side all through the Federal campaign, so
it was good policy to get people out of the cus-
tom of regarding Reid and Lyne as represent-
ing one side of the province’s politics and
Barton the other. What both parties wanted
to impress on the country was that there were
only two parties, and Reid was one and Lyne
the other, and that there was no such person as
Barton —the man who badly licked them
both when all N.S.W. was polled at the Con-
vention election and again on Referendum day.
The motion couldn’t do Reid or Lyne any
harm. Lyne, to all intents and purposes,
moved that he and Reid were the two
individuals who must run the whole
Federal movement, and he asked the
House to affirm that it would rather
trust a Lyne Government in the matter
than a Reid Government. Obviously the Free-
trade party would rather trust Reid, and the
Protectionist party would rather trust Lyne;
if either crowd changed its allegiance it would
get mu equally bad Federal leader and a more
oli finable fiscal leader, so the parties were
bound to rally up on the old party principles
and close, to some extent, the breaches in their
ranks. If the motion had given the House a
chance to say whether it preferred Reid, Lyne,
or Barton, there would probably have been a
wonderful break-up—but it didn’t. The Legis-
lature was really asked to decide whether it
would prefer to be hanged or drowned ; and it
had no chance to vote that it would rather
remain alive than either of them. W hichever
way the vote went, one of the two provincial
leaders would be able to say that the country
had, through its elected representatives, de-
clared itself wilh him ; the vote was so arranged
that there must be a victory lor provincialism
either way. If Reid won, he remained in
office. If he lost, he went to the country for
its decision, and he had to go to the
country in any case, so no harm could be
done to either man, while both got some advant-
age by rallying up their disorganised parties.
Then Reid had a chance to point out that the
only man who had any right to succeed him
was his noble-hearted friend Lyne ; seeing that
he can lick the slow-going Lyne much more
easily than the alert and brainy Barton, he
naturally desires Lyne to he his only possible
rival and successor. And both sides got a
chance to slander and malign and jeer at Bar-
ton (who, not being an M.L.A., couldn’t be
there to defend himself) and to get their slan-
ders spread all over the country by the news-
papers carried at the taxpayers’ expense. As
Barton is the only man who is likely to
deprive Reid of his billet, and Lyne
of his reversion of the billet, and as
his party seriously threatens the seats of
about 70 members out of 125, the unanimity of
the Assembly in expressing its disgust at the
alleged awfulness of Barton’s character was
not surprising. And, as all the four great
leaders of the Federal party are outside the
Assembly, Lyne’s sham motion of censure gave
the provincial party inside a chance to do a
shriek about the sinfulness of the late Bill
without any serious opposition. Altogether it
was a mean and pitiable pretence. I lie two
men who professed to be fighting each other
for the Premiership forgot even to pretend to
hit each other in their anxiety to throw mud
at the man who was absent.
The Case of William Whitton.
Sometime in the middle of ’96 an ex-Queens-
land farmer, named Schutt, was robbed of
,£512, in Melbourne, by two confidence-men,
one of whom is now serving a sentence of four
years in Pentridge for his share in the game.
Lately a man named Whitton was arrested
at Albury as being the man’s confederate. He
was brought to Melbourne, committed, tried,
and found guilty. At his trial, farmer Schutt
swore positively that Whitton was the second
of the two men who had robbed him. He also
swore that he saw him in Melbourne a week
afterwards —which is where he made the mis-
take. An Albury constable also swore that, in
Nov., ’96, Whitton admitted to him that lie
(Whitton) had been present when Schutt was
robbed. Accused then swore equally positively
that he had nothing to do with the robbery,
that he had not been in Melb. since ’93, and
that, though he could not remember his where-
abouts on the day of the robbery, on the day—
a week afterwards—when Schutt said he saw
him in Melbourne, he was in the lock-up at
Germanton, 40 miles from Albury. Schutt
then said it might have been a day or so later.
On this evidence, the jury convicted. Whit-
ton still protested his innocence, and
said that, for some time immediately sub-
sequent to the Germanton episode, he
was employed at Clifton station, near
Germanton, and was present at the muster-
ing. Judge Hood, not being of a cocksure
turn of mind, postponed sentence, and directed
that further enquiries should; be made, the re-
sult being that it was clearly determined that
Whitton was present at the Clifton mustering
the day after the Germanton affair, and re-
mained there for some time afterwards.
Schutt’s evidence having been thus clearly
disproved, the Administrator of the Govern-
ment, on the recommendation of Judge Hood,
has been pleased to extend to Whitton
the prerogative of mercy ! After an in-
nocent man has put in nearly two months
in gaol, part of the time with the prospect of four
years’ hard labor hangingover him, the Governor
is pleased to show “ mercy.” There is no word of
any compensation for those lost two months
and for the anxiety and degradation after con-
viction. Yet it would have been quite as easy
.to test the man’s statements before his trial as
afterwards ; but, until instructed by the Judge,
the police took no action, whatever. They
weren’t concerned as to the man ; all they
wanted was a conviction —of somebody. And
had the trial taken place before almost any
other Judge, another Suffield case would
have been added to the long list, and another
man would have been rotting in gaol for a
crime he never committed.

A Syndicat.
The struggle between the Maoriland Govern-
ment and its great Buee octopus, the
Midland railway syndicat (the accent falls
on the “cat”), still rages, and the end of
the trouble seems even yet afar off. In the
genesis of the affair the Midland syndicat
agreed to build and own and work a railway
to the West Coast, in consideration of a tre-
mendous land-grant in Canterbury and else-
where. That was so long ago that the begin-
ning of the Midland scrum seems almost too
far hack for the memory of men now living.
The agreement provided that if the Co. failed
to carry out its contract the Government might
take possession of and finish and work the line ;
the net revenue was to be credited to the co.,
and it was to be debited with the cost of com-
pletion, and the bill for the amount owing by
the co. was to be presented regularly. If it
wasn’t paid, things w T ere to culminate, after a
long rest, by the absolute forfeiture of the con-
cession. The Midland co. w'as a gaudy fraud
from the beginning—a thing of champagne and
boodle and big c'gnrs and tremendous pomp
and glory and belllopper. It refused to put
its own money into its own speculation ; the
amount of capital actually raised was in-
finitesimal, and it was mostly spent in the
London office. The line was commenced with
money borrowed at high interest, and continued
with money borrowed at higher interest ; and,
as huge cracks began to yawn in the co.’s
credit, there came a time when it could hardly
borrow at any interest at all. Its liabilities
grew so ponderous before the railway was well
commenced—mainly by reason of the crowd of
expensive parasites living and drinking on the
scheme in London —that no possible earnings
could have made the Midland line pay. lhen
the doormats of the Ministerial oilioes were
worn thin by the big aggressive feet of the co.,
and the loud, aggressive voice of the co. blew
through every Ministerial keyhole demanding
concessions. It got permission to vary some
parts of the contract and to abandon others.
It collected its land-grant by instalments as it
went along, and howled for more land to be
granted in advance. It demanded large assist-
ance in cash and a guarantee of its debenture
interest, and wanted the earth generally.
Later on it stopped payment of its debenture
interest, and gave up even pretending to con-
struct the railway, and, when the State took
possession in terms of the contract, it demanded
huge damages and forced an expensive arbitra-
tion, in which it was most deservedly licked.
After that, it lay low for a wdiile and contented
itself with attempts to wreck Maoriland loans
that were put on the London market, and with
calling the Seddon Government a defaulter
and repudiator. The very latest phase of the
never-ending trouble is that the syndicat s
unpaid debenture-holders —partly on their own
account and partly sooled on by the syndicat
—demand that tlie Maoriland Treasury shall
pay the money which they lent to the awful co.
and which the co. blewed so royally in London,
and a stupendous law-suit is pending. And by
the time all these things are finished, and the
costs are paid, and the ownership of the frag-
ment of railway is definitely settled, and the
syndicat and the Government have ceased
dragging out each other’s hair over the land-
grant, and the old politicians who let the pro-
vince in for this lamentable transaction are
dead and their bones comfortably planted
beneath a five-ton monument, Maoriland ex-
pects to be full up of British syndicat. Leery
province which experiments with British
syndicat gets full up of it before long.
Another Syndicate.
It is a good sign of the awakening of a Demo-
cratic spirit in Westralia that the Government
inclines to kick against the monstrous monopoly
of the great Jarrahdale Timber Co. This awful
British steal first got its claw on the rich lands
of Westralia in 1874- In those days W.A. was
a Crown colony, and was regarded as fair
plunder for every Bull adventurer who chanced
that way. The Jarrahdale concern got a lease of
250,000 acres of rich timber-hind, close to Perth
and Bunbury, on the South-Western railway.
It uses this land for timber-growing purposes
only, and the rental is just £5O a year. It has,
among its still unexpired claims, the right to a
14 years’ renewal of its lease on payment of
£5OO, and another renewal of 14 years on pay-
ment of £3OOO. This carries its stupendous
monopoly up to 1930, and, including the rents
and the payments on renewal, the Jarrah-
dale grab will cost the grabbers an aver-
age of £ll2 a year, or about one-tenth of
a penny per acre per annum. The Jarrah-
dale co. blocks progress over 300 square
miles of the richest country in Westralia, right
at the back door of the capital, in a region
which, but for its presence there, would be the
most thickly peopled part of the province. It
is located there for another 32 years. Its divi-
dends go almost entirely to England, where the
boss vultures hang out, and where.the boodlers
are gathered together. It prohibits all settle-
ment within its lease on any terms, and keeps
the land a wilderness. It owns a private rail-
way through its domain, but it refuses the
public the right to travel thereon at any fare
whatsoever, though it is expressly provided in
its concession that its railway shall be open to
the public. The co., in its lofty contempt for
the sandgropers and the old sandgroping ways,
has simply and cheerfully damned the public
and damned the terms of its contract, and if
any ordinary individu il has business inside the
300 square miles which it owns at one-tenth of
a penny per acre per annum, and the co.
doesn’t " arrest him for trespass —well, he can
walk. The most it will do is not to charge him
a heavy rent for being found on its property.
There is no town on the Jarrahdale lease ; the
co. prohibits the laying out of towns in its
domain. This was good enough in the old,
slow days; but now Westralia is filling
up, and good land is wanted, and
£109,000 or more a year of golden revenue
is being lost to the State by the
co.’s big grab ; therefore Westralia begins to
kick. The one thing which is certain is that
the absurd bargain made by the foolish old
fathers of the land can’t last. It will probably
cost the province about a dozen times as much
to get rid of the co. as it ever received from it,
but that is the end of most syndicate trans-
actions. The average Bull co. persuades some
fool of a Government to let it have a decent-
sized principality at £5O a year, and pays £5OO
for ten years’ rent, and then has to get £50,000
to induce it to leave.
More about the New Guinea Steal.
The great Papuan land swindle is still alive,
and, judging by present appearances, it seems
an open question whether it won’t pull through,
despite the disunited opposition of this un-
federated land of fragments. There is no cen-
tral government which can speak with a loud
voice for all Australia, and the small falsetto of
even the biggest single province doesn’t amount
to Very much in such a case. Meanwhile, this
huge British swindle (like most schemes for the
introduction of foreign capital to “ develop the
resources of the country ”) keeps looking uglier
and more vicious the more closely it is in-
spected. The following propositions about sum
up the situation :
(1) Governor Hitt M'Grkoor has constantly and em-
phatically warned off Ausl ralian land-buyers on the
ground that large land-sales were “contrary to the
policy” ot the Papuan Administration. The present
deal stamps this statement as untrue. It is only land*
sales to Australians—the people who pay the cost of run-
ning British Papua—that are objected to. Land-sales to
British grabbers and Htealers are evidently all right.
(2) The Vink and Lowles British syndicate is to have
250,000 acres at 2s. an acre. It has also extensive powers
of additional purchase and enormous miscellaneous
powers. Till it gets its 250,000 acres nobody else has ft
right to buy. . ,
(3) The only way the Government can obtain land to
sell to the swindle-cat is by purchase from the natives. The
latter don’t want to sell. It has taken over 13 years to
buy 40,000 acres. At this rate it will take 66 more years
before the syndicate gets its full 250,000 acres. And till
it is satisfied the miserable Ausiralian who is paying for
the maintenance of the pro' nee can’t get a look in.
(4) The local nigger keeps asking higher and higher
prices for his land, and he will probably want ss. or more
per acre long before the contract is finished. Then the
Papuan Government will either have to buy land at ss.
an acre and sell it to the awful shindykitat 25., or else
give up buying land altogether. In the latter case, all
white settlement is blocked. Even if the Papuan Govern-
ment has to pay the nigger 20s. an acre for his land, and
sell it to the shindy kit for 25., it has to give the shindy kit
its full 250,000 a. before it can sell to anybody else. It
doesn’t matter even if other people were willing to give
30s. an acre instead of 2s.
(5) Somebody has to make up the loss, which will
probably be a big one, on the deal.
(6) The Papuan swindle co. is authorised to maintain
armed forces of its own. And the clumsy incubus who
runs the province actually forgot to specify the nation-
ality of this force, or of the co., or of the directors. The
grabbers may sell their rights to a Russian or German or
French co. if enough money is offered to induce them to
part, and the new grab may maintain an armed Russian
or German or French force in this British-Australian pro-
vince.
(7) The Queensland Governor and Ministry boss New
Guinea. They are Bill M'Gregor’s official superiors,
but it is specially provided by law thatN.S.W. and Vic-
toria are to be consulted as the other two biggest contri-
butors to the support of British Papua. And both these
provinces were illegally sold by the Queensland Govern-
ment, or some part of it, acting, presumably, in conjunc-
tion with M'Gregor, for the above'steal was put through
without either of them being informed. Therefore it is a
purely illegal steal, and N.S.W.’s “natural ally”—or
rather the ruling gang in Brisbane —seems to have acted
a pretty low-down part in the matter.
(8) The Papuan grab was hatched in England in the
Jubilee time. Some fearful job is always put up on
Australia when its Premiers go to England at a Jubilee
time. Lowles, Vine and Q. ex-Premier Nelson came
back together, and it is admitted that Nelson knew all
about it and cordially approved. Present Q. Premier
Byrnes also knew, and if he didn’t approve he at least
kept dark. Nelson promised to consult N.S.VV. and
Victoria, as provided by law r , and he didn’t. Byrnes lay
low and allowed N.S.VV’. and Victoria to be illegally kept
in the dark, even if he didn't actively assist in suppressing
the truth.
Australasia has liad a good experience of the
hig, benevolent Bull swindle-cat which brings
out British capital to “ develop the resources
of the country.” The Australian Agricultural
Co. in N.S.VV., the South Australian Co., the
Jarrahdale Co. in W.A. (with a lease of 300
square miles for over 50 years at one-tenth of a
penny per acre per annum), the Midland Rail-
way Co. in Maoriland, and the "Vine shindykit
in Papua, are fine examples of the blessings
which Brilish capital has squashed down on the
head of this miserable community. A law for
the summary execution of every British syndi-
cat which comes within 100 miles of any pi>rt
of Australasia would do a vast amount of good.

A threat is abroad that the gas of Justice is
to be cut off from Albury if juries there persist
in using their brains. N.S. W. Attorney-
General’s office has sent out a several-barreled
instrument of public disorder which Albury
juries will do well to reload and send hack.
One barrel complains that for a couple of years
past Albury jurors, serving at the Circuit
Courts there, have failed to find guilty in num-
bers of “clear” cases; another barrel directs
the 'Sheriff to revise the jury panel, taking
care to put on the “ intelligence ” of the dis-
trict ; and a third intimates that if more con-
victions don’t follow, the Circuit Court will be
withdrawn from Albury altogether. First,
what are “clear” cases? Probably, cases
“ clear ” to the prosecutors. But juries exist to
get between these and accused, and to see as
“ clear ” for the prisoner as for the prosecutor.
Second, what is the new “ intelligence ” to be ?
Is the panel to be made up of the straight-
backed, glossy class who believe that all men
in moleskins and corduroys are horn criminals?
Third, if the Attorney-General withdraws the
Circuit Court without reasons intelligible to the
whole community, Parliament will likely with-
draw the Attorney-General.

A drama containing some great possibilities
and a real steam-engine and circular saw is
“ Indiana,” at Sydney Lyceum. It is a rather
remarkable play, with streaks of tragic force in it,
and streaks of very depressing foolery and weak
irrelevance. A young candidate for the Congress
of that great land where the artificial egg hangs
out comes to Rising Sun with his beery political
mentor looking fur votes, and like any other young
candidate he falls in love with the wicked, lovely,
dark-eyed girl who is the mystery of the village.
The old German boot-repairer objects to the can-
didate because another man of the same name
stole his daughter 20years ago; and a casual work-
house girl drops into the story. Said girl admits
that she was fired out as being too bounceable and
not sufficiently humble for the workhouse. There
is a gap between acts just here, and when the gap
is over it transpires that the candidate has married
the very bad girl, and left her because she has
another husband living, and is about to marry the
workhouse girl, who has been tried and acquitted of
attempted murder in the interval; but can’t
wed her because she proves to be his own ille-
gitimate sister, and the daughter of the boot-
repairer’s lost child. Yet Act 111. finds him
married to her, it having transpired in another in-
terval that she isn’t his sister —only his cousin.
Also the candidate has suddenly discovered he
can’t prove that his first wife’s first husband is
alive and that his first marriage isn’t legal after
all; and he is in a cold perspiration about bigamy
—and all these shocks and weddings have hap-
pened so suddenly that the election is still pend-
ing. Certain uncouth, comic people named Haw-
kins, who had no previous connection with the
drama, cast a cloud over the candidate’s happi-
ness by coming to dinner in Act 111. and putting
their muddy boots on his dinner-table. He begins
to quarrel with his wife—any man would quarrel
with a wife who had comic friends that put their
boots on the dinner-table. And about this time
he tries to lock the first wife up in his mill for
safety, but her other lover—whom the second wife
had shot in the loose part of the back of the pants
—ties him up under the circular-saw, and while
his second wife is -rescuing him the first wife
escapes and harangues the electors so successfully
that he only gets five votes altogether.
• • » *
Afte* this the candidate packs nis various
nightmares in a carpet-bag, and goes wandering
over the face of the globe for three years looking
for his wife’s first husband, so that he can prove
that he himself was never legally married to her
but is lawfully wedded to his second wife. And
when he comes back in despair he finds another
unaccountable funny man crying for whisky on
his premises, and identifies him as the missing
husband. The chief objections to “ Indiana,” are
that it is no use for the candidate to find out that
the workhouse girl is his sister when she isn’t;
and that the girl’s coarse friends who come in and
put their feet on the table turn what was a very
fine drama into stupid burlesque. For two acts
“Indiana,” is far above the average; then the
cheap boorishness of the funny people named
Hawkins reduces it to the ordinary level. The
very bad girl of Miss Bensusan is one of the best
things that clever actress has done up to date.
She is a tempestuous savage with bright eyes and
vivid lips and vivid clothes —a mixture of the
tomboy and the bull-ant; and, regarded as
a bright, original, glittering Sin, she is superb.
Harry Diver makes a strong, manly hero ; and
Miss Watts-Phillips, as the good, plain, little girl
whom the candidate marries after he has abjured
his painted Sin, struggles coinmendably with a
rather hard part, and shows a good deal of girl
through a large tear in her frock. The rest of the
show, from an acting point of view, consists of
Frank Hareourt with the glare of countless cock-
tails in his nose. The Hawkins family requires
prompt extirpation. Its comedy is wearisome,
and its remarks about how it doesn’t wash itself,
and about its inflamed corn, and about the. piece
which the dog bit out of the least respectable part
of it, require to be put down by cannon. “ In-
diana ” never gets the show it deserves after the
Hawkins family comes into it and starts to be
amusing by putting its cowhide boots on the table.
** * *
By tbe time this appears in print,
Napoleon will have ceased tottering on his throne
at Her Majesty’s, and the Julius Knight co. will
be half-way through a hasty spell of the
“ Prisoner of Zenda,” and the “French Maid”
will be just about due. Bert Gilbert and Miss
.Ada Reeve make their last re-appearance on
Saturday. The Firm will probably be through
with this clearing sale of remnants in another
week, and-will then break out in a perfectly new
blaze of glory. At time of writing, however, the
Napoleonic play supplied enough blaze for all
reasonable purposes, and the roar of Waterloo
was still disturbing the peaceful drinkers in all the
public-houses for half-a-mile around.
*# # *
“White Heather,” a drama which is quite
new to Australia, starts business at Sydney Royal
on Saturday. Therefore, the guillotine will thirst
no more for Baker’s neck after this week, and the
red headed man will cease from troubling, and
Miss Gill, who is shaken to death on the sandy
coast of Algeria, will be at rest. The Bulletin
never knew any lady to be shaken more cordially
on the .coast of Algeria, or anywhere else, than
Miss Gill has been in Bland Holt’s late drama,
but it devoutly trusts that she is none the worse.
** * *
The Peggy Pryde boom at Sydney Tivoli
gathers weight every night, and by this time
three-fourths of the local push would sacrifice its
girl cheerfully—the girl with the red hat and the
large beast in it, and the high shoes, and the
cheap ring, and the speckled apple which she eats
between the acts —for one smile from the daughter
of the old-time “ Vital Spark.” As an evidence
of Miss Pryde’s popularity, there was a small
trouble in the gallery of another theatre one night
last week ; and, in the course of it, a gentleman,
who claimed that Peggy was the best variety-lady
who ever visited Australia, seriously bit another
gentleman who had doubts upon the subject. As
a real proof of popularity, this episode goes a long
way. George Beauchamp, the Terry Sisters, the
ever-popular Winton and George Woods assist to
keep the boom„nfroving, and to preserve the lustre
of Harry Rickards’ affluent top-hat unspotted
from the world.
#* * *
The “Powerful Organisation,” at Sydney
Royal Standard, has at last finished with “ Fun
on the Bristol,” and all that is left of that injured
combatant has retired into a corner to recuperate.
At latest advices the co. was heaping up a drama
called “A Cruel Wrong.” The writer, after a
long experience of melodrama, assumes that ho
wouldn’t marry the girl, and she put on a long,
dun-colored cloak, and took her shrieking infant
under her arm, and went forth to drop the large,
round tear of woe in the ash-box late at night. If
that wasn’t the exact nature of the wrong then
the assumption is that the will was stolen so that
the heroine and her husband suffered great priva-
tion until the fifth act, when the funny man pro-
duced the document from inside his dog-skin cap.
“Hands Across the Sea ” is the next production
at the Standard, where the huge cordial paw of
British loyalty will probably find itself consider-
ably crowded on the very small stage.
** * *
“Alcestis,” adapted to modern endurance
by Prof. Marshall Hall and Dr. Deeper, the War-
den of Trinity College, was staged at Melb. Town
Hall last week for one consecutive night only. A
revival of this unique experiment is promised for
next Easter. Ten months interval is a long time
from a financial point of view, seeing that the
receipts (about ,£450) from the first performance
didn’t cover the expenditure on scenery, dresses
and fixings ; but from a Greek artistic standpoint
the period of waiting for some more “ Alces-
tis ” seems not too long. Dramatic action in
ancient Athens was a slow and stately matter. It
moved at the rate of about one yard per minute,
and its charm lay in a classical determination not
to be rushed. In the diffusion of restful senti-
ment the hybrid musical spectacle at the Melb.
Town Hall scored a complete success. It breathed
the spirit of an age when civilisation cultivated
dignity as a fine art, and the first duty of man
was to strike an attitude.
* * * •
The “ Alcestis ” which Euripides gave to a
tired world in 438 b.c. was brought somewhat up
to date. Marshall Hall had operaticised the
tragedy, and it was acted on an early Grecian
stage to a flowing accompaniment of rich music,
interpreted by a full orchestra of 80 Liedertafel
choristers, whose singing from the sides was sup-
posed to be the outcries of 13 silent posturers repre-
senting a Greek chorus. The combined effect of
music, stage picture, orchestral lighting, and
measured movement in the play was very fine at
times. The present paragrapher, quoting from
Pinero, would “ wish to speak of great things with
gratitude.” Being persuaded that “Alcestis”
was rather a great thing, he is not disposed to
ask whether the performers spoke good Greek, or
otherwise. He knows little about old Athenian
language, or pronunciation, but he will swear
that the display filled him with a strange desire
to clothe himself in a sheet and stretch forth his
arms towards Marshall Hall, and the learned
Leeper, and architect Annear, who built the
solemn scene, and cry “ Hail to you 1 ” after the
manner of Mrs. Brough in “ Niobe.”
• » • *
Yet the fact that a work by Euripides
could be produced last week in an attractive form
doesn't prove its alleged worth. Old Greek
dramatists were allowed any amount of scope for
tragic narrative, and they made very little use of
their opportunities. “ Alcestis,” for instance,
is the story of a Queen who gave her life to save
her husband’s. The Fates had doomed King
Admetus to die unless he could find a respectable
substitute. His aged father refused to peg-out to
oblige his offspring, neither would any of his
uncles, cousins or aunts undertake the sacrifice.
So his wife, Alcestis, having taken a tearful leave
of the family, and the best bedroom furniture,
died in the kindest manner possible. Death is a
leading character in the plot, likewise Hercules.
Hercules, out of friendship for King Admetus,
who allowed him to get drunk in the royal palace,
engages in a Graeco-Roman wrestle with Death
for the body of Alcestis, and puts the “hammer-
lock ” on his adversary, and wins the lady back to
her husband. This crude work of imagination is
famous merely because it happens to be a relic of an
early literature, and the small minority of pedants
conversant with its dead language are always con-
spiring to over-rate the importance of their
knowledge thereof. Give the modern Pinero,
Sydney Grundy, or even plain Henry Arthur
Jones the license that Euripides enjoyed; give
them permission to introduce such stage charac-
ters as Death, Hercules, Envy, Hatred, the Sun,
the Moon, and the Milky Way into a tragedy’
together with a guarantee that the plot will be
taken seriously, and how would “Alcestis” com-
pare with their efforts ? Euripides had the luck
to be born 8.C., whereas Pinero and co. live in
telephonic times.
Several of the Trinity College students
played their parts gracefully. D. J. Bevan made
a picturesque King, whilst Miss Towl, a thought-
ful-looking lady, sang her dying remarks in a
strong soprano voice, and was duly intense. Miss
Lambert shaped well as a chief waiting-woman
who conies down the steps of the Palace and talks
vigorously to the chorus ; likewise, C. F. Webster
and W. G. a’Beckett left a good impression. It
is easier, no doubt, for intelligent male ama-
teurs to get through in Greek costume, with
the assistance of a long stick, than to appear
natural on the stage in sac suits. When in doubt
re the disposal of their arms they hang on to the
long stick and look classically composed. The
god Apollo was an unheroic libel on Greek sculp-
ture, and Hercules ought to have been half a foot
taller, anyhow. But as a set-off to these weak
suggestions of mythical items the management
could point to a spirited figure of Death by G. M.
Long. Death amounted to a caution. His painted
face, black robes and bearing were all right. He
walked more eerily than any professional Ghost in
any “ Hamlet,” and his appearance at the head
of the funeral procession was a supernatural suc-
cess. Nature must have intended him for an
undertaker. “Alcestis” was cut up into three
acts. Said Marshall Hall in a Proem printed on
the programme : “ The introduction of pauses be-
tween scenes—which occurred at the decadent
period of Greek Art—and which in modern days is
considered essential—must be regarded as an indis-
pensable evil, a sop offered to British barbarism.”
But there was no need to offer barbarism such big
doses of sop. Marshall Hall took about 20
minutes for each of his two decadent periods.
Presumably he went downstairs to suck refresh-
ment through a narrow straw.
** * *
John Lemmone’s new concert co. disclosed
its virtues at Melb. Town Hall, last Saturday,
and a crowded audience found it a select as-
sortment of artists. Madame Alva, the prima-
donna, is a tall and handsome vision, her voice
being tall and handsome to match. A mezzo-
soprano organ more suited for the leading part in
“II Trovatore” than for concert singing, it
comes as a surprise nowadays, and will re-
mind many people of the Ciuti and Tagalavia
whom Sitnonsen imported with his first Italian
opera co. a dozen years back. Samuel Masters,
the tenor of the co. (there is no baritone
with them), is also capable of making the
welkin ring with his top note on occasion. His
strong, sweet tones may lack something ol the
mellowness and “ breadth ” of Newbury’s popular
tenor, but he sings admirably ; though, as a rule,
the woids get clipped by his teeth ere he lets them
go. The combined ages of Madame Alva and
Mr. Masters can’t be much more than the indivi-
dual age of some visiting singers, and their throats
seem to have taken kindly to the new climate.
Lemmone and his welcome flute took a very smalj
part in Saturday’s proceedings. A very large
portion of the success fell to Miss Adela Verne, a
young pianist who may be expected to repeat
the Hambourg sensation. Miss Verne, in a
’Murkan way of speaking, is a “daisy” at
the box of music. She travels to and from
business in a cheery, unconventional manner,
and is a charming performer not less than
a wonderful executant. She started her Austra-
lian career with a Fugue (Bach-Liszt), confirmed
everybody’s good opinion of her by playing a
Be rceuse and Polonaise (Chopin) to perfection,
and finally she “ knocked ” the multitude into en-
thusiasm w th a Concerto (Saint-Saens), her sister
taking the orchestral part on a second piano. A
pair of sturdy arms and exceptional wrists enable
the damsel to put a man’s force into her attack.
She is especially clever with her left, and she has
her own particular method of slipping in her
right, but when she cares to tease the keys she’s a
true girl. So, taking one consideration with
another, the new concert company looks likely to
flourish. Only four concerts in.the first Melbourne
season—to-night (Thursday) and Saturday finish-
ing the series.

Nothing fresh at the Melbourne theatres
this week. “The Little Minister ”is leaving the
Princess’s on Friday, and on Saturday the double
bi lb “ Sweet Nancy ” and “A Bit of Old Chel-
