Girl.”
** * *
Darkness prevails at the Royal. Tither-
adge farewelled there last Saturday, when in the
snow-white wig of Wilfred Denver he stated that
he was going to the World’s Market with his wares
and would probably come back, sooner or later, to
the land where he has made his home, friends,
and reputation for 15 years. Tith.’s speech was an
effective finishing-touch to the Holloway season,
which had started in a quiet sort of way at Xmas
and gone on prospering ever since. The Royal
re-opens on July 9 under Rickards’ management,
with Cartwright and co. in “The Middleman.”
#* * #
The Bijou was to be resumed by Myra
Kemble on Monday last for a few more perform-
ances of “The Alhambra Girl,” public interest in
that breezy tale of mother-in-law not having been
exhausted when the M.K. co. gave place to the
“Perichole” amateurs. On Saturday they will
take some new forces into the business, and tackle
a weighty comedy-drama, “ His Terrible Task.”
** * *
John Coleman, and the stranger who dances
in top-boots, are this week’s new goods at the
Opera House. Also, the Poluski Bros, remain as
good as new, and the Tiller Quartetesses have
grown dearer than ever, owing to their approach-
ing disappearance. These are the last nights of
their four bright smiles and winsome whirls.
Biondi is coming. The exact date of Biondi is
not yet announced, but it will happen shortly, and
when it does happen Melbourne will get a thrill.
** * *
Harry Coghill’s show at the Gaiety is ring-
ing weekly changes on a number of old favorites,
and the little hall gets a full load of humanity on
Saturday. Another hall, St. George’s, will open
(July 2nd) with an opera company at less than
popular prices, and “Maritana” will test the
general feeling of the community re this new in-
dustry.
At Poverty Point.
m It seems that Bob Love’s alleged statement re the circus
tiger and the Chinaman are not quite according to facts.
The Chow, in the first place, was told to clean out the
animal's cage with a broom pushed between the bars,
instead of which he went into Ihecaire and unconcernedly
put things straight. Love, on hearing that the heathen
had no fear, decided to give him the run of the cage,
with a view to his “ working the tiger,” and appearing
]t 'sently as a Great Mongolian ’Lamer. But next time
]• Chow went in with liis broom he carelessly knocked
it against the tiger’s leg and the beast threatened an
attack. Whereupon the almond-eyed intruder smacked
him on the side of the head, saying “ Wlioffor?” and the
tiger slunk away dismayed.
The frequent presence of Miss Florence Schmidt at
towns visited by Premier Byrnes during his political pil-
grimage in N.Q. tended so much to add a lustre to the
proceedings that no one wondered to find the sweet
singer invited to take a hand at the tightening of the
silken bonds on the occasion of the great Warwick gift
shivoo. In between the bursts of oratory Miss Schmidt
gave the banqueters “The Minstrel Boy” and “Home,
Sweet Home.” Parliament does not open till July 22,
and unfortunately Miss Schmidt farewelled in Brisbane
29th June, so that her services cannot be obtained for the
opening of Parliament. It is a pity. The first night of
the political show would be looked forward to with much
more interest if the recital of the weary opening speech
could be interspersed with a little music.

The grey charger which so ably supports Julius
Napoleon Knight Bonaparte in “ A Royal Divorce ” and
poses so effectively that many people doubt its being real,
has a long professional career in standing parts. When
not extra-professionally engaged she is a good draw in a
Sydney cab, and studied and acquired the art of standing
on the rank by Sydney poor-house. It is claimed that
she is the oldest cab-horse in active work in Sydney, also
the most docile and intelligent.
“ Scribe ” : A Melb. daily says Miss Eloise Juno imitates
the Scotch dialect well, in the “ Little Minister.” Same
paper said, many years ago, that her dialect was not good
in the “Bonnie Fishwife ” ! A junior managed to edge
that in. The blood of everybody in the office ran cold.
He became a Distinguished Pressman.
“ T.’’ : How those auld sayings appeal to the Scots 1
Other night when Lely sang “Auld Lang Syne ” an old
fellow in the gallery stood with doffed hat and an expres-
sion of patriotic exultation upon his face that made me
wish I was an artist —or, better still, the Scot of
memories himself.
Wallace Brownlow, who seems to find more profit in
hotelkeeping than in personating gay Henry of Navarre,
is the vocal idol of Weslralians. If any singer from the
East, or elsewhere, ventures along, some prosperous
mining-man is certain to back Wallace B. to sing the
head off the newcomer, whether tenor, bass, or baritone.
And the local people seem to regard Brownlow’s refusals
as the acme of foolishness.
“T.D.” : To give entertainments on the Lely system
would mean speedy ruination if one employed his natural
voice in the lecture portion. Lely talks falsetto, as oper-
atic tenors generally do. But even that trick must have
its evil effects upon the singing production, ’’alk or sing
—man cannot serve two masters.
George Beauchamp, of the<London ’alls, had to appear
at Sydney Tivoli an hour or two after arrival, and
knocked the gods with a broad item. But the newcomer
did not understand essential differences between this
country and Cockneydom, and an appeal that the ladies
should sing the refrain by themselves resulted in a con-
vincing silence. The Australienne doesn’t mind listening,
but—oh dear, no—she wouldn’t repeat the rorty thing
for worlds.
Add to the list of doctors who have taken to the stage,
Frank MaeVioars, who plays the part of chief elder in
“The Little Minister”at Melb. Princess. Mac was for
some years house-surgeon at St. George’s Hospital, Lon-
don, but abandoned medicine for mumming.
The personage who scribbles musical criticism in
Melbourne Age (writes a Bulletin correspondent) alleges
that Marshall Hall plagiarised the opening of Beethoven’s
Funeral March (in the Eroica Symphony) in his “ Alcestis”
music. The silly man had not sense enough to under-
stand that the very obvious suggestion was deliberate,
and intended to heighten the sombre effect. Wagner
“ plagiarises ” a German folk song in Die Meistersiriger,
Mendelssohn “ plagiarises ” in the same way in the
Scottish symphony, and Beethoven himself, in the Eroica,
“plagiarised” old melodies and worked upon them.
This sort of “plagiarism” is the compliment an artist
pays to ihe greatness of his predecessors and the intelli-
gent imagination of his audience ; but little criticaster
persons think they discover theft, or lack of originality in
it. Perhaps a member of the orchestra ought to be set
apart to chalk quotation-marks on a blackboard when
such passages are played.
A. Willard was the original Spider in “Silver King,”
and now his namesake has been playing the part at
Melb. Royal. But where there’s a Willard there isn’t
always the same way.
A Silver-Salver Ballet is expected to make a big hit in
the coming “American Girl’’show. The salvers, imported
from ’Ome, are tuned to certain notes, and a pretty
musical effect is got by the dancers clashing them.
Unmistakeable evidence that W. and M. have scored a
solid London success with “ The Belle of New York ” at
the Shaftesbury is found in the fact of Cissie Loftus
(at the Alhambra) mimicking various members of the
“ Belle” company.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
They do things differently in the United States.
When the 3 per cent, war loan of £120,000,000 was issued
lately, it was cut into bonds of t's and upwards, so as to
reach the very small capitalists of the community, and
applications for bonds were received at every post-office
in the States. Also, these securities were exempt from
all State, municipal, and local taxation whatever. That
is a strong reason why the securities will always have a
tendency to remain in’ the republic. Taken to any other
country they become at once subject to that country s
income and property taxes, therefore they will depre-
ciate in value ; the place where they are exempt from tax
is the one where their value must be highest. They do
things a lot otherwise in Australia. Here the State
securities that are held locally have income-tax charged
on the interest, while those that are held abroad are
exempt. It is the blessed absentee thfit Australia yearns
after ; the mere local Australian doesn’t count. There is
certainly a 1 t to be said for the Yankee idea of exempt-
ing loja ,y held State securities from all taxation. Any-
thing that encourages Australia to be its own creditor,
and tends to reduce the lien that Bull has on its body and
soul, has advantages about it.
» » * •
Under Sunday Brunker’s promised N.S.W.
Friendly Societies Bill it is stated that “ provision is made
for the appointment of a responsible person as inspector
and examiner of the accounts of the various societies.”
In other words, there is to be a State audit of friendly
societies’ accounts. Why not of life-insurance accounts
also ?
* * * *
The National Bank of Tasmania, a respectable
little concern which never boomed much (its biggest div.
was 8 per cent.), or suspended, or reconstructed, or did
anything very unusual, reports a profit for the half-year
ended 31st May of £4129. This is equal to about £4 14s.
Bd. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds, which
is above the usual average of bank earnings nowadays.
The profits of this steadv-going little concern for the last
few years have been as follows :
30th Nov., 1892 .. £5382 30th Nov., 1895 .. £4136
31st May, 1893 .. 4776 31st May, 1896 .. 4299
30th Nov., 1893 .. 4701 30th Nov., 1896 .. 3534
31st Mav, 1894 .. 4618 31st May, 1897 .. 4012
30th Nov., 1894 .. 4695 30th Nov., 1597 .. 4127
31st May, 1895 .. 3989 31st May, 1898 .. 4129
These figures look as if the National of Tas-
mania had got round the rather bad corner which it
struck at the end of ’96. The usual 5 per cent. div. and
income-tax absorb £3991, leaving £l3B to the good. The
business shows an increase all round, and it is con-
siderably larger than it was in ’93, which is con-
trary to the usual experience of Australian banks.
The cash reserve (tT16,074) equals 28 per cent,
of all liabilities to the public ; this is distinctly above the
average, and the National of Tasmania looks a very sound
institution all round. Its weakest point is the reserve-
fund, which only amounts to £22,500 ; and the profit-and-
loss balance of £lB7O makes a total accumulation of
£24,370 as the result of a blameless existence of 13 years.
In ’93 the reserve stood as high as 37,500, but £15,000
was written-off to cover losses. Since then the National,
through paying 6 per cent, when it should only have paid
5, or 5 per cent, when the case only justified 4, has cut
things so fine that it has only accumulated £3O0 —which
isn’t much to show for four years, and is an exceedingly
poor attempt to repair the big hole in the fund and to
provide against future possibilities. Also, the humble
£22,500 isn’t separately invested in State securities, but is
buried in the general business, where reserves are very
hard to find when wanted.
• • • *
The Maoriland Government Life Insurance
Department still keeps, in point of size, a good second
place (next to the A.M.P. Society) among Australasian
life offices. Its business grows steadily in good times and
bad, and, considering that it is restricted to one small
province with a population of some 700,000, whereas its
rivals spread over all the Seven Provinces, and some of
them over all the world as well, the steady growth is
remarkable. Some figures of the M.L. Government Insur-
ance Department are as follows :
Premiums. Interest. Total Income.
1891 .. £234,496 £96,399 £330,895
1892 .. 241,965 100,651 342,616
1893 .. 253,574 108,109 361.653
1894 .. 254,204 115.274 369,478
1895 .. 266,032 119,980 386,012
1896 .. 270,720 124,265 394,985
1897 .. 269,688 128,433 398,121
The Maoriland Govt. Department is thus the
only one among the six large life assurance offices of Aus-
tralasia which can claim that its interest earnings and
its total income have both grown steadily larger every
year for the last seven years. Last year it added £133,764
to its funds, making them £2,725,106. The interest
earned on its invested funds during the year was £4
16s. 7d. per cent., which is a shade above the average for
the Australasian life societies. The expenses absorbed
£l4 17s. 2d. per cent, of the total income, a smaller pro-
portion than any other Australasian office, the A.M.P.
Society only excepted. The assets, too, look remarkably
sound. The M.L. Government Department has a far
larger proportion of its assets in State securities than
any other office of the Seven Provinces, and these
are the only securities (barring loans on the surrender
value of policies) which are practically free from risk of
depreciation. The item, “ Properties acquired by fore-
closure ” represents only £24,867, which is a remarkably
small item against £933,000 worth of mortgages, and
says a great deal for the good condition of the securities,
arid overdue interest is only £482. Altogether the
Department shows the best record among the life-assur-
ance institutions of Australasia.
It would be interesting to know if there is any
valid reason why this institution should not extend its
business at least as far as Sydney and Melbourne. At
present its operations are confined lo one small province,
and it has to compete on its own narrow ground with a
number of Australian and other private institutions which
have the whole world to do business in. Even under
these disabilities—disabilities from which no other office
suffers—it has put up a remarkable record, but the pre-
sent writer can see no reason why it shouldn’t spread
itself a little further. The M.L. Department is the only
office which is both State-audited and guaranteed by the
State, and in addition to these two advantages, which
make it vastly more reliable than the very best privately-
managed institution ever can be, itcharges lowerpremiuins
than the great multitude of private offices. And, if the
slow-going Governments of Australia won’t extend the
advantage of State-guaranteed insurance to their pro-
vinces, an extension of the M.L. Department to Australia
would be a blessing of no small dimensions.
• * * *
The Scottish Metropolitan Life Assurance Co.
publishes a balance-sheet, particulars of which appear in
the Insurance and Banking Record. This little pro-
prietary concern came all the way from Scotland, for
some reason not fully explained, to do business in Aus-
tralia, but it is so very small and weak that it is a wonder
it didn’t die and get thrown overboard on the voyage.
The whole income of the life department for 1897 was
£77,083, and of this £29,172 was added to the funds, mak-
ing them £333,001. The Scottish Metropolitan is 22 years
old, so its progress isn’t very rapid. Expenses in the life
department, including dividend to shareholders, were
over 17 per cent, of the total income—a much heavier
expense rate than that of either the A.M.P. or Maoriland
Government Department. The accident department
had an income of £29,569, and saved £1157
of it, and at the end of the year the whole
accident insurance fund was only £5 t(i4. Com-
pared to a concern like the N.Z. Accident Co., with
its £38,000 of reserves, this looks like a very small finan-
cial fly-speck on the far horizon. The Scottish Metro-
politan, at its last three investigations—in 1885, 1888 and
1893 —was apparently unable to declare any bonus. Its
surplus in 1885 was £4030. In 1888, apparently by de-
claring no bonus, it managed to increase this little margin
to £4196. In 1893, its surplus had dropped to £2342, so,
seemingly, the life-assurance business during the inter-
vening five years was an actual loss. And to produce
even these very humble results it had to estimate future
interest on its invested funds at 4 per cent.—a rate that is
beginning to be regarded as too high in Australia, and is
much too high in Britain. The Record announces
that it is now proposed to issue 10,000 new £5
shares at a premium of £2 10s., and, if the
transaction conies off, this will leave a profit of £25,000.
Part of this, or possibly all of it, is to be put into the life
assurance fund—presumably so as to enable the co. to re-
duce its estimated future interest to 3.1 per cent., or some
other reasonable figure. The Bulletin has no idea how
this transaction is getting on, but it seems a pretty big
contract to get £7 10s. for £5 shares in a co. which only
yields 6 per cent., and which, with so much more capital
to pay dividends on, probably won’t pay 6 per cent, in the
future. And, if the life assurance fund can’t stand on its
own feet without getting assistance in the shape of a big
contribution from shareholders, it is very bad for the
fund. The Scottish Metropolitan’s next investigation is
due at the end of the present year, and the above financial
device, coming just now, vaguely suggests that it is con-
sidered necessary to do something rather decided before
then. Altogether, there are no very cheering features
about the Scottish Metropolitan, which looks a weak con-
cern of no particular promise.
The Liverpool and London and Globe, a large
English co. which does life business in Australia, and over
most of the world as well, publishes a prosperous-looking
balance-sheet. The premium-income for 1897 was 6242,140,
and interest on investments yielded £140,050, making a
total income of £382,220. The life-insurance fund, at the
end of the year, was £3,719,260, and the annuity fund was
£1,470,985. The interest realised on the funds during the
year was £3 16s. 7d. per cent., which is a reasonably good
figure as things go in Britain, especially considering that
the Liverpool and London and Globe has only a very
small proportion of its funds in mortgages, and invests
mostly in safe but low-interest-bearing liquid securities.
A particularly good feature is that expenses are kept down
to about £9 ss. 4d. per cent, of the total income, which is
very close to the A.M.P.’s figure of £8 15s. per cent., and
much below the record of any other Australian office.
* * * *
A gloom has been cast on Melb. lawyerdom.
Two Supreme Court Judges have recently said straight
out that they, at least, will have no monkeying in con-
nection with opposition to wills. It has been the fashion
to lodge a caveat against probate on the flimsiest of
grounds, work up a lengthy case, and get the costs of
both sides paid out of the estate. Justice a’Becket lately
came down heavily on certain obstructionists, and the
other day Justice Williams went one better : “ In any
cases I have to try, and where I find just and equitable
wills unnecessarily opposed, I will make the unsuccessful
party pay the costs of both sides, as I have no hesitation
in doing in this one. It is quite time the frittering away
of these small estates was put a stop to.”

Now’s the time —when a solid English demand
for Australian oranges is beginning to make itself felt—
for the exercise of that stringent care in selection and
packing without which a really good thing will be knocked
on the head. Present season’s demand from N.S.W. is
expected to total 30,000 cases ! S. A. and Westralia, which
grow splendid oranges, should have a small pull over
N.S.W. in the orange-export trade by reason of their closer
proximity to Europe. ,
“ Texas ” : Re no Chinese making money out of
tobacco in Australia. On this river (the Dumaresq, Q.)
there are hundreds of them. Not only do they make
money themselves, but they make what you might call
mean whites rich also. We have several who drive their
buggies here and never do a day’s work —they let the
Chink do it for them. Set Chinamen to grow, say, 10
acres of tobacco for you and give you from one-fifth to one-
third according to circumstances, and you can sit back and
smile from ear to ear. Tobacco sold here this season up
to 7£d. per lb., and it is a poor crop that will not run half
a ton to acre. I have seen a ton per acre. The Chow has
almost completely monopolised the tobacco business here.
The chairman of the S. A. Fire Brigades Board
(Mr. T. H. Smeaton) has publicly announced himself in
favor of compulsory fire insurance under Govt, auspices.
There is a special significance in this fact because he is the
Govt, nominee on the Board.
* * » *
The Kingston Govt.’s proposed establishment of
a State fire-insurance department—the Bill is now being
introduced—is welcomed by Adelaide merchants, who
complain that since the death of the S. A. Insurance Co.—
the last of the local co’s—foreign institutions have com-
bined to greatly increase rates.
A money-lender who provided the powder to
enable a speculative litigant to sue an Australian news-
paper for damages for alleged libel wept a year’s grime off
his face when the jurv returned a verdict for defend-
ant. “ The first time,” he sobbed, “ I do dot risky pizness,
but no more, no more, any times.”
• * * *
“ Rap.” : That the Louis-Napoleonic-looking
David Henry’s funeral-procession last Sunday contained
every hook-nosed “ financier” in Melb. is most probably
due to the fact that nine-tenths of them wanted to make
sure that David had been buried beyond fear of resurrection.
For years past he has been a thorn in the sides of the
lenders, who yearn for the young and inexperienced bor-
rowers “ mit eggspegdations ” who desire to go the pace
at any cost. That advertising was the keystone of his
success he freely acknowledged. What traveller is not
acquainted with the pictorial ads. on the Melb. railway-
stations ? Of the many, perhaps “ The Blind Beggar ” is
about the most familiar and the most appropriate for
those who took advantage of his “ money-to-lend ” invi-
tations.
That the ex-church deacon who backed and pro-
fited by Henry in his early days was absent, is not sur-
prising ; but there are scores of Melb. men vyho believe
that David often spoke truly when, in pressing for his
usury, he pleaded that he would willingly let them off
more easily but that the money wasn’t his, and that, his
principal insisted on the full pound of flesh. St. Kilda
will miss the two-horsed covered landau which every Sun-
day was tooled to the Bluff by a gold-laoed coachman’
and which invariably returned via St. Kilda Esplanade in
the nick of time to see the scores of his well-dressed bor-
rowers taking an airing after church.
An idiosyncrasy of Henry’s was his invariable
practice of getting a receipt for cash, and a signature on
the back of his cheque as well, for any moneys paid, and
then to take the cheque back and give casli for it. In
many quarters there is a “ funk,” lest tire executors
should claim on both receipt for cash and receipt on
cheque. Anyhow, if his “business” happens to be
wound-up, there will be trouble amongst those who have
for years past simply “ renewed.” In his favor, it may
be said that he worked his way up from a sailor,
that he was once the best amateur swimmer in Melb.,
saved many lives from drowning, and never forgot a favor.
His estate, it is said, will probably be worth £BO,OOO.
« * * •
There seems to have been very slight solid
foundation for the recent little sprint in the silver (metal)
market. The U.S. Senate proposed to coin four million
dollars of silver monthly, taking the £2,400,000 profit for
war purposes. But the Representatives promptly threw
the scheme out; and on this occasion the western Silver-
ites don’t seem to have had nerve enough to fight for the
absurdity. Also, at about the same time, it was an-
nounced that the India Office hadn’t done anything
further in connection with the suggested introduction of
a gold standard to India. Before that announcement
was made, the British Bimetallic League had issued a
circular, addressed to “ the producers of Australia,” urg-
ing that, as a further fall in the prices of commodities
might be expected to result from the extension of a gold
currency to India’s millions, and as Australian producers
are interested in maintaining such prices, colonial Govts,
should be moved to protest against any change in the
currency of India. The India Council has intimated that
it proposes to introduce gold simply because it despairs of
the nations again setting up silver into a position of
stability.

The luck of mining ! The speculator whose lot
of 10,000 Lake George shares was forced on the market
the other day, was the holder of a large parcel of Mt. Lyells
at bed-rock cost, and he disposed of these, and put every
cent into Lake George. On his Lyell holding he could
have commanded a comfortable competence ; now—alas !

• * * »
The latest idea of the Chillagoe Proprietary Co.
is the utilising of the Barron Falls in works for the treat-
men t of Chillagoe ores. Minister for Lands Foxton lately
visited the locality and seemed not unfavorable to the
co’s proposals. The Chillagoe folks will bear watching.
In the first place, it would be a burning shame if they
were permitted to ruin the attractiveness of the Falls-
one of the none-too-plentiful beauty-spots Australia is
possessed of. The American power companies have suc-
ceeded in making one side of Niagara about the most
hideous place in a big continent. Foxton weakly re-
marks that he doesn’t object provided the co. doesn’t
want “an absolute monopoly”—and, of course, no co.
ever does. Rights up to 10,000 horse-power will satisfy
them—for a time, and apparently nobody in Bananaland
cares a rap whether they get them or whether they don’t;
Wages are good in the North ; it’s infernally hot most of
the°year—and what’s the good of bothering about any-
thing?
* • • •
Mt. Morgan has just paid a 7d. div. Seven
years now since the co. paid tnorb than a 6d. div. Before
that the monthly Is. div. had been a delightfully monoton-
ous occurrence.' Then came the (id. payment and corres-
ponding fall in shares. The 6d. era has run from ’9l' to
present date. On a million shares the additional penny
represents about £4009. The 7d. div. (be it noted) is de-
clared when the month of June is only two-thirds over.
Does this signify a possible return to the old Is. glories—
or what ?
* a * ♦
Pearl-sheller Clark asserts that there are about
1200 Japs on Thursday Island. “ Things are so bad,” he
continues, “ that it is' a question now of my firm being
wiped out or the Japanese.” Clark suggests the buying
out of Japanese boats and the re-selling of them to our
own people. Whether this be deemed advisable or not it
is certainly high time the Japs were denied proprietary
rights. Both South and Western Australia will not allow
an alien to own a boat in the pearl fishery, and it’s about
time Queensland followed their example. Clark laughs at
the idea of Queensland gaining compensation from
Japanese trade. The poverty of the Japs, with whom
16s. a month is good wages, effectually prevents this.
Said an intelligent Jap, recently : “ We like woollen
clothing well enough, but are too poor to be able to buy it.”

Gympie is humming, metallically ; on June 21st
18,756 shares were sold on the Exchange. A record for
the year.
• * * *
Clermont holds the premier position in Q. for
’97 alluvial returns. Population of diggers has been
trebled and yield of gold has advanced from 12,6830 z. to
22,0660 z. Warden estimates population at 4350, out of
which 3600 are actually digging. Ravenswood has in-
creased its output for the year to 20,1760 z. Eidsvold,
however, shows up badly for the last 12 months. The
leading mines are held by four co.’s with £23,000 capital.
During the year these have paid no divs., but their calls
amounted to nearly £6OOO. Stone put through batteries
amounted to 2696 tons yielding 87200 z. gold—an average
of loz. Bdwt. 16grs.
• • • •
What a revolution has taken place in the gold
industry ! All the larger Q. goldfields (the Etheridge ex-
cepted) have railway communication with the coast, and
Govt, has now to provide but one gold-escort—from
Georgetown to Normanton. As for sticking up the escort
or anything of that kind—it’s all over. Enterprising
spirits find it pays better to stick up the Treasury.
• * * *
The Q. gold-output for ’97 was eminently satis-
factory, but nearly every other mineral showed a decrease.
Tin has gone down from 1554 tons to 1203 tons ; silver
from 279,2840 z. to 234,0350 z. ; lead from 618 tons to 385
tons ; coal from 371,390 tons to 358,407 tons ; copper from
580 tons to 288 tons. Opal produced at Fermoy is valued
at .£6000; Duck Creek (near Thargomindah), £4OOO.
Under-Secretary for Mines thinks opal-mining can never
become an important factor in mining industry as “ from
our present knowledge of the occurrence of the stone, its
winning can never rise much above the level of fossicking.”
* • • •
They are talking about pyrites records at Ben-
digo. A clean-up from a tribute in the Specimen Hill,
Eaglehawk, has just yielded 28oz. 6dwt. per ton ; the stone
from which the pyrites was taken gave very poor returns.
But in the past there have been a few yields of over 300 z.,
Lansell holding the “record” for this as for many other
things in connection with Bendigo mining.

In the first 24 weeks of ’97 Bendigo had an
excess of calls over divs. of £20,825 (£63,088 and £83,913).
In the first 24 weeks of ’9B the excess of divs. over calls
was £21,470 (£96,800 and £75,330). And the improve-
ment continues. Yet, though the field gives about one-
third of the province’s divs, frequently no more than
three of the couple of hundred of shares quoted on the
Melb. Exchange are Bendigo stocks. Bendigo pouts
about it ; yet one is inclined to say “ Happy Bendigo ! ”

Moorabbin (Vic.) Shire Council has a plan of its
own for making owners of boom allotments pay the rates
they owe. It advertises their names in the Age. That
fetches ’em.
* * « •
A clause in the law controlling Vic. Mining
Boards declares that no payments are to be made to mem-
bers excepting when they sit for three days. Result, a
terrible strain upon the intellects of members to devise
ways and means of making half-an-hour’s business extend
over three days, but they invariably succeed in their
patriotic effort.
* • » *
Castlemaine has hopes again ; the Spring Gully
Co., at Fryer’s, has unearthed a prospect equal to any-
thing found in that district this last quarter-century.

Menzie’s Barossa, the much-boomed S.A. mine
which failed to crush any gold, is to have another trial, as
shareholders have decided to put through another 250 tons
before liquidating. Menzie, the promoter, sank the whole
of his Westralian-made fortune in the mine.

laide School of Mines and Industries, reports that he took
the students to Menzies’ Barossa the other day and pointed
out “ how a mine should not be worked.”

Of all the wild foolishnesses of the recent little
boom in Tasmanian silver specs., the Dora foolishness
seems to have been the wildest. Every disinterested visi-
tor to the Dora “ field ” gives it a bad name. A bad name
is the fate of every show that gets boomed from behind
hotel bars, as the various Doras were.
* * * »
A little but pertinacious crowd at Kalgoorlie
begins to make a determined “ set ” at the policy of Work-
ing seven days a week at the Boulder mines. Strangely
enough, though, the opposition rarely comes from the
working miners in such cases ; it is the owners who
usually first discover that it does not pay, especially as
the workings get deeper and the ventilation more defec-
tive.
* * * *
Kanowna alluvial rush has corneas a godsend to
the whole of the Westralian fields ; and the huge tribe
which, a few months ago, was living on J. Bull’s cham-
pagne and truffles, has bought a shovel and put clay on
its pants and corns on its hands.
Said that Hooley’s interests in purely mercantile
ventures in Westralia are so large that realisation by his
trustees in insolvency is sure to cause more than one col-
lapse amongst the now seemingly-prosperous businesses
of that province.
If the production of the first five months of ’9B
is maintained, Westralia is now the boss gold-producer of
the Seven Provinces. Figures up to May: Westralia,
359,9420 z. : Queensland, 34i,2900z. ; Victoria, 312,3220 z.
* * * *
Of Westralian goldfields Kalgoorlie is easily
first in point of aggregate production ; it has yielded
515,547 oz., an average of 2 oz. 9 dwt. per ton. Murchison
comes next with 152,080 oz. ; average 1 oz. 4 dwt. Then
Coolgardie, 147,215 oz. (average 2 oz. 3 dwt., thanks to the
early days of Bayley’s, Wealth of Nations, etc.); and
Menzies, 84,6 8 oz. (average 2 oz. 4 dwt.). Norseman’s
aggregate is 8454 oz. (average 19 dwt.)
* * • *
Coolgardie tradespeople in desperation offer on
their own account a reward, guaranteed, of about £l5OO
for the discovery of a substantial deep lead within 4Jt
miles of the township.
* • • •
Already they quarrel in the far West over rival
claims to the discovery of the goldfields. Down to date
the facts seem to be that the first find of gold in quantity
was made at Enuing by Anstey, whose mate was Payne ;
they received • 500 from the Govt, as a reward. Bernard
Oolready and H. Huggins found Golden Valley ; they also
got £5OO as reward. After that T. R. Riseley and R. M.
Toomey found Southern Cross, and claim to have named
it ; they pegged out the Golden Pig on Feb. 8, ’B7.
Parker’s Range (found by Parker and Hickey), and others
westward followed.
* * • *
Generally held among the alluvial diggers on
Kanowna (W.A.) that the leads there are the bed and
tributaries of an ancient river that at one time ran into
the Gordon lakes, eight miles distant. The main lead has
been traced some miles north of the township in the
direction indicated. These lakes, which extend east and
west for some scores of miles, are themselves believed by
many to be the remains of a great river destroyed by vol-
canic action. _ _ .

COCHAO
Some of the friends o’ he late’y-appqiufcjd
Australian K.B.’s suddenly stopped jubilating
when they d scoyered that a K.B. falls so terribly
short of a G. or K. C. M.G. in pomp and circum-
stance. A K.C.M.G., for instance, marches
abroad with a star, a ribbon, a mantle, and other '
fixings ; hut a K.B. is entitled to wear no em-
broidery distinction whatever. Again, the com-
pany of K.tCM.G.’s, to keep it select, is limited
by charter to 200 members, whereas there may be
as many K.B.’s as there are sons of Adam. The
K.B. simply carries the title “ Sir,” and his Mrs.,
among the-.courteous,, may be called “Lady.”
But the most galling affront in offering a man a
K.B. lies in the historical fact that when needy
James I. sold titles for cash lie charged up to
<£looo for wha,he called titles “worthy of a gentle-
man,” but handed over K.B’s for sums down as
low as £5. ..
* * * *
Curious that most Governors sent out to
Australia nevrr seem to learn anything of the
character, tastes, and. opinions of the people who
pay them salaries. Vio. Gov. Brassey is a
shocking case in point. -He has seized the oppor-
tunity provided by his being temporarily at ’Ome
to urge the Imp. Government to impress upon
Australia the propriety of keeping up a force
of 50 0 mounted rifles, “ who ,could be called
on for service abroad wherever British require-
ments demanded military assistance—in India,
South Africa, or elsewhere.” He also advises
that a subsidy of £2O per head should be paid for
each man available for foreign service, and evi-
dently thinks that he has suggested a valuable
future addition to the Australian export trade.
Prime Australian mercenaries ought to look well
on an invoice. Pity they couldn’t be frozen or
tinned. When Lord B. is not falling off some-
thing, or has just fallen off something and is laid
up for a day or two, his diseased mental activity
shows itself in the strangest shapes.

Mrs. Freeman Thomas (Gov. Brassey’s
daughter) confirms the stories to hand rc the wreck
of the China. Bhe tells her London friends that
she was giving a “cheery supper-party” when
the vessel came to grief.
The Women’s Temperance Christian
Union recently started a superior hash-mosque in
Perth (W. A.) with the object of saving bibulous
and susceptible youth from the danger of dining
at places wli tc the der-rink is sold. Under Wes-
tralian law all eating-houses have to be licensed,
and the keepers thereof have to come before the
Licensing bcich and demonstrate that they are
of good character, in the same way as the mire-
generate publican. The representative W.C.T.U.
lady did this and was granted a license, but was
horrified to learn from the bench that under the
license the establishment would be compelled to
provide beer for dining customers should it be
asked for. The bench, with fine humor, asked the
lady whether, if he dined at the establishment and
asked for beer, it would be supplied, but the
scand disod lady, with an emphatic “ No,”
gathered up her skirts and fled with horror from
the court.
* * * *
W. D. Traill, in Literature, commends
the Australian press for its “liberality” in en-
couraging young poets. Where does the liberality
come in ? The Australasian, for instance, never,
or hardly ever, pays for verse. Do any of the
other papers, Bulletin excepted?
♦ * i m
Apropos the Queensland Govt.’s complaint
to South Australia that Chinese are in the habit
of overlanding from the Northern Territory and
sneaking over the border at Camooweal, Murif,
the overlanding cyclist, tells how he encountered
one such party, near Newcastle Waters, equipped
for the expedition with three packhorses, well
loaded with provisions. His inquiries led him to
believe that, although generally moving in similar
parties and fairly well armed, not more than about
a-half of those who leave the coast get through to
the border. Blacks spear many of them ; and
naturally, on account of their undertaking, they
don’t seek police protection, but rather avoid those
places where police influence is most felt.
* * * «
Miss Elsie Margaret Robertson, of Mel-
bourne, departed on a three months’ trip to Bris-
bane, last Saturday, with a Mr. and Mrs.
Eddington. This is not a sassiety item, but a
piece of legal intelligence. Miss R., who will soon
be 2i), is being administered by the Vic. Court,
and when she wants to journey beyond the con-
fines of the province, her executors appeal to a
Judge, and the Judge (like Chancellor Brough, in
“ lolanthe ”) sits in his court all day, so that the
agreeable girl can go away. Justice Hood was
the paternal administrator last week, Mr. Topp
appeared to state the case on behalf of Miss R.,
and Mr. Weigall held a brief to the effect that the
damsel’s executors “consented” After listening
to arguments on both sides and satisfying himself
that Miss Robertson merely wished to net out on
bail, as it were, his Honor made an order for her
trip to Brisbane, likewise he increased her allow-
ance by another <£loo a year, on the distinct
understanding that she shall be escorted back to
Melbourne by “a suitable companion.” The
Bulletin’s idea is th<* . ::be should travel under
the care of a Legal Commission, costs to be
charged to the estate.

Administrator-of-the-Government Madden
and his fair belongings must have felt
quite happy after conferring their patronage on
“Alcestis,” at Melb. Town Hall, last week. A
large orchestra and Liedertafel chorus honored
them with two big doses of “God Save,” one on
their arrival, and another when they trooped out.
It is beautiful to observe the clean-shaven fiance
of “the engaged Miss Madden ” participating in an
all-round enjoyment of Family Greatness. He
seems to be softly whispering to himself —“ What
price me ! ”

The Cinderella is coming along nicely.
Perth papers record with appropriate awesome-
ness that when big John Forrest was in London
Lady F. took him to the church of St. Andrew
Undershaft, at the corner of St. Mary-axe,
London, where lies buried one of her ancestors,
Sir Hugh Hammersley, who died in IGI7. Now
a photo, of the church and a water-color picture
of it have been sent out—no doubt to remind the
family of the crimson bonds, John not having
had much success at 'Oine with the other sort.
* * * *
S.A. Oppositionists aver that they intend
to assail the Kingston Ministry on its Hospital
administration. This is no surprise to those
people who believe that these hoary old troubles
—they began over four years ago—will outlast the
present generation. The original Hospital staff
disappeared long since, and the last trouble was
between Ramsay Smith and Morrison, t«o
of the imported doctors. As showing the
bitterness of the trouble, most of the old staff
still maintain expensive nursing establishments
rather than allow cases to go Hospital wards.
Most people have, however, completely forgotten
what was the original cause of the dispute—ex-
cept that a woman and a tennis-match were indi-
rectly connected with it.
* * * *
About the most original humorist among
Australian doctors is wastii g h uiself at Broken
Hill, where he is attach d to the hospital.
Once, a little time ago, having had a slight differ-
ence with the Govt, medical-officer as to the
latter’s right to conduct the post-mortems and
pocket the fees therefor, he circumvented the
enemy after the death of an accident ease by
promptly removing the injured part of the spine
and substituting therefor a piece of wire rope.
Now a patient who had signed the usual guaran-
tee-form having died, the same doctor re-
fused to give up the body to the funeral party
which came to bury it, claiming that the hospital
had a lien over the corpse till the maintenance-fees
were arranged for. The hearse had, therefore, to
be driven away empty, and the mourners dispersed
until the question of the lien was settled.

silence and enforced idleness and its starvation ;
never a word of the punishment-cells underground
where no light, no sound can penetrate ; never a
word of the hand-cuffs and anklets and waist-ring

sentenced—Star-Chamber fashion—to be put in
irons ; never a word of the triangles. No, a visit
to Pentridge is a farce—they dare not show the
hideous system in all its nakedness.

A party of officers from the Japanese war-
ship recently paid a visit to Sydney University
Medical School. In the dissecting-room, what
struck the professional members of the party most
was the fact that in S) dney a “ head and neck ”
is allotted to two students only. In Europe,
“ subjects ” are so scarce that it is usual for eight
men to be at work on the same one. Elesh and
blood are cheap in Sydney. Look at the eleven-bob-
a-week twelve-hours-a-day of the 15 per cent,
dividend North Shore Ferry Co. !
* * * *
It is not generally known that when the
Confederate vessel Alabama was at Melb., during
the American civil war, some local Federal Yanks
formed a plot to blow up the ship. It was frustrated
only by an attempt to get the assistance of some big
American merchants, who, while sympathising
with the North, weren’t taking any personal re-
sponsibility.

Up to date, the queerest Yic. explosive-
case was where Green was tried and acquitted for
sending a little fig-box, which exploded under the
chisel of Mr. Reynolds, of Reynolds and English,
carriers. The matter was never cleared up.
Green was an American gentleman who had had
business relations with Reynolds.

The last important Vic. will-case of alleged
priestly influence, a Collingwood affair, had the
present Chief Justice Madden for the defence.
He said : “ Your Honor, everybody is aware that
the doctrine of the church does not sanction the
interpretation put on it by the other side.” His
Honor: “The question is what the ignorant
believe.”

Apropos “The Screen.” In Darlinghurst
prison church, a man condemned to die is brought
to the services—Butler was the only exception—
and placed behind a screen secure from observa-
tion ; he cannot even see the parson or priest who
preaches at him.
SUCH SHEEN OF HAIR
FOR THE BULLETIN
UCH sheen of hair hath Phyllis fair;
(J Such smiling wonder-wise ;
Such chin of dimpling daintiness t
Such deep o' dew-bright eyes ;
Such merry mischief lurking there
Behind their blend of blue —
I pity those who love her not,
l weep for those that do.
When Even conies the Witch doth set
Her Lamps of Love aglow ;
A nd we, a tribe of silly moths ,
We flutter to and fro ;
In vain—in vain ye bid us pause.
What reck we who may rue ?
O, luckless moths that love not Light t
Ah, hapless moths that do !
A nd those who tell her o'er the tale
How laughing eyes allure.
She bids them ware the deadly snare
And wend away, secure;
But who could heed such cruel best
When red lips smile anew ?
When, though she bids us love her not.
She draws us till we do ?
Now some there be who suppliants come
With lover-lute to sue,
And list—with silv'ry laugh she mocks
The mesh-entangled crew !
A nd some there be who come to scoff
And then remain to woo — 'Qj
A lack, for those who love her not /
A las, for those that do /
And yet, despite the wrongs we dree ,
She holds us so in thrall
That voices raised in malison
In mellow blessings fall ;
And so, the vengeance we invoke
Is but that Cupid too
May kindly move her yet to love —
A nd speed her if she do I
©
P. LUFTIG.
READY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fate leads me by a stormy way,
But if I'm in a battle
I’ve always one to share the fray
When fast the bullets rattle ;
I’m sure of one to make a stand,
Of one to hold me steady ;
She’s first and last at my right hand,
And so I’ve called her Ready.
She’ll smile beneath the hail of blows,
Nor hate the hand that makes it;
She’s like a little wind-toss’d rose
That scents the blast that breaks it.
And when the day seems still and fair
New love-waves round me eddy,
Till I can almost drown despair—
And hope with my sweet Ready.
Maybe your Ready’s just like mine—
A mate for aye, and after !
Then don't secrete the simple sign
That links her heart to laughter;
A tender word, a kindly deed,
Each day to her discover ;
So shall you keep your friend-in-need,
So shall she know you love her.
Parramatta.
LESLIE QAKES.

m • * * *
“ Kifwa ” : “ Scotty the Wrinkler’s ” sug-
gestion as to collecting funds in churches is nothing
very new ; the late shepherd of an up-the-line
Anglican Church used to supply the choir-boys
with coin to put in the plate every time same
went round. This was specially marked, and re-
moved in the vestry, and used for the next occa-
sion, and so on. In fact, the good clergyman used
his choir-boys as decoy-ducks.
* * * *
A prominent feature of old-time Wes-
tralian journalism was that the scribes were
chiefly drawn from the ranks of talented convicts.
This is not now the case, but the idea that any
sort of treatment is good enough for journalists
still prevails. This humorous par. from the West
Australian (Perth daily) of 8/(>/’9B is evidence of
the status of the pressman in Forrestland, and
suggests incidentally that there is a wild and un-
controllable mutiny on the staff of that journal:—
Officers’ Quarters at Mundaringl— A statement was
made by a Northam correspondent in a letter which
appeared in Friday’s issue of the West Australian in re-
ference to the extravagant expenditure by the Public
Works Department on erecting camps and official quarters
for the engineers employed on the waterworks at Mun-
daring. The Director of Public Works supplied one of
our reporters with an explanation of this at the beginning
of the week, but in our reporter’s hands the explanation
became so unintelligible that it had to be returned to
him. So far, our reporter has not furnished us with the
corrected version.

* * * *
At Broken Hill, t’other day, the police,
after a 3-years’ slumber, raided the local shop-
keepers and detected nearly 100 in possession of
unstamped and light weights. The cases were
taken in batches. The P.M., dealing with the first
lot, fined the unstamped-weight folk 20s. and
costs, and the others 40s. and costs. The trades-
men had a meeting and protested, apparently in
all seriousness, that the police should have warned
them of the intended raid ! And when the second
batch of cases came before an honorary bench (the
P.M. being away) the intelligent unpaid fined each
offender (whether for light or unstamped weights)
Is. and costs ! And now the offending hucksters
have the front to expect the Justice Department
to remit all the fines. The Jay pee system— it
stinks. It always has stunk.
* * * *
The Sydney constable who recently ran-in
a stricken-down cabman might have been excused
for not knowing paralysis from merely “ paralytic
drunk,” but men in charge of lock-ups should cer-
tainly possess sufficient rudimentary medical
knowledge to prevent the possibility of a delay—
as in this case—of 12 hours in the discovery of
the true state of the case.
* * * *
The costs on a recent case, lasting 11 days,
in Brisbane, totted up to the tidy total of £2OOO.
Lawyers’expenses principally, of course, but the
time occupied in the trial was probably the chief
cause. Fancy a Judge, in this era of progress,
having to take notes of evidence in long-hand !
Wouldn’t the employment of a decent staff of
shorthand-reporters and typewriters be really a
big saving in the long run ?

“To what base uses” may not the noble
descend. The other day, when the papers pub-
lished the news of the demise of Britain’s fore-
most statesman, a Queen-street (Brisbane) draper
improved the shining hour by putting a portrait
of William Ewart in a conspicuous position among
his mourning goods, with an intimation that
never could the weeds of woe be bought cheaper,
or grief be indulged at less expense.

Cabled is the portentous intelligence that
the Prince of Wales, as Grand Thingabob of
Whashernames, “ has appointed Mr. J. G. Davies,
Chairman of Committees in Tasmania!! As-
sembly,” to be District Grand Triton of Minnows,

heard in all his life) is one of the Australasian
press-cable syndicate.

“ Engliraustralian ” : The weak response
of the Victorian Scots to Melb. Mayor M‘Each-
arn’s appeal for a kilted regiment indicates either
that the Seotch-parented Australian is Australian
first and Caledonian afterwards, or that his yearn-
ings for bare knees and philabegs and things are
not in it with his interest in gee-gee-ing and
ball-bashing. In fact, despite the “ clannishness ”
charged upon those of Scottish parentage as well
as of Scottish birth, the sScoto-Australian is a lot
more genuinely Austral than either his Anglo or
Hiberno-analogue.
* • * *
In a lazy, disjointed fashion Justice Wil-
liams told some home-truths in Dr. Strong’s Aus-
tralian Church the other Sunday. His Honor
was talking on the administration of the criminal
law. The illogical diversity of the sentences
passed on men committing precisely the same
offence, under similar circumstances, he did not
hesitate to style a social blot. Also, Judge Wil-
liams considers crimes against the person much
more heinous than those against property. “If a
man batters me in a brutal fashion,” says his
Honor, “ I would feel it more than if he stole my
watch.” Best of all, however, he thinks that
before a Judge is competent to give solitary con-
finement to a prisoner, he ought to have seen men
undergoing that particular class of punishment.
Judge Williams believes in solitary for old
offenders, and in mild solitary for first offenders,
as a preventive against their coming back for a
second dose. “I once saw a woman,” said the
Judge significantly, “ who had just undergone a
month’s solitary confinement at one stretch, to
which she had been sentenced by one of the Judges
in this province—l won’t mention names. I never
want to see such a sight again.” Tell us— who is
the Judge that sentences women to solitary con-
finement? Also, does he flog women? If not,
why not ?

One sees a queer witness in the box occa-
sionally. In No. 2 jury court, Sydney, one day
lately, counsel was trying to extort evidence from
a benevolent-locking old gentleman (who turned out
tobeHy. Clark, the esteemed M.L. A. for Bega) as
to his client’s sobriety. “ How long have you
known ? ” “ All his life—so years.” “ Did
you ever see him drunk ? ” “ No.” “ Would you
regard him as a temperate man, then ? ” “ Er—
er—well—er—I think—er -moderately temperate.”
[lt recalls the A.N.A.’s “moderately moral.”]
Counsel (despairingly): “That will do.” Sup-
posing the poor fellow had got drunk once every
25 years, what would the brilliant old gentleman
have called that ? How many years would a man

In a recent Melb. divorce-case in which

Pcntridge prison, Vic., has run out of
stone-masons and bricklayers, and this is a serious
matter. Great additions were being made to the
prison when suddenly all the prisoners competent
to do the work gave out, and the operations
have since been suspended pending the arrival of
more masons. But masons refuse to arrive, and
now the authorities have their choice of two
things : they must either pay to have the work

Burglars break in and steal from the homes
of several Melbourne parsons. Silver plate and
“ quantities of gold jewellery” in each case. Now,
if the burglars were humorous enough to contri-
bute their loot to the fund for the relief of the
famished back-block parsons, over whose hard lot
there has been so much wailing and teeth-gnashing
of late ! “ Lay not up treasure,” &c., &c.
The treasures of this world of pride
That lure the sinful crowds
Which parson here will put aside
For others —in the clouds ?
The reverend’s faith is great (we know
That by the texts he quotes).
Yet luxuries he will not spurn,
E’en though he’s given in return
God’s promissory-notes !

* * • »
Will somebody please to take a week’s
holiday and explore Tasmania ? A steamer, lately
philandering round the coast of the flyspeck in
search of a missing prospector, discovered a fine,
broad, navigable river which nobody had heard of
previously. The captain of the steamer promptly
named the stream the TJrquhart, as a tribute to
the Tas. Attorney-General, who has the reputa-
tion of being both broad and deep. The oldest
and smallest of the Australias offers the best
chance to an explorer.

What woman-franchise has effected in
Maoriland. A Dunedin newspaper clipping :
City Police Court.—Before Messrs. J. Carroll and H.
Calder, J’s.P. Maintenance.—Alfred Myers, a young man,
was charged with, on the 14th inst., leaving Dunedin
without making provision for the support of his unborn
child, of which Sarah Sparks was the mother. Sergeant
O’Neill asked for a remand till to-morrow with a view of
having the matter brought before the stipendiary magis-
trate. Accused, who was employed on board a steamer,
was about to leave for Sydney yesterday. The mother
was afraid he would not return, and she had a warrant
issued for his arrest. The remand was granted accord-
ingly, bail being allowed accused in his recognisance of
£2O and two sureties of £lO each.
Which recalls the Gilbertian hero who, to carry
out his imposed task of committing a daily crime,
disinherits his only unborn son.
* * * •
Lead-poisoning manifests itself in a score
of different ways ; but one of the saddest is
mental derangement. Yet, at Broken Hill, which
owes its very existence to silver-lead miners, there
is no provision outside the prison for such cases ;
and only the other day one unfortunate sufferer,
who had been taken in charge by the police, died
in the gaol hospital!

The Cosme Monthly announces the with-

from the Communist settlement, along with

drink the Communistic cup down to the bitter
dregs. Bananalad took back to her broad bosom
50 ex-new Australians in the year ’97 —weariful
idealists sick of quarrelling over the great washing-

In Melb., recently, Judge Casey flattened
out, with <£s damages and costs, the mis-
creant who cuts oil-cloth on the footpath and
leaves the rolls lying around to capsize the un-
wary. And Sydney Judge Backhouse has put his
foot down, to the weight of <£3l and costs, on the
gay youth who dashes around, firing 9-inch shells
of “ great dailies ” among the lieges. Life was
indeed a lottery when the deli very -carts were bom-
barding the news-agents.
The new Melb. postal ar-
rangement which gives the mail-
men free tramway travelling to
and from the G. P.Q. and their
respective suburban districts, is
proving a wiser economy than
was expected. Melbourne —not to
mention the rest of Vie. —is in
a state of postal barbarism, all
the same. The suburbs have no
evening delivery of letters. It
costs twopence to post a letter
from the G.P.O. to Parliament
House. And the provincialism of
the new Act is a satire on the
Government’s Federal longings.
No newspaper published outside
the province can be mailed in
Victoria for less than twopence,
and the cost of sending a Bulle-
tin to one’s pious old aunt in
Hingland is— fourpence.

Asa general rule, when one
man quits a responsible post his
successor has already been be-
spoken. So universally is this be-
lieved that when Fitzgibbon va-
cated the position of Town Clerk
in Melbourne not a single ap-
plication was received by the City
Council in response to the lona-
tidc advts. published in the daily
papers. A fresh advt. had to be prepared, pro-
longing the appointed date of commencing office,
and assuring the public that there was nothing
cut-and-dried about the matter.
* * * *
Two S.A. legislators recently visited the
Q. white leper station in Moreton Bay. A grue-
some extract: —
The inmates of the hospital were 11 in number, or two
more than in ’96. so that leprosy among the white popu-
lation of Queensland is evidently on the increase. But
the leprosy curse is a much more widely-spread evil than
is here indicated. It must b ■ remembered that there are
considerably over 100 colored lepers isolated on Friday
Island, while all Chinese infected with the disease are
sent back to their own country. There is also a white
female leper confined on Peel Island. . . . The terrible
disease was to be seen in all its stages. The horribly mis-
shapen and distorted features of one poor lad were puffed
up in an extraordinary manner. The fingers were twisted,
several toes had dropped off, and the throat was so
affected that the voice was almost inaudible, with a pecu-
liar huskiness. But the sight that made our hearts grow
sick and a feeling come over us as if the blood were
freezing in our veins, was when the doctor look us to the
bedside of a little boy who used to attend one of the
State schools. The ghostly appearance appalled us, and
can never be forgotten. The flesh had been eaten away
from the crown of his head to the soles of his feet. We
were gazing on a breathing skeleton. The whole body
was the color of a piece of raw meat turning black with
decay. The features had disappeared, nothing being left
but the bones covered with large, repulsive scales. He
could not suffer the bedclothes to touch him and was in
constant pain. He scarcely ever slept, and the appetite
was almost gone. Yet he was exceedingly patient, but
anxious to die. He had no fear of death. The voice of
the sympathetic doctor trembled as with averted look 1 e
questioned the poor boy, and told him he would give him
some more morphia. We quickly left the room, and it.
was with feelings of relief that we learned from the doctor
that the boy could not possibly last much longer.

Usual distinction. In Melb. recently two
women (“ respectable ’’) were fined 40s. for
prigging ribbons from a draper’s shop, and same
day a woman (“disreputable”) got six months for
annexing a ring of trifling value. Police-court
justice practically does take the bird by the
feathers.
* * * *
By a curious coincidence, on the same day
that the trustees of the Elder bequests in Adelaide
were discussing, with persons interested, the plans
for disposing of the =£25,000 left for the provision
of workmen’s homes, the aldermen of the holy
city were touring the quarters of the unfashionable
Totties. The competition in Adelaide is particu-
larly keen, and the squalor consequently intense.
One black-eyed sexagenarian or so apologised for
the lack of furniture in her hovel. She had just
started for herself, and her entire stock-in-trade
consisted of a dilapidated bed. Some day, "per-
haps, some other practical philanthropist will be
bold enough to leave <£20,000 to provide quarters
for the Totties ; and the subsequent experiment
will be worth watching.

Many of the match-selling women in the
streets—who often stop at their corners till late at
night—carry infants aged one or two years, pre-
sumably as a spur to charity. What chance have
these exposed mites —many of whom are hired —of
keeping out of the hospitals later on, or ever be-
coming strong and healthy ?

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 28, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
Government House lent itself to the Girls’
Friendly Society for a tv.o days’ bazaar last week.
Subject to the rain, a garden fete was to be held
in the vice-regal grounds, but the wet weather
drove the fete to the verandah,, and kept it there
Monday and. Tuesday. The youngest and pret-
tiest society girls sold pin-cushions and bootees
at bazaar prices, and served tea in a marquee on
the lawn, and the tract stall sustained the correct
note of sobriety in the midst of all the levity and
worldliness. The public poured in heavily each
day, and strolled about the gardens, and sniffed
the rare atmosphere of the upper strata, and
endeavoured to wander casually by mistake into
the vice-regal interiors. Mrs. Willie Mitchell
bought jam lavishly in a blue serge sac coat and
skirt, and humored fashion’s latest whim by wind-
ing strings of pearls round her neck. Men were
at the usual bazaar discount. The two little fair-
haired aides, and the tall, pale, languid Lord
Richard Neville, who is the very lord of a school-
girl’s dreams as far as looks go, were the only
noticeable black-coats.
The central attraction of the fete was the toney
bicycle ride on the tennis court. This wa3
headed by Miss Dolly Brand in a cold white
blouse and cycling-skirt, with sailor-hat, pink tie,
and bicycle decorated with pink ribbons and
flowers. Pretty Miss Nea Mort, in another shade
of pink, won the prize for skilful riding, and ten
society eyclistes divided blues, yellows, and rose
colors among them. The prize for the best-
decorated bicycle fell to Miss Atherton, who had
contrived a Frenchy-looking wheel of deep rose-
colored baby-ribbons and tiny brass bells. These
ribbon adornments are not just the simple little
matters they appear to be. I am told that to
make your machine anyway smart, nearly 300
yards of ribbon are indispensable.
The Tempe Ball is always one of the biggest
Charity dances. The camaraderie of the Church
makes a good attendance a certainty. This year’s
dance was just an imperceptible shade less radiant
than others, perhaps, but was well-groomed and
quite “society.” A few fancy dresses speckled
the horizon, not too successfully, .for fancy dress is
a quantity that needs to be laid on regardless if
the picture is to be a striking one. The three sets
of fancy dresses had the Swedish dance all to
themselves. The prettiest of these was Mrs.
Prondergast’s Gainsborough circle. As far as I
could see, the Swedish dance is a mixture of Sir
Roger and the lancers and that scene from real
life of long ago, when a man goes down on his
knees for his lady’s hand.
Somewhere after supper-time, Peggy Pryde and
a gay company of Ti volians appeared, and dancing
flagFpd suddenly. The ball resolved itself into
knots ; conversation ceased ; and everyone gazed
at the fair little woman in black and red who
holds the public heart in her hand just now. And
those who could never know where the Tivoli
locates itself, were as happy as if they had been to
the Tivoli without paying.
One of the smartest dresses in the room was
a lovely creation of black net over turquoise
silk, heavily embroidered in jet and silver. Mrs.
Prendergast wore a pretty frock of white brocade,
with epaulettes of turquoise velvet. The other
secretary, Mrs. Brennan, wore a lemon shade of
pink. Miss Austen, in cream silk, draped her
fichu ingeniously, and made an artistic success of
one of the most difficult items of a woman’s ward-
robe. Before closing this notice, I would like to
shed a word of pity for the men who took part in
the Federation set. Big white badges hung round
their necks, and marked them even in the darkest
corners.
Next night, the welfare of the Boys’ Brigade
was danced for by a merry company in Needs’
rooms. Without being too terribly toney, this
little hop was, perhaps, the brightest of the
season. Mrs. Gullet, the treasurer, worked hard
for her good cause in white moire and black velvet
trimmings. The secretary, Mrs. Playfair, had an
un.secretarial appearance in a telling arrangement
of black brocade and jingly steel trimmings.
Then there was the very tall and very pretty girl
from Bananaland, who did credit to her native’place
in banana-tinted crepe-de-chine-; and the inevit-
able suburban belle, in the inevitable pink and
black of the suburbs. A poet could not but be
gay in such a jocund company, and I noticed the
latest exotic, Mr. Arthur Adams, from Maoriland,
tripping gaily with brown eyes and a brown
beard.
The Freemasons’grand festivity takes the shape
of a ball at the Town Hall, on Thursday, July
28th. This will not be quite the ordinary dance
ball, as an organ recital and a grand procession of
the craft in its aprons and sashes are thrown in
before dancing proper begins at nine o’clock.
Rosehill is not yet the fashionable racecourse
Randwiok is, and Society can’t find its way out
there without a strong lead. But a partv from
Govt. House journeyed to the Rosehill Cup on
Saturday, so a large body of Society people fol-
lowed quietly. Miss Alice Brand, in a purple
coat and skirt, performed the ceremony of the
day, and presented Kiteware’s master with the
triumphant silver trophy. Kiteware himself
kicked out briskly at the crowd hanging on his
heels, and ate grass in silent scorn of all cups that
were not buckets. Miss Elliott Page walked
round with His Ex. in a long braided cloak of
prune cloth and a big black hat with a little white
veil. A frock of flaming red sat in the corner of
the vice-regal box with its head down, and I can-
not tell you who was inside the vehement coat of
black, white, and red—possibly Joseph. I noticed
two young brides reproducing each other unwit-
tingly in vine-green tailor-costumes, with violet
hats and feather boas. I also noticed, with awe,
the happy forgetfulness of various ladies who
plucked large bunches of blossoms between the
races and carried them home, undaunted by rules
and regulations and similar trifles.
But surely afternoon-tea, at Rosehill, is a vainer
thing than need be ! Unless cake of a bygone era,
washed down with acute essence of tannin, is in-
tended to uphold the picnicky aspect of the pretty
little course. In fact, sweeping innovations arc
needed at all our racecourse tea-rooms. The
Rand wick tea-cups would let a horse’s head in,
and three bites of a horse’s teeth would make no
impression on the density of the tea-cup walls.
The same remark may be applied to the bricks of
seed and currant-cake that hurried waitresses
throw at your head at their own sweet will.
Just think of asking a man to a party to meet
his own wife ! Don’t you think it was an artless
idea of the Ladies’ Club to invite Admiral Pear-
son last Friday to meet Mrs. Admiral Pearson at
the Club’s “at home ! ” The Admiral might have
replied that he would be meeting his wife at
dinner that evening, and had already breakfasted
and lunched with her early in the day. However,
his naval bravery bore him across the water, and
enabled him to stand round in the Salon rooms for
an hour or two, and even induced him to don the
expression that belongs by right to the wives of
other men.
After a sleep of nine years roller-skating has
roused up again and is going to enter, into the
ranks with cycling. The Hampden Club is lend-
ing its very nicest patronage to the-ample old
Darlinghurst Skating Rink, and I believe the pub-
lic is to be let in next week. Thus one more
chance is offered to those pincushion persons who
can fall without making a noise. And, speaking
of expansive parties, reminds that a scraggy
Johnny was heard inquiring plaintively of his
tailor the other day, “Do you make Reid’s clothes
regardless of expanse ? ”
Mr. Burns Walker’s invitation concert was a
heavily patronised affair that gave the debutante,
Miss Alice Braund, the advantage of a large hear-
ing. Miss Braund is yet another lady of the rosy
locks, and according to backers of red-haired
songstresses, ought to set fire to the Thames some
day. Her organ is a big soprano not yet quite
under control, but full of possibilities. The popu-
lar Burns Walker, who is responsible for Miss
Braund’s pleasing style, contributed two songs,
though suffering from a relaxed throat.
A sad theatrical tale tells that the Firm has
forbidden all its members to bike. The various
smash-ups of Ada Reeve and Bert Gilbert are
said to be the reason of the mandate.
The Firm was also largely responsible for the
success of the charity matinee last Friday. An
enormous crowd seethed up the back stairs and
down again, and changed its admission money
from two shillings to three, and from three to five,
all in vain. Hundreds of people were turned
away. The zeal of those who stood for four hours
without seeing, hearing, or breathing when any-
one else was breathing, is a remarkable example
of Sydney’s veneration for Shakespeare. Every-
body knows that the desire to hear another Romeo
commanded by another Juliet to swear not by the
same old moon was the reason of the mighty army
of women, who paid five shillings rapturously for
their negative joys.
As it happened, Romeo had a cold, and Juliet
could not be heard beyond the front stalls. There
was that much mitigation in the martyrdom.
Julius Knight made a heavy, handsome lover,
without a spark of passion in his voice. Miss
Elliot Page played Juliet as a lovely worldling in
white with nut-brown hair, who wooed her Romeo
as knowingly as a society woman bringing down
her millionaire, and flashed coquettish teeth at
him, and treated him and herself as though they
had both been out some seven seasons.
The most popular feature of the programme was
Peggy Pryde. A red-hot reception greeted the
sprightly little figure, in its funny coster frock,
big white gloves and hat trimmed with one rose
that nodded furiously from the top of a two-foot
stem. She sang a lively ditty about her sister
Sally’s wedding. When she' reached the line
“talk about the Duchess of Devonshire’s fancy
dress ball, my dear—no class, no class ! ” she
twinkled her merry eyes towards the vice-
regal box where Lady Hampden and her party
were beaming on her fun. After Peggy, the house
took a long time to settle again, and when the
curtain had acquired an ache in its backbone from
bobbing up and down so often, and the demand
for more Peggy was as eager as ever, she flashed
out for a moment, and explained she was suffering
from a terrible hoarseness. When she had kissed
her hands to everyone for several minutes, things
settled down once more, and the rest of the pro-
gramme went slowly towards its end. The only
other noticeable feature was Mrs. Maesmore
Morris as Marie Louise in the “ Royal Divorce.”
She cried “Oh!” with a pretty round mouth
that seqmed made to cry “ Oh ! ” on the slightest
provocation.
The gifted Florence Blair, well-known as
“ Cleo ” and “Victorian Girl,” was married in
Melbournelast week toMr. Archibald Bavistock,an
Englishman who is occupied with business in great
waters. It does one good to hear of a bride who
refused to shiver all a winter’s day in midsummer
muslin and orange-blossom, and decked herself
warmly in a deep red cloth costume. Mrs. Bavis-
tock intends to make tier home in Melbourne.
Another daughter of old David Blair wedded a
Sydney journalist last year, and settled down in
Sydney. Both Mrs. Bavistock and Mrs. Hunter are
notoriously brilliant conversationalists who talk
as brightly as they write.
The eloquence with which even the diffident
advertisement will denounce bold bad man and
all his wicked ways, was strongly in evidence the
other day at Circular Quay. She acted demurely,
but glanced invitingly, as she tripped to an
opposite seat on the wharf. He presently loitered
along, and an ocular artillery commenced. They
were too deeply occupied to detect the adjacent
amusement caused by Him and Her respectively,
making conquests of each other directly in front
of a fine large advt. board, bearing the legend:
“ Assurance Coy.”
The funny little dodge of a school marm is here
recorded. Chiefly for the sake of other school-
marms who may like to follow where someone
else has led successfully. Every, year the junior
exam, asks its candidate to write an essay. Of
course the subject of the essay is a dead secret
until the English day comes round, but have you
ever noticed that the supply of subjects suitable
for school girls’essay is—well, just a little limited?
This clever school mistress has sent up many
pupils, and has been through the whole gamut of
essays, from Friendship and Bush Fires down to
Truth and War. And each year she picks out
half-a-dozen likely subjects, and sits her down,
and writes out half-a-dozen essays, which she gives
to her girls to learn by rote. And generally
favored by luck and the weird limitations of
girly-girly matter, she hits the right subject, and
passes her girls with big A’s.
But, hold ! How comes it, if several girls learn
essays by rote, that they can each of them palm
off the self-same essay upon the examiners as an
independent effort ? There is a hole in this story
somewhere.
Another quaint but plaintive story comes from
one of our own suburbs. Two poor old school-mis-
tresses found that their little shilling-a-week
school was not adequate for their bread and rent.
In their straits they tried to sell a handsome
mantle, relic of a moneyed past. The mother
of one of the pupils was induced to purchase. She
named her own terms. Time payment at one
shilling a week. The sisters agreed. An extra
shilling a week was full of meaning to them.
But suddenly the child of this same mother
stayed away from school. Days and weeks passed,
and she still stayed away, and her weekly
shilling with her. The mother continued her
payment until her debt was cleared off. Then the
shilling for the mantle ceased and the child re-
turned to school. And the mother got her mantle
for nothing. The child did not lose much. And the
poor little school-mistresses, who are not as good
at arithmetic as at “accomplishments,” are still
pondering over the profit and loss of their trans-
action.
For a long time the innumerable little weekly
magazines for women have been pouring out pages
of the most appalling instructions for the manu-
facture of health and complexion, and golden hair
and long eye-lashes. A lady who calls herself
Aunt Homebody has, at various times, issued
prescriptions that make doctors’ hair stand on
end. And sometimes she orders sulphate of zinc
instead of sulphur, and strychnine instead of
quinine ; and next week, when the patients are
just about settled in their graves, she corrects her-
self with a calm dignity that snares her a fresh
load of victims. But a sensible book has been
written at last on the science of Beauty Culture.
Ellen Browning, the author of the work in
question, knows her hygiene down to the toes, and
whenever she feels her knowledge shaky, is quick
to say so. One of her hints for a speedy refresher
has been followed by thousands of those hard-
working society women who never have time to
rest from their enjoyments. Hold your face
over a basin of boiling water for five minutes,
covering the head with a towel to keep
in the steam. Then wash first in tepid, then in
clean cold water, tie a silk handkerchief over the
eyes, lie down flat on your back for twenty
minutes or half-an-hour, and you arise a new
woman, with the spine and nervous system rested,
and the tired lines gone from the face.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ At the Greek play a dressy crowd filled the best
seats and wished Euripides was a shade less long-
winded and that “ Greek roots’’learnt at school
had more bearing on his tragedy. “One touch of
nature makes the whole world kin,” and it was
delightful to see the audience smile as one, when a
mourner, as frequently happened, uttered that
well-known word “ Eheu ! ” Acting-Governor
Madden and a joint quartette of daughters and
aides gazed fondly from the balcony on the plump
posturing of son and heir Guy. Across the hall
the Bevan parents tried not to show sinful pride
in their sonny-boy as hero Admetus. White tunic,
purple mantle and chestnut beard were the strong
points of the latter’s royal array.
“ Doctor Beeper gave his classical gentry ever so
much more skirt than was indulged in by Wilson
Barrett or Julius Knight in similar rbles. Claudian
and Marcus Superbus, you remember, affected
thrillingly brief kilts and undressed their legs in
the Highland manner. But the Trinity collegians
wore nice, decent petticoats to the knee and from
that downwards, clean white stockings. Elastic-
side prunella boots would have just put the lacking
touch of perfect propriety to the picture. Nothing
pleases some people. A girl in the audience that
night complained that the rear-view of ‘poor Mr.
Bevan’s tunic was just horrid, exactly like a night-
shirt.’ Come to think of it, it had rather a down-
tailed, betwixt-and-between air of the archaism
referred to. Most of the clothes were made by a
clever lady-sympathiser. Among her few failures
were those green (baize) decollete blouses and
three-quarter-length skirts to match, worn by a
brace of lie-attendants, topped by smoothly-parted
unclassic heads of hair, and freshened up,by white
Stockings down under.
“ A new chapter of the Deeming horror is Kate
Rounsevell’s appeal from Scotland to Melb. police
authorities for valuable love-tokens given to her,
in dear old Australian days, by her scaffolded
sweetheart. Local paragraphists wonder at the
heroine’s hankering after what was virtually the
price paid for the pleasure of killing her by her
would-be murderer. A Melb. girl who, during
the Deeming trial, chanced to chum-up unknow-
ingly in a railway-carriage with the heroine of the
hour, is less surprised at the fair exile’s hard-
heartedness.
“ That day, says her late travelling companion,
La Belle Kate vvas radiant in the best of navy-
blue cashmere and satin, plumed picture hat of
bridal white felt, gold-topped parasol and spick-
and-span gloves and boots. This was to have
been her ‘going-away ’ attire, so she explained,
when breaking the news of her identity. Her
fellow-traveller, nervous after influenza, hopped
off the Deeming topio every time, like a cat
on hot bricks, only to be determinedly dragged
back again by the gruesome prattler. ‘ The handle
of your fruit-knife reminds me of one he’s got,
she would say, ‘but his is as sharp as a razor, and
such a big blade.’ Shuddering listener tried to
seem grateful when her new acquaintance pro-
mised her ‘one of his autograph poems.’ This
literary gem, it seems, began ‘ Oh, Kitty, dear,
and did you hear the news that’s going round ? ’ to
be sung to the tune of ‘ The Wearin’o’ the Green.’
Miss Rounsevell also extolled herself highly in
conversation, because, so she said, she had never
treated her sometime fiance to a kiss, though, she
added, many a ‘society girl ’ would have willingly
linked her fate with his, even in the condemned
cell. At this time the arch-murderer’s mash was
a comely girl with a flawless rose-leaf complexion.
Her weak point, facially speaking, was a bare
build of forehead, her hair growing scantily on the
temples in spite of a fringe at the same time
touzled and weedy.
