“ A bike-prohibition clause in theatrical engage-
ments has been on the list of things inevitable for
months past. Manager Lemmone nearly suffered
an accident to the successful Hambourg season
when younger brother Jakoff ‘ coasted ’ against a
snag and damaged his valuable arm. Since then
the Firm has actually lost money, no doubt, through
the upsets of Mr. Bert and Mrs. Ada Reeve
Gilbert, and henceforth any valuable performer
who gets hurled violently from any breed of
‘wheel’will sustain a compound fracture of the
contract in any case.
“ Re that accident to a widely-esteemea widow
thrown from her horse t’other day : life-long ad-
mirers almost regard the mishap as a providential
intervention to check their favorite in a new and
mistaken course. For long enough, she has been
prized as a rare relic pf the nearly-extinct type of
staid, motherly matron, almost superseded in
sassiety’s topmost layer by the biking, shooting,
capless grandmother. Considering all things, 16
did come as a shock to read of one’s ideal society-
leader, barely out of her year of widowhood, riding
to the hunt and carrying off the brush, especially
as during her married life she never exactly posed
as a sport. Not that the broken bones are the
direct result of horsey recklessness. A quiet
family ride served, strange to say, as the instru-
ment of Fate.
“ A Melbourne penstress devotes about six
inches of reading* matter to an awful exposure of a
Bulletin error re the title of the first Baronet
Clarke’s widow. The Bulletin didn’t consult its
red morocco-bound volume of Burke and Debrett
ere presuming to state that William’s decease
left his Lady Clarke with the same name as be-
fore. Circumstances over which she had no con-
trol have enforced the distinction of the Christian
name upon the amiable lady, it seems. Burke
and Debrett leave no room for doubt. Had
widowed Lady C. been the mother, or grand-
mother, of the new bart, or were he a bachelor
she might have continued to enjoy the old form of
address, but being the step-mother of her late hus-
band’s heir, she has become Janet, Lady Clarke.
This form, says authority Burke, is ‘imperative."’
The penalty of disputing an ‘imperative’ social title
is .£5, or a month, perhaps. It will be safest to
give all widowed stepmothers their Christian
names for the future. So look out for such items
as ‘ Eliza Mrs. Jones looked nice in launder and
black.’
“Nowadays, the ‘nicest’ children Wear their
hair ‘ well-braided and neat ’ like Gilpin’s cloak.
The touzled mop framing the face has gone down
in the world. Nursery tots, at present, are per-
mitted a single row of clustering English curls,
too short to hang into the eyes. Senior lasses band
their smooth, silky tresses with a yellow fillet, and
all together, the tiny maiden of to-day may hope
to grow up with a better head of hair than her
predecessor of the period when the very infants
wore their scanty scalp down in nightly ‘ pins ’
and nurselings of both sexes were more inured to
curling-tongs than to soap.

“ A young Melb. matron, who went poultry-
mad for a while, invested all her substance in a
chicken-farm, and devoted herself, body and soul
house and home, to the craze. She even made
room in her neglected piano for a choice clutch or
two, and was absolutely violent to an unsuspecting
pul-friend who, starting* to strum, drove the
brooders to such frenzied flapping, fluttering and
cackling, that before they could calm themselves
all their prize eggs were addled.
“A young Melb. sassiety matron, meditating
some alterations in the family mansion, had in a
well-known and rather rugged contractor to make
estimates. In the course of his measurements he
came to a small, luxuriously - fitted bedroom,
which he said would have to go. “Oh, Mr,
Plaster, this can’t go. It’s my brother-in-law’s
room. He is a bachelor, you know, and so par-
ticular ! ” “Can’t abide bachelors, mum.” “Not
like bachelors, Mr. Plaster? Why, they’re ever
so much nicer than married men.” “Maybe,
mam ; but when yer sees a marrid man yer knows
the woman as belongs to ’im, but when yer sees a
bachelor yer danged if yer know who she is ! ” He
didn’t get the job.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners.will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
PERSONAL ITEMS.
Anagram. Federation: “ One fat Reid.”
On July 3rd Dean Cowper, Sydney-born,
will enter upon his 89th year.
Sunday, June 26, was the 37th anniversary
of the deaths of Burke and Wills, the explorers.
There are two N.S.W. ex-Attorney-
Generals who do not claim silk —B. R. Wise and
G. H. Reid.
The N.S.W. anti-Federalists are led by
Want and Necessity. Reid is Necessity, for
‘ Necessity knows no law.”
(( The father of the Federal leader—
“ Toby ” Barton —was a Bluecoat boy, and Toby
is the tenth in a family of eleven.
The two oldest medical practitioners in
Sydney are Dr. Hansard, (1839) of Burwood and
Dr. R. D. Ward (1842) of North Sydney.
June 15 was the 55th anniversary of the
first general election in N.S.W. Only one of the
then elected is now alive—Charles Nicholson,
resident in London.
Forrest, the Westralian, is now the only
non-lawyer Premier in Australia. Byrnes, Reid
Kingston are barristers and Turner a solicitor.
And Forreßt is wobbling on his perch.
Attorney-General Isaacs—who, it is said,
ia retained for the R.C. Church in the Crotty will-
case—attended the consecration of St. Patrick's,
and was charmed with Cardinal Moran’s sermon.
The strain of the Federation struggle has
temporarily proved too much for Argus editor
Willoughby, who is ordered complete rest and
ohange, but is at present too unwell even to leave
his home.

Significant of the depth of the unrest in
the gold-rusher that among the first men to leave
Westralia for Klondyke was “ Bob ” Martin, who
had not so long before brought in the 400 oz.
Monarch nugget.
Bishop Reunion, lately of Adelaide, has
scandalised some of the ancients of Bath and
Wells by making his diocesan visitation on a bike,
and remarking that, for the first time, he had
found gaiters an advantage.
Despite the dispute with Premier Kingston
over the Harrold Bros, case, it is said that Insol-
vency Commissioner J. G. Russell is a “dead
bird” for next S.A. Supreme Court Judgeship.
Federal ambitions put Symon, Q.C., out of the
running.
H. : I anticipate that Victor Daley’s book
of poetry—it is poetry—will be the first successful
Australian one of the cosmopolitan type. An
Australian poet has as much right as any other to
appeal universally— to be, if he can, a Shelley
rather than a Burns.
A well-advertised Australian “ explorer ”
has not. yet paid the man, an ex- Argus scribe,
who wrote his (the “ explorer’s ”) last book. That
book is the best that the great “ explorer ” ever
brought out, which is not bestowing on the
Aegus man all the praise his work deserves.

Leslie Thomson, the sick Little Minister
(he has been crippled with rheumatism at Menzies*
hotel ever since he arrived in Melbourne), and
Leslie Thomson, the painter of the picture
“Summer” in N.S.W. National Gallery, are
first cousins. Artist L.T. belongs to the Glasgow
school of painting. His strong point is a Dutch
fidelity to detail and careful observation in land-
scape.

Mrs. Morwitch, an old Scone (N.S.W.)
identity who was buried a few weeks ago in
the Jewish portion of Kalgoorlie (W. A.) cemetery,
attended the opening of that cemetery, and ex-
pressed the hope that she would not be the first to
find a grave there. She was the first Jewess, it so
happened, to take a plot.
Miss Clara Fielding is stated by Yic. rail-
way authorities to be the most capable contractor
for the removal of gate-houses with whom they
have ever dealt. She has a first-class equipment
of teams, cranes, jinkers, and screwjacks, all
belonging to her own presumably pretty self. An
ideal wife for a Tired man !

Queensland Assembly habitues will miss
Nelson and Tozer this session. Nelson’s irritating
cough, when some member on the other side was
trying to convince him of his iniquities, and
lozer s dramatic gesticulations and amazing self-
contradictions, when bowled out by one of the
Labor men, did much to liven things up.
A mounted-rifle corps is being formed at
Charters Towers, Q. The greatest enthusiast
and captain-elect is an ex-sailor, whose knowledge
of equitation is limited, but who boasts that as he
was trained to ride a spanker boom in a gale of
wind, he'll “bet no blooming horse can throw
him. The first parade is eagerly awaited.
Burne-Jones, head-master of the British
“ greenery-yallery ” school of art, died last week.
He was a fine, dreamy painter, and his female
creations had the longest idyllic legs of any women
m the canvas world. The Bulletin has some-
times dreamt, over a pipe, that the kingdom of
Heaven must be like unto a Burne-Jones ballet.
President Richard Chaffey Baker (S.A.)
was 56, and Premier Richard Seddon 53, on
Wednesday, June 22. Premier Kingston, who is a
great admirer of the second Richard, telegraphed
congratulations to Maoriland, but it is to be
feared that he did not send a messenger across
the road with birthday wishes to the other fellow.
Recently-deceased John Gibb, of Ballarat,
was a pioneer of 40 years ago, and, starting as a
millwright, worked his way up to the head of a
big manufacturing business. He was an import-
ant factor in Ballarat’s industrial progress, and
was besides a good fellow and a universal
favorite. Yet Ballarat Courier, after solemnly
recording the drowning of one of the wallabies
intended for Queen .Victoria, passes Gibb out with
the mere formal intimation that he has been duly
“planted.” J
Mr. F. E. Rogers, Q.C., has been once
more moved from N.S.W. Supreme Court to the
District Court Bench. The Reid Govt., in
shuffling Judges and barristers like cards off and
on to the Bench and up and down from major to
minor, is, probably without meaning it, demon-
strating that there should be no such difference
between the functions of the two Benches as the
present law-forms maintain, or between the
capacities and abilities of the occupants of the
Benches as their fixed salaries and pensions indi-
cate.
Broadhurst, the
Sydney Q.C., used,
retirement from practice,
old-time
after his
to attend
the courts pretty regularly as an onlooker. And,
as often as not, he would fall asleep. Once, when
Darvall was addressing the Full Court in an
appeal case, the old man awoke with a start and
cried out, “ Your Honors will find the case in ”
Then he paused, rubbed his eyes, and exclaimed,
“ God ! the ruling passion strong in sleep ! I beg
your Honors’ pardon. I dreamed that—er—yes—
humph,” and off he toddled to get a glass of
brandy-and-water.

Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart, who cele-
brated the “diamond jubilee” of his priesthood
on June 9, was born in “ swate Kildare ” 83 years
ago, on Waterloo day ; graduated at Maynooth ;
had charge of an Indian college at Mutiny time,
and ostentatiously stacked a heap of borrowed
rifles on the balcony of aforesaid college in-
order to bluff the benighted heathen. The Arch-
bishop read the burial service over Dan O’Connell
at Genoa. A man cannot have been 60 years a
priest without having a good many close shaves ;
but the closest shave the venerable prelate had
was 'When he just missed sailing from England in
the ill-fated London.
Melb. : “Tom Monahan’s will comes up
again on a side-issue. He was a groom and
public-house knock-about man, in this city, over
half-a-century ago. Contrary to the usual, he
saved money, and got the site at the N.W. corner
of Swanston-street and Flinders-lane, where he
built the Queen’s Arms, still bearing his. name at
the top. As a good Catholic he had the satisfac-
tion of making the kitchen of the establishment
out of the first Wesleyan Church or Sunday-
school built in Melb. Up to his last illness he
took a plunge in the ocean, at Hegarty’s Baths,
St. Kilda, first thing every morning.”
Leslie Stephen Dudley, the soap-and-Sal-
vation apostle who got gaoled in Melb. the other
day for a fortnight—having refused to pay the
fine inflicted—for preaching in Bourke-street and
soliciting subscriptions for soap, runs a relief
institute in N. Melb., one of whose quaint regula-
tions is that all persons relieved shall wash them-
selves before going away. Charity can be very
cruel when it chooses. Strange to state, however,
L.S.D.’s establishment is well patronised by the
vags. of the city, who look upon soap as an epi-
dermic poison. Perhaps it is the spice of danger
that attracts them ; perhaps it is the soup that
precedes the soap.
Former N.S.W. Principal Under-Secretary
Halloran used to write “poetry,” and could never
understand why it didn’t go. One day, walking
along deep in thought, a neighbor hailed him.
“ Hulloa ! Halloran. How goes it ? ” Halloran
looked up and glared. “Sir,” said he, “if it is
any satisfaction to you to know it, your rude and
vulgar salutation has lost me a
Great Thought! ”

Singer Madame Alva, whom John Lem-
mone has brought out, is married to an old Cock-
ney journalist, St. John Brennan. He was a
violent Irish patriot in the Fenian era, and, since
then, drifted into Tory society journalism. He
edited the defunct Piccadilly, and brought an
action against its aristocratic proprietors for re-
covery of arrears of salary. Brennan floats on
that semi-Bohemian lagoon that divides perpetual
hardupishness from the “hupper suckles,” and
has a varied experience of the journalism that per-
tains to that domain.
STICKING OUT A MILE.
MR. DOOGOOD : “And where does your father work, sonny?”
SONNY: “He don’t do work at all—he’s a perleeceman !”
James Thomson, who represented the for-
gotten electorate of St. Vincent in the first N.S.W.
Responsible Parliament, 1856, was banqueted the
other day in Shoalhaven. The ex-politician, now
in his 66th year, talks of those as the days of the
giants. He and W. B. Dailey began their political
careers together, being about the same age, and,
in the opinion of many, of equal abilities.
Parkes’ prophecies and observations about
Reid, at the time when the latter jockeyed the old
man out of the leadership, are being bandied
about at present. How wrathful the old warrior
was when the choice of N.S.W. Freetrade party be-
came known ! Reid, always a thorn in, as well as
on, the Freetrade side, was the last man expected
to be selected. Parkes’ fury was boundless. “If
they wanted another captain, there was no neces-
sity to put a pir-r-r-ate in my place!” he screamed.
Some of the Melb. papers have been
giving portraits of the orators of Victoria. Pur-
porting to be instantaneous photos., they am
manifest posing and posturing. Forinstance, here
is Trenwith, in a crucificial attitude, with the

Deakin, Burke impeaching Warren Hastings is
nowhere, but the ill-concealed grin shows it is
put-up.

Some Melbourne pressmen actually run
to broad-cloth and bell-topper ! ! Two seen in the
law courts in connection with recent libel actions
were tailored to the last degree. Full froekeoats,
fancy vests and brilliant silk hats ! The jury
absolutely stared when one of these mounted the
witness-stand and swore to being a journalist.
How times do change ! It makes a man think of
the good old Sydney E. News days, when the
deputy market-reporter, who had just had a
smoked pig’s-head presented to him by a grateful
produce-dealer and was walking home in triumph,
heard behind him stealthy footsteps and took to
his heels : he thought that another member of the
staff was stalking him, in quest of half that pig’s
head.
District Court Judge Murray, who has
received charge of the Royal Commission to
inquire into the criminal accusations of Levien,
M.P., against Sleath and Ferguson, Ms.P., takes
top place among N.S.W. public men for the
number of special Government commissions en-
trusted to him. A few of his late prominent
appointments were Local Government Commis-
sioner, Commissioner for dividing the Electorates,
Commissioner for reviewing appeals under Land
Tax Assessments, and Commissioner to inquire
into the charges of Varney Parkes against the
administration of the Public Works Department.
He is a graduate of Sydney University, a mathe-
matician rather than a rhetorician, and pains-
taking over minute details.
Perth (W.A.) is laughing long and loud at
Vosper, the hirsute vaporer of Kanowna. This
modern Absolom is now running the Sunday
Times (started last year by Edward Ellis, late of
Sydneyside), and the other day his paper pub-
lished a “pome” bearing the harmless, unob-
trusive title of “A Fireside Reverie.” An ob-
servant scribe on Perth News next day pointed
out to that sleepy city that the “pome” was
really a rather clever single acrostic—the first
letter of each line forming the uncomplimentary
sentence—
F. C. B. VOSPER IS AN APE !
The office of the Sunday Times is still redolent of
sulphur and burnt hair.

‘ 4 Yarrum ” : The other Saturday a young
half-caste, carrying a sugar-bag containing several
dozen oysters, was half-way across Minamurra
(N.S.W.) bridge when a fast milk-train suddenly
came along from the opposite direction. The
darkie had either to jump 95ft. into the river or be
mangled. He jumped,.and swam a hundred yards
to a point, where, in an exhausted state, he landed
with the oysters. A fellow never knows what he
can do until he has to.

Great commotion in Central Q. chemist’a
shop. Yells and smashing of glass. Bullocky
bolts wildly out of shop, with dental instrument
hanging out of mouth. Chemist in hot pursuit,
catches him in middle of road, trips him up, falls
on him, digs knee in chest. Grasps instrument,
grinds it in and jerks out ancient molar amid the
plaudits of crowd and blasphemy of subject.
* * » *
“ Yarrum ” : A friend of mine on tho
South Coast (N.S.W.), an alderman, suffered
much some years ago from indigestion. One
day he noticed a horse rolling, and, being
a thinking man, he concluded that the animal
was perhaps assisting digestion —as it had
just had a big feed. Every evening, after
dinner, since then he (the alderman) has
rolled, and is now the healthiest man about town.
He gets at it in the backyard; and in the
“altogether,” as clothes hinder the movements.
•‘Let a man,” he says, “eat pollard and roll
plenty, and he’ll be full of health.”

“Mallee” : Turkey-shooting is a precarious
institution. Writer once went out with a friend
in a sulky to bag that guileless bird. We spotted
a hatter on a big plain, and, after circling round
him, got within range and I potted it. At the
sound of the gun our horse bolted ; we were get-
ting to an ugly belt of timber, when Bill, who was
hanging on all he knew, yelled, “ No good, I can’t
hold him—shoot him !” I shot him just behind
the ear, but we didn’t allow for the unanimous
way he’d stop, and, when I picked myself up a
few chains ahead, I was minus pants and half an
eyebrow. After emptying the grass and saltbush
out of my mouth, I cooeeyed for Bill. I heard a
polyglot row in a tree a few yards away, and found
him jammed sideways in a fork, head and feet on
one side, and —well, he was close to “the alto-
gether.” I levered him out with a forked stick,
and his language didn’t show unqualified approval
of the method. We ripped a chaS-bag we had in
the sulky in two, took half each, cut holes to put
our legs through, and secured the slack round our
waists with the reins. We didn’t go back for the
turkey ; and, the whole six-mile tramp home, Bill
varied the monotony of cursing his brother —who
had “ run ” us that the horse was broken to the
gun—by discussing the possibilities of sneaking in
the back way so that the dogs wouldn’t set on us.

More wallaby-track :
The swaggie who never looks for graft, but travels
on the “ game,” is to be met with everywhere in N.S.W.
After he has successfully “touched” you for tobacco
you may chance to see his swag open on the ground and
containing perhaps hundreds of small pieces of “ weed ”
similarly “ cadged.” Sometimes he will offer to sell you
a pound or so of the collection. Came across one of these
old “ river-bend campers” once on r> creek near Bunda-

berg, Q. He was Ashing with a stick, a strip of green-
hide, and a bent pin. Had caught a dozen small Ash of
the mullet species, too. Assured me he had thus existed
for three weeks, being stone-broke and in a place where
you can't cadge. Knew another of the tribe one Sunday
to get some Aour and plums from a station, make a
“ pudding ” in an old bag, cut off half a leg of his
moleskins, and boil the pudding in it with the ends tied
up. The half-leg was afterwards washed in the pudding-
water and sewn on again to be worn.
• * * *
The Old ’Un : Re use of kangaroo-tail. It
seems to me to act as a sort of rudder, because I
have often noticed that when moving sharply the
creature brings its tail to the ground with a smart
thud. Another use. On Broken River once, a
half-bred greyhound of mine was ohasing a big
’roo down a hill and made a dart for the root of
his tail. The kangaroo turned, and the swing of
the tail caught the dog in his ribs and knocked
him over. The beast got away for the time; but
looking over the river-bank, I saw the old chap up
to his shoulders in the water with his head pushed
under the bank. I had a light axe with me.
Leaned over to knock him on the head, but when
he turned his eyes upon me I couldn’t strike. He
was crying like a child, and if ever there was
piteous appeal expressed in a creature’s eyes it
was then. I have never hunted kangaroos from
that day. Which may be sentiment, but is fact.

The average bushman is full of strange
oaths, and takes it for granted that all the rest of
the world has the same cuss-vocabulary as him-
self. A far Western station, where the daily
outlook is pine-ridges, belts of mallee, mulga, red
sand and crows, was run by a bachelor squatter,
whilst the nearest station, 25 miles distant, was
blessed by the soothing influence of matrimony.
The eldest daughter of the latter establishment
and the governess, lady-like girls who would cer-
tainly never use a big, big D under any pressure,
one day drove over to call on the bachelor bull-
raiser and borrow a few stores. On their way
back, just as they left the station, they took a
wrong turning. This was noticed by old Sam,
the stockman, and, jumping on his horse, he made
after the buggy, explained their mistake, and set
the visitors on the right road home. The boss
was at the stock-yard, and Sam mentioned the
circumstance to him. “By Jove, Sam 1 it was

“ Ponte,” to Thb Bulletin :
Your portrait of “ Scotty ” has intensified a long-felt
doubt. I met six of him on the Murrumbidgee, but none
bore any physical resemblance to your photo. The first
one lived in a tumble-down hut that had once been the
station dairy. He made up dam measurements “and
settery,” and was kept in flour and tea. He fished and
shot the remainder of his provender, and I remember
that he held mountain crow to be the “gamiest” of all
game—far ahead of woodcock or grouse. Blue crane was
a common dish, and on several occasions that I dined with
him I got rid of my dinner on learning what had formed
the basis of his inimitable ragouts. He was a 0.E., and
spent his time working out a set of logarithms. It was
only during his quarterly burst that he disclosed his
Identity with “ Scotty.” The next one was flourishing a
oopy of Thb Bdllrtin when I saw him, and in the act of
consigning the whole staff to the uttermost depths of the
lowest inferno, because of some alteration in his “copy.”
The sub. had tried to bring the story to the level of the
ordinary reader, and even then it was as mysterious and
intangible as “ Scotty’s ” personality. When I met the
sixth I concluded that “ Scotty ” was as composite as the
editorial “ We,” and I cannot but doubt that you have
been imposed on as well. Till I meet him in the flesh,
and have him audited and certified to, and his correctness
vouohed for by unimpeachable witnesses, he is a vague
entity to yours£truly.
* * * *
E.M. : Safe way of dingo - stiffening.
Poison a marrow-bone and hang on limb in
locality frequented, with sheets of bark spread
beneath. The heat melts the marrow, which
drips on bark, whence it is licked by the dogs.
Another way : Use the paw of a dog to scratch a
hole and plant therein poisoned meat, scratching
dirt back again with paw. Dingo smells paw-
marks, and thinking he has sprung a fellow-
robber’s plant, gobbles the stuff without hesi-
tation.
» * * *
“ Queenslander ” : In the second number
of the new magazine, the Wide Would, there is
an article, by M. L. Lindsay, on “ Tree-blazing.”
M. L. Lmdsay accompanied his brother on the
luckless Elder expedition. The paper is illustra-
tive of that heroic struggle through the pathless
waste, and contains photos, (and good ones, too)
of trees marked by different explorers met with
by the gallant band. Naturally, the place of
honor is given to a tree marked by D. Lindsay, at
an early stage of the journey. The literary part
of the article is so amusingly ingenuous that it
might have come from the pen of the Rev. Mr.
Barlow, of “ Sandford and Merton ” fame. The
argument seems to be that explorers, in what is
called “ the awful wilderness,” mark trees so that
the next explorer who comes along may know that
there’s water about. Of course, if there isn't any
water at the time of year when explorers 2,3, 4
and 5 come along, they are likely to die “a hor-
rible death.” How the first explorer gets along
without any marked trees is not stated. Extracts
from the journal are given, showing the terrible
fate overhanging the party when their boots wore
out and the soap ran short. Here is one remark :
Of course, it need hardly be said that the head of an
expedition of this kind needs to be a born leader of men,
so great are the difficulties to be overcome.
Then there is an extract showing some of these
great difficulties : “ Had to tar some of the camels
for mange.” “Soled my own boots with green-
hide.” These are termed “ typical notes from
the journal ” of the born leader of men. Here
are some Lindsay-journal extracts not quoted in
the magazine article:
Friday, November 20 —Camp 85. —At seven miles we
oame to a paddock (post and wire-fence) in which was
(sicj two stone tanks with water and some horses. As
the gate was locked with a pair of handcuffs I concluded
it was a police-paddock, and that a township could not
be far off.
Another:
Monday, November 23. —We then went out into the
street and walked up to the next store, where an account
had to be settled. I saw that everybody was mounted,
and asked them if they were right, to which Mr. Leech
replied: “Yes; we are all right.” I mounted, and,
amidst the cheers of the assembled crowd, I took my
place at the head of the caravan, and we moved off along
the road towards Golden Valley or Knutsford.
How’s that for high? The awful wilderness and
the pathless waste!
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.— Be what dingoes will
venture. I have seen them run a Chinaman shep-
herd from the sheep-yard into the hut. It was
during a severe winter in the coldest part of New
England. Also, I have known them, in broad
day, rush a mob of 2000 travelling sheep ; myself
and Buchanan, of Warring station, having some
difficulty in driving them off. This was near
Pallamallawa, on the Big River, and can be
amply corroborated. —Harry Heney.

“Scotty the Wrinkler ” : Your “Un-
deserving Poor” was anticipated by me in a
lecture given to two idiots who worked the sheep
on a Castlereagh station. They had a constant
job, and cursed the drink when my mates and I
got on the tangle and left them to do double and
treble the work. They were both prayerful
youths, and by fair argument I got the younger
and greater driveller to insert a petition “Send
down lots of beer and grog, because without them
Ben and I would get the awful sack, and men
like Dubbo and Scotty would be put on.” His
prayer up to date has been answered.

“ E.S.S.” on some way-back incidents:—
The most pitiable object I ever met on the track was a
lad of 14, staggering under a bundle as big as himself,
with a jam-tin for a “ billy.” He’d been working for a
selector, at Bs. a week, for three weeks, when the bene-
volent cockie professed to knock off 35., on the plea of
insufficient payable work to keep him going. But the
little chap didn't see it, and took to the "track. I smiled
when he asked the distance to the next place—“ makings
of a good battler,” I thought—and “ what sort of a bloke
was the storekeeper?” Another pathetio sight was an
old woman, bending under a bran-bag half-full of indis-
pensables. Had on a pair of heavy bluchers, a felt hat,
black skirt, and a blue dungaree jacket.. Had been house
keeping at an out-of-the-way squatter’s place, and had
quarrelled with her mistress. Her husband and 15-year-
old daughter were rouseabout and nurse respectively on
a station 190 miles further south. “ Maybe we’ll be able
to live together now,” she said. “ We’re better off ’an we
were a year ago.” Then she pulled out an old black pipe.
“Can ye spare me a bit o’ baccy, mate?” Same day, I
met a full-blooded aborigine, with three gaudily-
dressed gins, in a spring-cart drawn by two horses,
with about 60 dogs following. The turnout was his
own private property, bought with the savings
of his wages. He was going to Yalpunga races.
I have frequently met black and half-caste couples riding,
gins with side-saddle, habit and all complete, and-driving
pack horses. Ahead of and behind them, ragged white
men humping bluey. . . . Saw a notable couple once on
the road from Milparinka to Wilcannia. A burlv, six-
foot man and a chubby girl of 14, who carried the billy
and water-bag. She was the man’s wife , and she had left
a baby with her mother in Tibooburra. They were look-
ing for a job. . . . On the Barcoo once met a Chinaman
in quest of a cooking job. The sole of an old boot was
lashed with greenhide and string to each foot. Carried
the customary “ jonkin,” or bamboo, on his. shoulder,
with swag dangling one end and tucker-bag and camp-
ware from other. Putting his load down, he said, “ You
savee Tambo?” “Yes.” “ How mnehee far—how
long?” “Oh, 20 mile yet.” “Oelise!” He picked up
his load again and went off at a great bat. . . . The most
ludicrous spectacle I ever saw on the track was on a road
west of Bourke. A city youth, not yet tanned, in long-
tail coat, hard-hitter and canvas shoes ; a carpet-bag and
gingham in one hand, and tin-whistle in the other. He
was turning round as on a pivot, and playing to a mob of
cows that were running after him. Seeing me, he
pocketed the “ music” and grinned, lie didn’t want to
know anything about roads or rations. Wasn’t even look-
ing for a “job,” but —“remunerative employment!”
Casually inquired what street I called this—the Milparink .

road 1 Also asked, “What’s the price o’bed and meals
’ere ? ” 1 told him best thing he could do was to play
“ I’m for slitherin’ back to town,” and slither. . . . I once
met an old fellow pounding along with a gingham over

swag and etceteras. This dog would bring wood in his
mouth for the fire, and fetch a billy of water from the
oreek. Good judge of a sheep, too, and would single a fat
one out of a flock in quick order, and hold it until his
boss came along. Swaggie mostly bought them in the
flock in order to practise “ Tracker.” The shepherds
greatly admired the dog, which, while the sheep was
being dressed, would scratch out a big hole, into which
he would drag the skin, head and other waste, and cover
them up. When the old chap told me he was never short
of meat I believed him.

In the good days, when sly-grog shanties
abounded “out-back,” an affable old woman, who
kept a roadside house near Bourke, was known to
do a big liquor-trade, but, despite all police efforts
to catch her, she always came out on top. Once,
two young troopers—new to the “ foorce ”—re-
solved to capture her. Disguised as shearers, with
packhorses, swags, etc., they rode up to tin;
shanty and asked if they could camp for the night.
The proprietress took them in unsuspiciously, and,
after tea, when they hinted at being thirsty, she
innocently produced several bottles of assorted
spirits, and served up drinks in style. The de-
lighted “traps,” imbibed freely, got a flask each


Messrs. John Hunter anti Son Ltd. are
doing a tremendous business with cycling shoes
made of “ kangara,” a leather which is unequalled
for wear and appearance, and looks splendid in
gentlemen’s lace and button boots and also in
ladies’and children’s boots and shoes of all descrip-
tions. “ Otto Herz,” too, is a make with which
John Hunter is holding the attention of those who
desire to be artistically shod. The American
factories are constantly watched by the firm’s
buyers, and the latest, handsomest and most
durable stock is constantly obtained for the Aus-
tralian trade. At present Mr. John Hunter is in
England; therefore Australian people may rest
assured that what is best in boots and shoos there
will be secured by “The Australian Boot King.”*

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
WATSON’S WHISKY.
At some recent low-tides it has been pos-
sible to walk right round the hull of the Maitland
—the Sydney-owned steamship wrecked by the
May gales, on her voyage to Newcastle, with ter-
rible loss of life. The unlucky vessel broke amid-
ships, and the accompanying photo, shows the
position of the stern-part, from which the sur-
vivors made their way ashore in the teeth of the
breakers. The inquest of the shipowners’ Marine
Board on the corpse of the ancient Maitland
resulted in the familiar act-of-God verdict. The
Bulletin is glad to notice, by the way, that Pre-
mier Reid has definitely promised that the
fossilised and discredited Marine Board will
shortly be swept away, and a more impartial and
enlightened tribunal formed, as recommended by
the Public Service Commissioners.
“ Wahirini” : The Bulletin talks about
W.A. mal-adminiBtration. Did you ever hear of
the Govt, locomotive that got lost? Was left on a
siding one night and wasn’t found for months till
the man who had been using it for his own private
purposes ran it back. Did you ever hear of the
firm that consigned by rail half-a-dozen ploughs to
a customer at Geraldton, and received a letter
some time after from Singapore, stating that the
ploughs were on the wharf there? Did you ever
hear of the contractor who was twice paid his final
payment on a contraot —somewhere about £BOOO ?
You never heard of the meat-truck despatched
from Perth which was not detached from the
train till it got to Coolgardie. Nor, perhaps, do
you know the following: A consignee made a
claim on the customs for two boxes of butter
wrongly delivered, and by which his shipment was
short. “ Here,” said an official, “ take this crate
of bent-wood chairs and call it square. We can’t
find an owner for ’em.” Do you know how the
W.A. telegraphs leak all over?

Wesleyan.
A N.S.W. legislator declares that the
“ preju-juices ” against teetotalism are very
strong. So they are—overproof.
Bull backs down again over the Niger
by giving France various territories in return for
towns and kingdoms seized from time to time in
the face of his indignant protests. Fact seems to
be that the Britisher has bitten off more than he
can chew. His numerous protectorates and
hinterlands, with their daily fighting and dreaded
fevers, are a constant drain upon his military
strength (which is none too boastworthy) and he
is, possibly, teaching the niggers and brown men
how to use Maxims and Martinis to his own
ultimate cost.
The American Archbishops of the R.C.
Church are daily praying that their country may
wallop Spain. But, seeing that Spain put in first
claim to the Blessing of the Saints, and is dis-
tinctly under the protection of those early pietists,
the Western section of R.C. faith can’t have any
influence worth mentioning to back it up. The
Saints are now being tested for what they are
worth in a civil-war of superstition. If they don’t
turn up trumps for the Dons it will look as though
their venerable bones and other relics are played
out.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S.W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Beware of imitations. Senior,
chemist, George-street Sydney.*

S.S. : A Gippslander tells of the origin of
his “ rise. ” “ One day over there at the foot of the
ridge I picked up a stone to throw at my dog
who’d been chasing sheep. Spot brought the stone
back again, an’ dropped it near, an’ I picked it up
to have another shot, but saw gold glistening
where the dog had wet it with his tongue. It was
a half-ounce specimen, an’ set me hunting for the
reef. I found it in half-an-hour and, and—well
here I am.” “And the dog?” “Oh, I had to
shoot him, he was such a terror for worrying
sheep.”

“Snell”: Sometime ago a young fellow
was tried in a Victorian town for the alleged theft
of a watch and chain, the property of his em-
ployer, a boss cockie. The case was very black
against the boy. It was shown that the owner
had hung his vest, in the pocket of which was the
watch, on a fence while felling a tree, and
that no other person but the boy had come
near the place in the interval between the
hanging-up of the vest and the disappearance
of the watch. The accused protested his
innocence, and as the watch was undis-
coverable he got the grudging benefit of the
doubt, and was told to “clear,” and be more care-
ful for the future. Some time after the boss lost
a cow under mysterious circumstances, and sent
for a vet. to hold a post-mortem. The cow was
duly opened and thoroughly inquested, and in the
course of his investigations the medical officer dis-
covered that the animal had died of pleuro. What
do you think of that ?
• * * *
A Chiltern (Vic.) vigneron was hauled be-
fore the local bench lately on a charge of hawking
wine. He was fined £2 and £2 2s. costs, and his
horse, cart, and wine-vessels were confiscated.
Hard lines on a man who was doing his humble
best to push an industry which will be the salva-
tion of Victoria sometime or other. That Chiltern
man was supplying a long-felt want. In the good
days coming, the liquor-seller will call around on
his customers as regularly as the baker or milk-
man, end supply them with beer, or wine, or rum,
or whatever other accursed drink they may re-
quire. And steady respectable citizens will leave
their jugs on their window-sills for their morning
revivers. And there will be an extensive tick-
system, and every other citizen will be genial and
stoush any of his neighbours who are not genial
and mellow. And the sun will rise every morning
in glazed-eyed glory and set with a red face of the
color of rum. And everything will be Gay.

selves to a certain planter per medium of an agent

it presumes nobody follows that profession from choice
Hospital Hogs.
“ Hermes ” upon the above subject:—
Publio opinion was recently very much shocked because
Broken Hill Hospital held a corpse as security for paymen t
of maintenance-fees ; yet in one Australian city hospital I
know of—perhaps the same thing happens in others as well
—greater outrages are inflicted upon the living. It is a
common thing for the dispensary department, without
wamtng, to stop the medicine of out-patients, no matter
how painful and dangerous their disorders, because they
have not the necessary shilling to renew their tickets at
expiry of two months’ period. Small though the sum is,
it is often more than the poor patients happen to have at
the time, and though presumably the department has
authority for its action, it is a very rotten brand of
charity that rakes in thousands of pounds by subscrip-
tion and subsidy for the relief of the sick poor
and then cuts off a perhaps dangerously - sick
patient’s physio for the sake of a shilling! Taken
on the whole, the dispensary staff would be much im-
proved if somebody wiped the floor with it and injected
beneath its official hide a little sympathy of manner and
delicacy of feeling. Doubtless among the out-patients are
many objectionable characters, male and female, but
there is always a fair proportion of decent people among
them, and this is how they are sometimes treated : A
dispensary-official oalls a patient to the medicine-window
—a shy girl of about 18, evidently of good up-bringing
and retiring manners ; he pushes out bottle of medicine
and paper packet, and, in a strident voice, instructs her
how to use it with the enema three times per day. The
poor girl almost drops as the ribald crowd around
guffaws brutally at her piteous embarrassment. All such
instructions are supposed to be appended in writing to
the bottle or packet. Again —same hog calls up a woman
old enough to be his mother, and demands : “ What are
you taking these pills for?” “Because the doc-
tor told me, sir,” simply replies the confused woman.
“Are you?” asks hog, and then loudly pro-
ceeds to inform her—and all the other patients
as well—of the exact action of those pills. And
for no other reason, apparently, than that he just feels
that way. Then a football scrum is not in it with the
way the patients are allowed to crush and wrestle round
the medicine-window. No attempt to keep order. As
long as they do not cheek the dispensary people the
strong are free to thrust the weak brutally aside, and
feeble patients, in their endeavors to pierce the crush
when their names are called, receive many an effective
shove along the road to dissolution. Seems a pity that
an institution with indoor arrangements so admirably
conducted and honorary doctors doing such splendid
work, should allow its reputation to be grossly sullied by
the brutal mismanagement of one department.

Flinders Petrie’s latest Egyptian excava-
tions, of 3400 years ago, show that beer was the
national drink. Herodotus says the Egyptians
made no wine.

ing recently on “ The Toilet,” quoted the case of
a woman in America (you bet !) whose hair had
grown to 18ft. in length, and was habitually used
as a bed-quilt by its owner on winter nights.

MELBOURNIANA.


No Humbug' Here.
AUSTRALIAN ENDORSEMENT IS WHAT
COUNTS WITH AUSTRALIANS.
“You can’t fool all the people all the time.”
They will find you out at last.
Every time one is fooled
Another sceptic is made.
Many the remedy that makes the sceptic.
They fail to fulfil their promises.
Warner’s Safe Cure brings renewed faith.
It cures the sceptic.
Plenty of proof of this at home.
Reliable Australians say:
“ It cures backache,
Cures kidney disorders,
Cures diseases of the liver.”
Conviction for sufferers.
Rev. R. A. Caldwell, Presbyterian Minister, Mt.
Gambier, S.A., writes “ I have much plea-
sure in saying that I have used several bottles
of Warner’s Safe Cure, and derived benefit
from it. I have also heard others bear similar
testimony to the good effects reaped by the
use of it.”
This reverend gentleman does not testify to
the remedial value of “ something just as good.”
He used Warner’s Safe Cure.*
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
The loudly-expressed conviction of the
N.S.W. anti-Bill party that it is the duty of Bar-
ton to run against Reid for King Division is a
pretty conspicuous trap with the hook and the big
hunk of tainted meat visible on the surface. No
army puts its general—the one man whose loss
may mean ruin—in the front rank of the forlorn
hope. Reid never considered it his duty, when
the fiscal issue was on top, to abandon his safe seat
in a Freetrade constituency and go and fight Lyne
among the solid Protectionists of the Hume. Not
much !
S.A. Oppositionists mourn the fact that
Symon, Q.C., their only possible leader, and a
bitter enemy of Kingston and all his works, is not
in Parliament. Downer is a conspicuous failure
as Opposition leader, but the other prominent
Oppositionists are riven by petty jealousies, and
decline to follow each other. Symon devotes his
whole time to his big legal and wine-making busi-
nesses, but the Federation movement has kindled
within him a desire to resume politics. The
general election next May may therefore see him
in harness again.
Premier Kingston announces that he is
determined to carry his long-shelved Law Reform
Bill through S.A. Parliament this year. He will
have a very warm time. Kingston has been
prominent in the Harrold insolvency litigation,
and, rightly or wrongly, is accused by his enemies
of having a finger deep in the pie. The S.A.
Premier now proposes, inter alia, a simple exam-
ination for legal aspirants, admission of women as
solicitors, and abolition of “precedents” and
“ case law.” Most S.A. lawyers live in dread of
cheap law and the destitute asylum.
The Age allows a correspondent, with the
honors of leaded type, to exactly describe its own
attitude towards Federation, while discussing the
remarks of the American press with regard to the
prospects of a British alliance. Says the corre-
spondent :
One can imagine a newspaper, or papers, writing an
occasional article that their citizens do not approve of,
but when they are not approved of by their citizens, the
papers do not go on continually writing matter that is
disapproved of generally.
This is what the lawyers call a “ damaging ad-
mission.”
By a vivid description of that hideous all-
night orgy in N.S.W. Assembly S.M. Herald
would have ruined the chances of many of the
E resent members for re-election, and so would
ave helped the Federal party. But the Herald
isn’t built that way. Also, had it been less stodgy
and TiMES-y in its methods—did it but possess
the modern methods, the verve and ginger and
500-horse picturesque lying-power of the rival
Daily Telegraph —it might have done much to
whip up the balance o£ the needed 80,000 Federal
votes. Alas ! the Herald only suggests a cross
between Divine Revelation and the Maitland
Mercury.
The Co-Operative Wool and Produce Co.,
Ltd. —During the coming wool season the magnificent
stores of this successful company, at Central Wharf, will
be taxed to their uttermost. The sampling floor (300 ft.
by 100 ft.) is the finest in the colony as regards equal
diffusion of light and absence of all obstructions. Receiv-
ing and delivering of wool can be carried on with the
greatest possible speed and efficiency, and clients of the
company, growers and buyers alike, may rely on their
nterests being in every way well looked after.*
The N.S.W. liquidarity Labor - party,
after banging into George Reid for four years,
after describing him as politically worthless ex-
cept to ratify its desires, after pounding the
breath out of Gould and Sydney Smith and occa-
sionally jumping on the pious Brunker and the
melancholy and nervous Young, now recognises
that in the Govt, success lies its only salvation.
Consequently, the Reid party is now mainly
defended by Labor members, and described as all
that is good and noble. No doubt Reid owes
much to the Labor-party, for he copied from it
his now famous feat of speaking one way and
voting another.
H. M. Nelson’s voice in the famous
M'Gregor-Nelson correspondences re Papua talks
thusly:—
The concessions will settle I li<* Papuan labor question,
for the native New Guinea labor being required at home
Queensland will have to look elsewhere.
Just fancy old Nevermind jubilating to think
the Papuan wouldn’t be' violently wrested front
the maternal apron-strings ! T. J. Byrnes an-
nounced the other day that he would strenuously
oppose the concessions because the syndicate pro-
posed to cultivate sugar, and thereby compete
with the Q. planters. Interesting to compare the
varying points of view taken by great men on the
same question.
Always leave a loophole in your” matri-
monial engagements ; it saves a lot of law-costs
and damages and worry. The English papers
tell a story of a Kentish farmer, recently deceased,
who became engaged in 1870. He thought he
loved the girl well enough to marry her, so they
became engaged, and he promised by letter that
the wedding would take place as soon as lie had
finished threshng a certain stack of wheat. Then
he began to hesitate, so he left that particular
stack over till 1871. In 1871 he found that he
was hesitating more than ever, so he postponed
the stack indefinitely. It was still waiting to be
threshed when he died in January, ’9B, and the
girl was still waiting also. She made one attempt
to sue him for breach-of-promise, but the lawyers
considered the saving clause about the stack of
wheat, and decided that she had no case.
HEALTH IN THE BOTTLE.
“D.C.L.” Scotch is a delicious and finely
blended whisky of excellent flavor, which, while
maintaining all the due stimulating properties of
the spirit, has been mellowed by age into a per-
fectly wholesome and mild beverage.*
The war between America and Spain
could easily have been prevented by a concert of
the European Powers. Neither nation, nor both
combined, could have dared raise one word of dis-
sent to the concerted arbitrament of Europe.
What a triumph it would have been for civilisa-
tion had such a humanitarian project been initi-
ated and carried out! If all had known the merits
of “ Our Chief ” Tobacco all would have ended in
arbitration and the pipe of peace.*
In Palestine drunkenness is known as
“the English disease.” Britishers may not b ;
more unsober, as a nation, than, say, the Russians;
but they travel more, and carry the Gospel of
Drink to the uttf rm wt ends of the earth.

NOTH—Our ONLY Australian Address— 90 KING STREET, SYDNEY.
The Rebellious Coal-Mine Owners.
The coal-mine owners of the three N.S.W.
districts, associated for this occasion only,
have introduced a brand-new political method
to the province. The greater part of a year
and a half ago an Act was passed, after long
and hard battling, to better regulate the work-
ing of their mines ; and among tlieprovisions was
one to the effect that where men *were paid by
the ton for hewing the coal every “ skip ” or
truck of the mineral should be weighed. It
was a plain, straightforward, unmistakeable
provision, and it was adopted by Parliament
deliberately. The Assembly mostly, it is true,
went home to bed while the measure was being
talked about; but in the Council every line
was fiercely searched before, in a much-amended
form, it was agreed to. Now, 15 months after-
wards, when miners ask that the Act should
be set going and the weighing provision en-
forced, the owners issue an official warning
that if anything like that is done they will all
shut up shop. And as these three districts
provide about four-fifths of all the coal that is
burned in and exported from Australia, they
would be bosses of the situation if they could
do what they say ; and if they could go on
doing it long enough Parliament w'ould have to
crawl down. (This is assuming that the enor-
mous size of the crisis didn’t make the nation mad
enough to pass an Act confiscating the coal-mines
bodily, and thus fire the owners out. When a
whole nation is kicked at one time by a mere
handful of owners it may get angry enough for
anything). If there were internal dissensions,
that would, of course, spoil the scheme ; and dis-
sensions might possibly be helped on by Govern-
ment proceeding to open up a few coal-mines
on its own account with extraordinary vigor.
There would have to be faced the awkward
fact that the province has already got rid of
almost every desirable coal-mine site, so far as
present knowledge goes ; yet the Government
experiment, if thus rendered costly, would, at
any rate, probably make a scatter in the other
camp.
r * * * *
However, matters have not yet gone so
far. Whether every coal-skip ought to be
weighed or whether the present system should
be continued is a comparatively small matter,
and not worth fighting about. Weighing-
machines would cost something, but not much ;
and the objection of the owners on the ground
of that expense is mostly sham. Just about as
ingenuous is the miners’ demand to have every
truck weighed. A few scandalous exceptions
apart, their troubles about the weighing 1
What they really do want is to be paid
for all the coal they break ; while, as the
rates have .been fixed on the basis of pay-
ment for only big or “ round ’’-screened coal,
payment for everything, small coal as well as
big, is really what the owners kick against. If
the provision were enforced and all coal had to
be paid for, the owners say that, the basis
being altered, the hewing-rate would have to
come down. But attempted reduction of
hewing-rate would mean a strike, and, since
competition became keen, owners have de-
veloped a habit of going back on one another
in such crises. From their point of view,
therefore, it- is wise to avert, by bluffing or
otherwise, the action that would put a strain
upon their loyalty. Whether they are likely
to be any more loyal in an actual strike against
Parliament cannot be stated with any degree
of certainty because, as said before, this is a
brand-new idea. And yet it is only new so far
as the alleged unanimity of the rebellious
owners goes. Every time a Govt, has proposed
to tax absentee landlords, for instance, it has
been threatened with the “ withdrawal of
capital from the country.” The Government
has not, as a rule, cared much, because at the
worst the land would remain ; and if
the landlord were contumaciously inclined
and would not allow the land to be
used the prospect of a still stiffer tax has gener-
ally led him to the penitent-form. Something
in that way might be done now, no doubt—if
the Govt, were game.
» * * *
The question is, What sort of a chance
in the un-reformed Legislative Council would
a Bill have which proposed to thus bring
the rebellious owners to a sense of the
supremacy of the Parliament that represents
the people? And still, if Parliament isn’t pre-
pared to assert itself, the rebellious owners
must win always provided they hang to-
gether. But suppose Parliament does not
assert itself, preferring to announce that it
is now satisfied that it made an ass of itself
and passed impossible legislation ? The answer
is obvious. To weigh all coal isn’t impossible ;
what may be impossible (and that point cannot
be settled off-hand) is either the continued pay-
ment of the current hewing rate or the main-
tenance of only the current selling-rate under
the new conditions as to weighing. And, as
the miners say that, for their part, they are
willing to take the risk of the former, there
seems no reason why the Government should
call Parliament an ass on its own account
and keep the law inoperative. Anyway,
before it crawls down it ought to be ready with
a first-class excuse. The Australian working
men, who seven or eight years ago began to
look to firesticks and battering-rams and other
tools, were only turned aside from that policy
by the vigorous preaching of the gospel of sal-
vation through Parliament ; but if they now
find that, employers having had time to take
the measure of tilings, Acts of Parliament can
be defied by them just as effectively, and more
comfortably and more economically, and with
far less danger of a ruinous burst-up in their
own camp than when workmen were defied, the
chances are that the discarded idea will be
taken up again. Government and Parliament
may then crawl down if they like ; but they
will have to take a pretty big risk of making an
unpleasant dust when they land at the bottom.
True, there will most likely be an unpleasant
dust, anyhow ; for though the law can compel
owners to weigh their coal, there isn’t, unfor-
tunately, any law which will even indirectly
prevent them from insisting upon a reduction
of the rate for hewing it.
The 9th Axiom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“No, I oan not understand,” the woman said.
“You tell me that ‘The Manxman ’ is a tragedy.
It may be, but I have read, looking eagerly for
greatness in it, and all I know is that though it
is now several days since I finished the book, I
have still a nasty taste in my mouth.”
The man looked up at the woman and smiled.
His smile distinctly said he was sure that some
day she would think differently, and understand.
But the woman did not see the smile. She sat
above the man lying on the grass, and looked
straight ahead.
“You do not understand,” he said slowly,
measuring each word.
“No; and I don’t wish to. Kate and Philip !
Pete’s struggles! Self-sacrifice; forgiving nature !
Sickening ! All of it! ” She shuddered.
“ I can understand close communion in thought
and word, appreciation of each other’s efforts,
healthy and enjoyable companionship, trust, re-
speot. But this other that makes a woman to be
as Kate ! ”
She shivered again.
The man lying on the grass turned over, and
took up a paper.
“ All right,” he said. “ The writer of the book
in question is by no means my favorite author,
but the play of passion is well worked out in
4 The Manxman,’ and I used it to show you that
the sensual is stronger than the intellectual.”
He reached over and touched her hand.
She smiled.
“No, we will not quarrel,” she said.
He looked at her. True, she did not under-
stand. The touch of his hand was to her the
index of a desire to be friends.
He turned over again, flung away the paper, and
began to talk of “ Sesame and Lilies.”
“Ah ! I never knew wliat a woman’s influence
might be until I read ‘ Sesame,’ ” she said, with
enthusiasm.
He smiled again, but differently.
“ And ‘ Marcella,’ ” she said. “ What did a
woman do there ? ”
“From Ruskin to Mrs. Ward,” he cried,
mentally. “ The vagaries of woman’s mind !
Made a fool of herself, and interested nobody,”
he answered aloud.
“Who made a fool of herself—the writer or
Marcella ? ”
“ Both.”
She did not answer.
“Do you not think that mere interchange of
thought is cold comfort ? ” he asked presently.
She smiled, but again she did not comprehend.
“Listen !” he said. “Up there, through the
trees, are the sun and the sky ; and the sun is
warm, and the sky blue, and there’s a sense in
them and in the air that fills one’s veins. The
insects are humming and buzzing, the birds in the
trees are singing, and twittering, and loving and
mating.” He paused a moment, then went on :
“ And the low of the cow from the meadow, does
it call to the sire on the hill ? ”
He paused again.
“ Motherhood ! How infinitely sweet to
woman 1 ” he exclaimed, as speaking to himself.
She looked at him with cold, wide-opened eyes.
He went on : “Of the beast, beastly ; of the
earth, earthly. You need not speak. I know
what you are going to say. But man has evolved.
He is the highest creation. Single affection ;
spiritual, intellectual, sensual, in one, is the plane
on which he stands to-day— love— in its full
sense 1 ”
He put out his hand again. She did not move
hers. She let it lie, and merely looked at him
with curiosity.
All nature was meeting in love that day, and
these were two of her creatures, yet—how dif-
ferent 1
Later, the work of life separated them from daily
association.
“ I miss your arguments,” she wrote, “ though
I never understood, or agreed that you could, in
ever so small a degree, be right. One thing, how-
ever, I must admit, interchange of thought at a
distance is not so agreeable as when together. Is
there anything that establishes the correctness of
your theories in this admission ? ”
The man read, and agreed ; but he did not
answer. While daily association had been theirs,
the intellectual had sufficed him, because a dor-
mant influence had underlain. In himself he
knew that it had only slept at his will; it slept
consciously. Apart, he drifted away. In her the
influence was both dormant and unconscious ;
therefore she did not change, be he near or far.
Months passed. She did not hear from him.
She missed him, but did not suffer. She com-
muned with herself and became daily more intro-
spective.
One night she leant over the balcony of a sea-
side hotel, watching the moon’s long white way
over the waters.
The night was warm, and the lights and shades
were intense.
Voices reached her from a garden-seat below.
She had thought herself alone. She started. One
voice she recognised.
“ There are no theories in this, little woman.
We only know. ”
It was he. Standing in the moonlight she saw
him. As he spoke he took a round, warm, little
woman in his arms and kissed her again and again.
Then the woman on the balcony understood.
The sensual had permeated and become one with
the intellectual.
The whole is greater than its part.

“ Melb.” : Coroner Youl departed for
Elijahland without fulfilling his mission of putting
down the cheap and tindery flannelette. I got a
fright over it, in my own household. A cuff
caught fire, which flew up to the elbow in a
moment.
From an “ Australian ” tale in Windsor
Magazinb : “ Montague told his story in full as
they walked along deserted Circular Quay and
round to Milson’s Point, where the trading vessel
lag.” Rather a frisky walk up the “deserted”
(ye gods !) Quay to the Point devoted to the use of
the giant galleons of the North Shore Perry Co.
H. : “An Indian lady interested me more
than anything, at Melb. Old Colonists’ carnival.
I think she is the only one in the city. Petite
and eminently graceful, she was not a mere lay
figure, but, knowing English well, transacted the
business of her Oriental stall in bright style. Her
tiny leaf of a hand was a glove-size smaller than
any other in the place.”
The sons of W.A. stick closely to
the old blood-and-horror methods of the day when
their brutal ancestors were lagged. Recently, a
prisoner, age I 22, doing five years for a small
theft, climbed over the wall and broke away ; he
was re captured, and sentenced to wear 251 b. irons
for 12 months. Also, a two-line par. in a daily
paper states that a prisoner, who had just been
flogged in Premantle gaol, is dying. Prisoners
die pretty fast in Premantle gaol, especially after
a rousing W.A. flogging.

Same old George- - always different.
“Federation is in the hair.’'— Parkes.
Now, George Reid is bald. No hair, no Federa-
tion.
Presidents of disjointed Australian Re-
publics, a la South America—Reid, Turner, Kings-
ton, Forrest.
Premier Reid is a man of many parts.
But the hole he has got himself into now is greater
than all the parts.
The foxes have holes, and the birds of the
air have nests, and No-Yes Reid is keeping N.S.
Wales Agent-Generalship open for himself in case
of accident.
The 80,000 minimum was imposed by the
votes of 78 members of N.S.W. Legislative As-
sembly, representing many thousands less than
80,000 electors, and 28 members of the Leg.
Council, representing nothing in particular.
A free and independent elector at Narra-
mine (N.S.W.) was asked “Are you going to
vote for Fed eration ? ” “Freddy who?” “For
Federation.” “Blasht Freddy Reardon ; my
mahn’s Morgan ” (the ex-member for the Bogan).
A BROKEN REED.
Gorgey Porgey, glass in eye,
Joined the Federation cry ;
But when the people went to vote,
Georgy Porgey turned his coat.
“Kati-Kati” : What good would America
be to Britain in the event of an immediate war ?
“ Latent resources ” are of no use against a nation
that is ready, and unless you can defend latent
resources you cannot rightly be said to possess
them.
Last week’s proceedings of the dying pro-
vincialist Parliament of N.S.W. presented a loath-
some example of provincialism, where the little
Freetrade parochialist fought his little Protec-
tion parochialist brother for the last scrap of the
mangy corpse of fiscalism.
Treas. Holder, of S.A., threatens to over-
shadow Premier Kingston, though, when Parlia-
ment gets fairly into session
the latter will soon prove
who is the strong man
there. Holder has, how-
ever, made much pro-
gress lately, and was
a stronger pro-Fede-
ration factor in S.A.
than his chief. Till
lately he simply
poured out unbrok-
en streams of argu-
ment upon his lis-
teners with a Meth-
odist - pulpit man-
ner ; now he is
cultivating a patch
of humor which he
has discovered, and
so becomes about
the most generally
acceptable and forci-
ble speaker in the pro-
vince.
The latest inven-
tion in Maoriland
politics is that the
Government in-
tends to sell the
Bank of N.Z. to a
syndicate and retire
from State banking

N.S.W. Opposition-Leader Lyne, who has
been mostly anti-Federal, and Premier Reid who
has been anti-Federal all his life, are now so
agitated about the sacred standard of union that
neither can think of leaving that pure and noble
thing in the hands of the other fellow.
The Judge highest in position in France—
corresponding in status to an Australian Chief-
Justice, but administering law over thirty times
the population of N.S.W. or Victoria—draws a
salary of ,£llOO per year. Less than one-third of
what Sam Griffith voted himself in Queensland.
Is the Australian native in Queensland
always a Tory ? This question is forced by the dis-
tressing spectacle of the Northern branches of the
Australian Natives’ Association abasing them-
selves before their new-born, peerless Panjandrum
—T. J. Byrnes. Will Australia have to import
her Democracy to supply deficiencies in the native
crop ?
N.S.W. Protectionist party has missed a
splendid chance of killing two political birds with
one stone—first, relieving itself of the incubus of
Lyne ; and next, consolidating itself on the legiti-
mate side of Federation. In attempting to cover
Lyne’s retreat, it has taken upon itself the burden
of his blunders.
Of the 125 N.S.W. electorates, 122, at the
late Referendum, recorded informal votes running
from Ito 19. The five distinguished for a perfect
poll in this respect were Balmain South, Gundagai,
Sydney Cook Division, Sydney King Division and
Yass. In four of these latter, the vote was for
the Bill; in Yass, against.

John Norton, M.L.A., had been in
N.S.W. Assembly just four days when he was
hustled outside by the Speaker’s order for saying
t apropos of nothing in particular.
John, judging from his maiden speech, evidently
finds the atmosphere of Parliament very different
from that of the hustings. His first speech, as he
himself admitted, fell flat.
The Vic. Ministry says it has decided not
to renew the bounty—if it can be called one—
granted to the local coal industry in the shape of
reduced freights. That alleged bounty, and the
dead certainty that it was going to be renewed
and increased, were among the choice lies with
which the N.S.W. anti-Billious persuaded the
blinded local voter on June 3.
In the Far Northern agricultural areas
such progress has been made that Chinese capi-
talists can afford to hire kanakas at 15s. a week—
rather more than many white men are receiving
at present from the cookies. By-and-bye the
kanaka will sub-let his job to the mean white,
J a P e > a feeble sort of way, the grandeur of
the Chow contractor who bosses them both.

has obtained from S. A. Govt, has never been pub-
lished. -Ihe scheme will be opposed by the
Laborites and many other Ms.P. who object to
such works being controlled by a private co.

N.S.W. Speaker Abbott's suppression of
John Norton when, on taking his seat, the latter
complained that a member of the Leg. Council
had taken part in a recent election for the Leg.
Assembly, left a constitutional point in doubt. A
member of the House of Lords is not permitted to
interfere in the election of members to the Com-
mons, and Speaker Abbott, instead of suppressing
John when half-heard, should have taken the
whole tale and set at rest a point doubtful in Aus-
tralia.

Premier Reid promises, before the House
dissolves, to pass a Bill providing for a Referendum
on the N.S.W. fiscal question. Naturally, he
will impose his minimum of 80,000 on the Protec-
tionists, who have to show cause for the change.
And, of course, Opposition-leader Lyne will vote
for such a minimum on the fiscal question as he
did on Federation. He owes this to his con-
sistency.
Batchelor, M.P., has been elected leader
of S.A. Parliamentary Labor-party. He is by
far the ablest man in the Labor ranks, but, like
M'Pherson, will probably prove a poor leader.
With the secession of Charleston, M.L.C., the S.A.
Laborites lost the only man who was popular and
influential outside party ranks. Batchelor is
Kingston's senior in the representation of West
Adelaide.
“That tired feeling.” A few days ago a
bush elector rode into Narrabti (N.S.W.) and
found great excitement. He pulled up his horse
and cocked one leg over the saddle in a reposeful
attitude. “ What’s up here ?” he enquired. “Don’t
you know ? Why the general election, of course.”
(Sounds of cheering in the distance.) Bushman
reflectively: “General election, eh! I s’pose
Charley Collins ’ll get in ? ”
Sydney pressmen are of opinion that the
uproar in N.S.W. Assembly on Friday morning
last surpassed anything they have ever seen.
Between 30 and 40 members were on their feet at
once, and, when Chairman M‘Court opportunely
remembered the breakfast-bell, five or six fights
seemed imminent. The onlookers blame Mr.
M‘Court for the rumpus, as he completely lost
control of the House early in the sitting.
A fable. It was in the Zoo. Some of the
animals were criticising the pelican :
“Don’t think much of that for a bill,” said the ass;
“you couldn’t very well eat thistles with it.”
“ Or munch meat,” said the bear.
“ Or catch fleas,” said the monkey.
“ It would be in the way when one was creeping," said
the tortoise.
“ Or crawling,” said the snake.
“ Or sleeping,” said the owl.
“ Or browsing,” said the goat.
“ You’ll excuse me, gentlemen,” remarked the pelican ;
“but this bill is a very good bill for what it was designed
for ; but nobody expects an ass, a bear, a monkey, a tor-
toise, a snake, an owl, nor a goat to see the value of it.” &
Now the animals that made these remarks are
second-rate politicians who had billets to keep.
“Locust ” : Visited N.S.W. Assembly the
other night. M'Court in chair. Sixteen members
all speaking at once, like a German ladies’ kaffee-
klatch. Occasional alarums and incursions by John
Norton, followed by a chorus of imprecations from
Labor benches. Awful language used nearly all
round. Premier Reid apparently asleep, except
for an occasional interjection of “It’s a lie, and
the hon. member knows it! ” Every one, accord-
ing to the version of everyone else, was either a
liar, a rogue, a murderer, a thief, a gaol-bird, or a
police-court solicitor. Chairman M‘Court sat as
if paralysed, and Sergeant-at-arms Harnett looked
like a man struck by lightning. However, the
conduct of the strangers’, the ladies’, and the
press galleries was exemplary.
Suppose Reid carries his fiscal Referendum
—the Upper House consenting—and N.S. Wales
gives a majority for Protection, will he simply
change the policy and go on governing ? Suppose
the province returns a Protectionist majority to
the House and votes for a Freetrade policy by
Referendum, which is possible according to pre-
sent electoral arrangements, and in the absence of
the second ballot, what will happen ? Will Reid re-
sign and let Lyne come in, to bring in a Freetrade
policy with his Parliamentary majority, or will
Reid remain and attempt to govern by a. minority ?
There are infinite possibilities in the Referendum
without the second ballot in the constituencies,
and with the constituencies all manner of different
sizes. The probability is that the Upper House
will decline to pass the fiscal Referendum, and
Reid will resuscitate his old cry and add it to his
programme.
The threatened Separa-
tion movement in Riverina
(N.S.W.), with a view to mak-
ing a new province which can
federate on its own account,
while the stump of the old
Botany Bay State stands out,
has aroused the Sydney pro-
vincial party to scoff a very
loud scoff indeed. Yet one
of the Sydney provincialisms’
worst grievances against its
late lamented friend, Feder-
ation Bill, was that there was
no provision for secession.
They were as solid as a granite
rock on the inalienable right of
every State to secede when-
ever it wanted to. And, if
one-fourth of a Commonwealth
has a right to secede, one-third
of a State surely has it also.

J. L. Parsons, S.A. Consul for Japan
(formerly member for N. Territory in S.A.
Assembly) is now in Japan arranging with Jap.
capitalists for the purchase of a large area of land
on the Victoria River for settlement. If terms
are arranged with S.A. Govt, it is proposed to im-
port a large, number of. cheap Japanese laborers.
Premier Kingston is violently opposed to the
scheme, but it is believed his Cabinet is divided
on the question.
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly should insist that
the Attorney-General of the new Ministry shall
have a seat in the Lower House. The Attorney-
General’s official actions intimately concern the
public, and in many debates a direct statement
from the law-officer of the Crown would save time
and temper. The shelving of him in the Council
deprives the working House of an essential part of
his services, and this is the less excusable as, with
the Col. Secretary, he is the highest paid officer in
the Cabinet.
The Federation poll shows up the rotten-
ness of Vic. electoral system, and exposes the
reason of the wholesale disfranchisement of
ratepayers. Under existing machinery, a rate-
payer, after settling in a district, cannot get his
name on to the electoral rolls for about 18 months.
If the new arrival builds, his property has to be
valued, then assessed, after winch the rate is de-
clared. This takes about six months. But after-
wards there is a long rigmarole t the making out
of new lists, submission of same to the registrar,,
printing of lists, and a lapse of months to permit
of the lodgment of objections. Then follows the
revision-court work, and finally the manufacture
of the certified roll, and, 18 months after settling,
the citizen, if he has luck, finds himself endowed
with a vote. A Russian Finn or the wild man
from Borneo would not have to wait much longer
to qualify. Premier Turner promises reform, one
of these days.
The tremendous shindy in N.S.W. As-
senibly last Thursday morning—it mostly had its
origin in the refreshment room—recalled the old
drunken, roaring times of a dozen years ago, when
N.S.W. politicians wiped up the sacred precincts
with each other, and debate was mostly incoherent
yell and blasphemy, and the member who insisted
on sitting in the Speaker’s chair with a waste-
paper basket on his head had to be removed by
main force amid much loud hiccup. In those
days the Cornstalk Legislature was so famous that
a Maoriland bard rose to inquire :
Is it lagdom in the blood
Of a convict-gotten brood
Ever breaking out like scrofula in language loud and
lewd ?
Or what is it that ails
These men of New South Wales
That they emulate the rhetoric of the bagnios and the
gaols ?
Things have improved a little since then, but a
good stock of the old gaol rhetoric is still on hand
and ready to break out at the shortest notice.

BICYCLE DONT’S FOR 1898.
DON’T buy a wheel that you never heard
of before.
DON’T buy a wheel assembled from parts
which can be bought for a few shil-
lings per set (all parts-wheels are
cheap wheels).
DON’T buy a wheel because a friend re-
commends it to you ; the friend gets a
commission.
DON’T buy a wheel from a firm which re-
commends a new make every few
months.
DON’T buy a wheel without a direct factory
guarantee ; you want some security
for vour money.
DON’T buy a cheaper wheel than the Black
Bird at £l6 10s., unless you believe in
cheap clothes and things.
DON’T buy a wheel which is consigned to
agents. It generally sells for £23 when
sold in this way ; £lO is its proper price.
DON’T forget that Red Bird Special, Red
Bird No. 1, and Black Bird Cycles are
all manufactured, sold, and guaran-
teed by—
