SPORTING NOTIONS.
There was a record attendance, as well as record
fields, at Rosehill on Saturday last, and, although out-
siders collared the first two races, warm favorites got
home in the last three, and sent backers home a “ bit
in.” The hitherto disappointing Lady Macbeth won the
Maiden Handicap, starting at Bto 1: while Bashful (0 to
1) accounted for the Nursery. The June Handicap went
to a country horse, Ailie, who was best favorite at 5 to
2; and North British won the Welter from an equal
favorite in Robin Hood.
• • • •
Sweep considerations had, no doubt, much to do
with the large field (39) sent on their journey for Rosehill
Cup. It was a good betting race, Oban starting favorite
at 5 to 1, and the ultimate winner, Kiteware, was amongst
the next division at 12 to 1. At the distance the favorite,
Earl Rosebery, Flower King, Alemene, Cin, and Eos were
in front, but Kiteware, getting a run in the centre, rushed
by his horses and won by three lengths from Oban, who
beat Eos by a head.
* • * *
- In Rosehill Cup owners who were not offered by
the drawers a slice to nothing of “Tatt’s ” sweep did not
hesitate to scratch their prads. For one reason or
another, several well-backed horses were withdrawn at
the last moment, including Vigorous, Robin Hood, Tele-
graph, Brazen Lad and Scrathroy. The owner of Kiteware
takes a substantial lump of the sweep from the sweep-
holder of that horse.
••* ' *
W. Forrester scored his first win since Melb.
Cup in the Nursery Handicap with the appropriately-
named Trident—Modesty filly, Bashful, who just managed
to scrape home from the unlucky Argo, with Foy’s Auld-
Lang-Syne (a promising looking youngster) third.
• * * *
Poor Daimio has found his bearings at last.
After well-meant, but unsuccessful attempts to gain lau-
rels on the English turf, he decided on returning to his
native heath, and accordingly, being somewhat “ stiff,”
travelled not per R.M.S. anything, but in a common cargo
wallah. Here his universal good qualities won the golden
opinions of all on board, from the skipper to the cook,
and a large assembly gathered open-mouthed to watch
the mastication of his morning bran mash. Ten days
from Melbourne, when stormy winds did blow, his tem-
porary stable gave way to repeated attacks on the part of
the briny, and the final collapse caused fatal injury to his
spine. A hasty consultation was called at 2.30 a.m. and
his destruction decided upon. The present scribe was
aroused from his balmy slumbers at 3 a.m. by repeated
revolver shots, and at 4, to the musical accompaniment of
the engine-room telegraph, his body was committed to
the deep, under the instructions of a deep-voiced salt,
newly turned out of his bunk, and thinking more of his
morning coffee than of Daimio’s big cross-country record.
The hoofs were eagerly sought after as souvenirs.
* • * •
“ A native-born Australasian wins the ‘ Darby ’
with a 100-to-l • rogue,’ and 120,000 people curse at Epsom
sufficient to make Jeddah shed his shoes,” writes Tub
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ With a 2-to-l
favorite and a 7-to-2 next-best, it was nevertheless a very
open race, though few expected 25’s for a place would be
Ist and 3rd. It is a pity that old Don Larnach, the M.L.
squatter, is not alive to hear that his eldest son’s chestnut
colt won the Epsom event at a price that breaks all re-
cords. Previously in favor, Jeddah was beaten in the
Guineas and disgraced in the Newmarket Stakes, and
relegated to the stable’s second-string, now to be dis-
carded for the blue riband. And the Prince and his
noble and ignoble pals, the johnnies, the bounders, the
costers, and the Heathers ‘ came down ’ one awful con-
glomerate * wop,’ and there was much corn in Israel.”
* * • *
Newhaven and Winkfield’s Pride frightened
everything out of Epsom Cup but three others, which
included Merman and the actual winner (writes Tub Bul-
letin’s London correspondent). No one questioned that
the battle would lie between these two, and so it did,
though another snatched the bone of contention. Bay
Ronald at 9st. 61b. with them, and on 81b. worse terms to
Newhaven than when the latter made his electrical d£but
in the City and Suburban, was relegated to tens ; wh.lst
the Irish horse, with his reckless gambling stable behind
him, was at 11 to 10 on, and our horse at 2’s. Merman,
with Bst. 101 b., was outclassed and last, but Cooper's
crowd piled the nuggets on Newhaven, and there must
have been several little Mt. Morgans on the result. The
duellists fought from the jump ; but at the mile, after a
tough fight, “-our” horse won and sailed for home. This
was Bay Ronald’s chance, and, tackling a hard-tried
horse, he won on a final turn of speed easily. Pride was not
quite up. Newhaven will be better, especially over more
than a mile and half, and the Bay was fit.
* » « *
The Dubbo, N.S.W., trotting-club has at last
made terms for the use of Kensington (Syd.) Ground, and
the country club’s fixture has been dated for July 22.
There are four trotting events on the card, together with
a couple of pony races; and Mr. Buckland will let
Sydneyites see Australia’s champion trotter, Fritz, do an
exhibition go. Trotting owners should make the most
of this opportunity, as, if a success, the meeting may lead
to better things later on.
• * * •
Getting pretty well known that when a certain
crack horseman puts his “ friends" on to back one of two
of his animals when two run in same race, it’s good
business to back the untipped one.
• * * *
A couple of months ago the Miller stable looked
like winning both the Nationals at Flemingyin July
meeting. To-day there doesn’t seem to be a tvump-card
in the pack.
* * * *
“V.S. Could have bought Truth (the
horse now running in Sydney) when a yearling for “ fifty
bob,” with his dam thrown in as well. Didn't want him
—worse luck. Saw him once win a “ drinks for the
crowd ” race, with 22 stone up ! We had been dancing
all nuht in the local church, and at daylight, Truth, who
had been tied to the fence all night, was backed to run a
cockie’s horse, “ from the river to the gate,” a little over
a quarter mile. Truth’s owner thought so little of the
other moke’s chance that he double-banked his neddy,
putting an list. “ cobber ” of his up behind ; notwith-
standing which Truth finished, hard held, 6 lengths to
the good.
• * * *
When Charmer, the well-known trotting mare,
was awarded a prize as a brood mare at Sydney show,
there was much discussion as to whether she should have
scored, in face of her having a hip knocked down. Opai,
winner of the Auckland (M.L.) Grand National Hurdle
Race, is out of an obscure-bred mare, named Polly, who
had a hip knocked down. In addition to Opai, Poily was
the dam of Strephon and Uranus, the latter winner of
Auckland Steeplechase, ’B7, from which it would appear
the knocked-down hip was no detriment to her as a brood
mare.
*•* • •
A well-known N.S.W. trainer was once running
a trial on a suburban course. It was a moonlight morn-
ing go, and every care had been taken to dodge the
touts, but after the horses had been taken to the start-
ing-point a figure was discerned crouching close to a
handy fence. The trainer’s man, after conferring with
his principal, walked over with a heavy quarter-sheet
over his arm, and found that the watcher was a well-
known tout, who affably cracked the usual joke about
the “ early bird.” Then he put a watch into work as the
horses started, and smiled away until the cattle were a
furlong from home, when Dick, who had shaken out the
quarter-sheet, threw it right over the man’s head and
upset him, and when he recovered his feet and sight the
horses were pulling up, and winner and time were out
of his ken.
• * • •
If the handicapper is nob too ungenerous,
Levanter, Maoriland’s rep. in Vio. G.N. Steeple this
winter, will be a certain starter. He competes at the
carnival, anyway. Captivator’s son is to be given ample
time to get trimmed up alter his trip across. Under
12st. 101 b. he ran fourth lately in Auckland G.N. Steeple,
conceding stones to some of his opponents. He had won
this race twice previously, the first time by a nose after a
3i-mile journey.
• * # •
When threatened with bankruptcy, a while
back, a cute M.L. ne’er-do-weel entered a palpable “ no-
chancer " for a pony race, and got confiding friends to
back it for him. There were three others in the race, and
he finished fourth. Then he objected to the other three
because they were over standard height. Measurement
proved this correct, and he scooped the pool.
* * * *
Wallaroo and Rand wick played an “off ”
match on Sydney Cricket-ground on Saturday. Rand-
wicks were without several of their regular team, and
played half-heartedly, with the result that Wallaroo ran
over them by 29 points to 3.
• * • *
As was expected, the only Badge match played
in Sydney, on Saturday last, on the Agricultural Ground,
between University and Pirates, proved a tough battle.
During first-half Pirates had the ill-luck to lose the ser-
vices of Kllis, who had a rib broken, but before the acci-
dent University had scored a try. The second half was
considered by many the best bit of football seen this
season, and when time was called University had scored
a win by 8 points to 5.
* * * *
For in-and-out running the average racing pony
isn’t in it with a Sydney senior football team. This
season Sydney has beaten Wallaroo and played a draw
with ’Varsity; Randwick played draws with Wallaroo
and’Varsity; beat Sydney and were beaten hyPi rates,
who have been beaten by both Wallaroo and University,
yet have beaten Sydney. Puzzle : Find the leading team
in the competition.
• • • •
In M.L. this season football is increasing in
popularity, but ferocious play- characterises nearly all
contests. At Dunedin,-six first-classers have been im-
peached for roughness and fighting; matters are worse
at Wellington ; and one alleged match on the West Coast
savored of the prize-ring, with a cursing contest thrown
in. Unions are now passing out the bashers.
• • • •
Recent football-match, Cairns versus Cooktown.
Cooktown team, on arriving home, found that they had
forgotten the ball. Wired to Cairns about matter.
Answer came to the effect that no one knew anything
about it. Cooktown wired again : “ Our ball was seen
being played with after we left Cairns.” Cairns : “Have
not seen your ball. Did not know you ever owned a
ball.” Cooktown wrathful : “If choose to steal our ball
—do so.” And so the little duel went on until Cairns got
called \ den of thieves, and a Cooktown footballer, who
had waxed eloquent per wire, was expelled from his club
tc appease the indignation of the alleged felonious Chow-
towners.
* * * •
On Saturday night last, in Sydney, Jack O’Brien
and young Sandy Ross fought eight rounds with
gloves. Sandy was untrained, and it is this sort of
11 go” that should be discountenanced by the police and
followers of the game. The effects of a “ knock-out”on
a trained athlete are not lasting, but to a man in Ross’
condition, a knock-out has a good show of proving fatal.
In the interest of the game, and themselves, proprietors
of boxing resorts should not permit untrained men to
box “ for keeps.”
• * * *
Jack M'Gowan, who recently beat Tweedie in
Brisbane, is out with a challenge to meet any fist. 41b.
man in Australia. The Queenslanders should give
Tweedie another showing with M'Gowan, when a big
house and a rattling set-to might be relied upon.
Tweedie had all the best of the final go, whilst it las'td,
until M'Gowan got in an unlooked-for “ knock out.”
The Sydneyite must have a weak spot in his defence
somewhere, as he was knocked out in much the same
way in America.

Adelaide laorosse matches are provoking much
exoitement this season. North Adelaide, erstwhile pre-
miers, have suffered three defeats, leaving University at
present at the top. Similarly, Melb.’s last year’s pre-
miers, University, have been beaten three times, whilst
Melb. is undefeated. S.A. v. Vic. eventuates in Melb.
Aug. 6, and the former province will not this time repeat
its run-away victory of last year.

Porta and other well-known racing cyclists,
finding the “ game ” at a low ebb, have been putting in
time on the Kanowna (Westralian) gold-leads.
* V • *
When the irrepressible Randolph Bedford was
in Sydney recently he ordered a bicycle to be sent to his
hotel for a few days’ use, and next morning was horrified
to find a machine with sassiety handles, saddle far in the
rear, and mudguards and a gear-case and a brake lum-
bering up the jigger. “ What’s this for,” demanded the
irate Randolph at the bike-agents’ a few minutes later —
“ a blooming elephant to ride, or what ?" The assistant
looked reproachful. “ All the aristocracy ride with their
machines that way,” he explained. “ Then,” said the
ex-Reckless, “ shift that saddle forward, lower those
handles, and strip off that luggage ; I’m a blanky
Socialist 1 ”
* * * *
The cabled news that Gov. Brassey has bought
another yacht may be taken as confirmation of the fact,
published in Tub Bulletin some months ago, that Uncle
Tom is desirous of selling the Sunbeam. It would not be
surprising to hear that he has found a purchaser for the
famous craft ere he returns to Melbourne.

Hayward, the most reliable and graceful bats-
man in Stoddard’s late team, made 64 and 85 for Surrey
against Essex, May 17. In same match Richardson’s

The Vic. Govt, has entered into a large con-
tract with the Massey Harris Cycle Co. to supply bicycles
for the pillar-clearers, letter-carriers, and telegraph-
messengers in Melbourne and suburbs, and, if this experi-
ment proves a success, the bicycle brigade will be en-
grafted on every branch of the public service.*
• * * *
Brisbane newspapers gave much notice to the
Austral Cycle Agency pacing-teams. One prominent
paper had a column interview with Williams, the team’s
manager.*

The Dutch Govt, has issued an order that all
the cycles for its military service shall be fitted with
Dunlop Tyres.*

Mdlle. Serpolette, the French cycliste (who has
since been much damaged by colliding with a bike) made
her first appearance in Sydney last Saturday, at the Cricket-
ground, when she pedalled round on a motor-cycle. The
crowd was not enthusiastic, and plainly showed it
was “ not taking any." Contests, man against man, or
woman against woman, are the only cycle shows that can
rouse the blood of a Sydney assemblage.
The action Cowlishaw v. The Bulletin,
wherein <£2ooo is claimed for alleged libel on plain-
tiff in connection with the loss at sea of the collier
Atacama and 13 of her crew, will not be heard until
22nd August, no N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge
being available during vacation.

In view of the vagueness of the London
salesmen’s stereotyped reply, “ Not suited to the
trade,” the Pastoral Finance Association has had
a number of mutton carcases reimported, as speci-
mens of what is most likely to hit the taste of the
British consumer, which carcases will, during the
Sheep Show week, be on view for the benefit of
exporters at the association’s freezing-depdt, North
Sydney.
Dear Bulletin. —Be carrying of live stock
on passenger-steamers. A Melb. steamer, by
which I travelled lately, leaving Adelaide for
Fremantle, had on deck 120 sheep, 17 horses, 4
bullocks, and a quantity of dog. The passengers,
2G9 in number, had, during the heavy weather in
the Bight, simply to wade in liquid manure.
Think it’s about time the s.s. co.’s stopped this
game.—T.S., Perth, W.A.
Be recent mysterious disappearance of
half-a-dozen locomotives from the Vic. railway
stock, the theft of engines is comparatively com-
mon in America. The private ownership of rail-
ways there has given rise to bitter feuds between
some of the concerns, in connection with which
sheds have been burgled, engines fired up and
driven along the intersecting lines to the stealers’
sheds, perhaps 1000 miles away. Next day the
stolen article would be taken to pieces, and so
transfigured that only the original builder would
know it again. Happily, the different gauges of
the Australian provinces preclude Victoria from
suspecting her neighbors—about the only good
thing that ever has resulted from the disparity of
gauge
Vic. Acting-Gov. Madden was promptly on
hand with his usual letter of sympathy to the
friends of the sufferers in the recent powder
magazine explosion. His Ex. runs Her Gracious
close as champion perfunctory condoler. Which
again recalls the miserable foolishness of plastering
the cable-column with such announcements as that,
for instance, the Duchess of York and the rest of
’em forwarded condolatory messages over the
recent wholesale launch-drowning. Of course they
did ! —it’s part of their business ! —but it’s stand-
ing the thing on its head to expend a lot more
cable on such a mere mechanical matter-of-course
than on the details of the catastrophe itself. But
flunkeydom is satisfied with its “ one poor half-
pennyworth ” of humanity “to all this intolerable
quantity ” of royalty.
“ C.P.” : George Loyeau, who died in Q.
the other day, was a well-known figure about
Adelaide in the middle eighties. Also, one of the
few Bohemians in the local Bohemian club—which
was quickly crammed by suburban saints, and
perished from sheer holiness. Loyeau had no
literary capacity—was of the trebly-cursed brand
with the scribbling itch and nothing to say ; but
his experience as digger, bushman, etc., would
have been a small silver-mine to a Favenc or a
Beoke. He edited Gawler Bunyip on a pound a
week; then took to editing “Men of Mark,”
“ Representative Men,” and so on. After collect-
ing as many guineas as possible, he would issue a
dingy volume scarred with unsightly “ cuts.”
His latter days were spent in dribbling awful
doggerel to admiring Maryborough donkeys. A
kindly, inoffensive old chap—said to be related to
a peer -probably was, as he seemed ashamed of
peers.
The one great sensation of the sham
motion of censure in N.S.W. Assembly was the
charge of attempted murder heaved by Levien,
M.L.A., against the two far-West Labor-mem-
bers, Sleath and Fergusson. Levien, a reckless,
hot-headed gentleman of the kind who habitually
wears his hat over one ear, undertook to prove
his assertion. But what a lot more interesting it
would be if Levien would only accuse Sleath of
suicide and prove that ! A clearly-proven charge
of suicide against Sleath would remove a
very dull and painful bore out of N.S.W.
politics. A Parliamentary Commission has
been appointed to look into the matter, and at
time of writing (Tuesday) it was getting on with
gradual rapidity. First sitting, Levien didn’t
turn up. Second sitting was mainly spent in a
muddled effort to decide exactly what Levien had
accused Sleath and Ferguson of doing, and what
he had to prove against them. The wild shriek
that sounded so alarming when fired off verbally
on the floor of the House by a hot red man, with
agitated coat-tails, looked very vague in the cold
print of Hansard, because Hansard doesn’t yell
and jump. And the average Parliamentary accu-
sation is mostly yells and jumps. This is probably
the reason why the Levien-Sleath inquiry is prov-
ing a very slow affair.

A pathetic little story is related in connec-
tion with the recent Melb. shell-fatality. The
slain man, Cowley, was a widower with six child-
ren, and the youngest, a seven-year-old boy, was
heard to say, between his sobs, that “Mother
must have wanted father more than us! ”
“ Back bl ox Junior”: Barton runs a big
risk in standing for this place (Bourke), which is a
hopelessly roads-and-bridges constituency—and
what does Barton know of Warragul culvert? He
could easily find a city or suburban seat. Anti-
Billite Millen, the sitting member for Bourke,
will not easily be displaced, although the elec-
torate did vote for Federation ; for he is per-
sonally much liked and respected, and already the
voters are carrying him round on their shoulders,
They won’t try to carry Barton.
“D.E.N.” : Near eastern end of Little
Flinders-street (Melb.) at lunch-hour saw a lorry,
laden with packing-cases, drawn up at kerb.
Solitary sweating horse with nosebag on. Driver
munching sandwich between front cases. Nose-
bag slips off, and driver, flinging sandwich into
roadway, jumps down with “ Half a mo., cocky,”
and fixes nosebag on again. Then he feels for
another sandwich, and, finding that he’s flung
away the last, stands watching his horse feeding,
with evident pleasure. Ought to be president of
a Society for the Encouragement of Kindness to
Dumb Animals.
The “ Long Arm” again, a. cute solicitor
got a “ lady ” to write to one of his clerks,
making an appointment at a doubtful house,
during ordinary business hours, so as to get
a chance of sacking him. The clerk did not
fall in, but anonymously exposed the trap in a
paper to which he was a contributor. The lawyer,
who had tried the same trick before with others,
was wild to think his game publicly exposed, and,
knowing the clerk to be a writer to the paper in
question, offered him .£1 Is. to find out who wrote
the par. Clerk took the £1 Is., and promptly
produced the pay-slip sent to himself by the paper
for the very paragraph in question.
At Bid leaden village, near Ashford, Kent,
an annua: distribution of quartern loaves and
cheese, likewise embossed “ penny-rolls,” has been
proceeding for the past 760 years. The emboss-
ment on the rolls is a crude representation of the
“Biddenden Maids,” who bequeathed certain
land (worth .£lO a year in modern rental) to the
parish church, with the stipulation that every
Easter Day bread and cheese should be served out
to all-comers by the parson and churchwardens.
The maids, born A.D. 1100, were of the Siamese-
twins order of architecture, joined together by
the hips and shoulders. They lived a pious life
for 35 years, when one of them expired. The
other sister, wisely refusing to be hacked from her
faithful partner by a mediaeval surgeon, died six
hours later, and they were duly buried in the
parish churchyard. Biddenden is such a very
quiet spot that the story of the joint maids was
never noised abroad till seven centuries had rolled
over their memory, but of late years it has got
into the metropolitan newspapers, and nowadays
the Easter ceremony is rushed by gaudy biking
tourists from London who fight for the souvenir
rolls, and cluster in knickers and “rationals”
around the tomb of their virginal benefactresses.
Dear Bulletin. — Re your comment on
the heterogeneous nature of U.S. population, I
should like to point out that the governing powers
of the States are now, and always have been,
essentially of British origin. Take the Presi-
dents, for instance. From Washington to M‘Kin-
ley they have been, with but two exceptions (Van
Buren, Dutch, and Buchanan, Irish) of British
descent. Same with the Cabinets of the various
Presidents. In M‘Kinley’s Cabinet the Attorney-
General, M'Kenna, has a name suspiciously Irish,
but all the rest are of either English or Scotch
descent. The members of both Houses of Con-
gress are almost exclusively of British origin.
During the War of Secession, again, all the prin-
cipal commanders on both sides (with the ex-
ception of Sheridan for the North, and Beau-
regard for the South) were of British extraction.
Same with all the leading statesmen the U.S. has
produced ; so that I fail to see why the idea of an
Anglo-American alliance should provoke a smile
from The Bulletin. The majority of the U.S.
people are of our blood and kindred, and an
alliance with them would be conducive to the
world’s peace and prosperity.— Trotter Bethune.
[This is mostly based on the assumption that every
man’s national'ty can be traced by his name—
which is absurd. The English language prevails
in America, and names get anglicised for one
thing. Also, plenty of names are common to a
lot of countries. —Ed. B.]


A quarter-oentury’s service of the Mutual
Life Association was considered by the staff
thereof to entitle its chief executive officer, Mr.
J. 'C. Remington, to a mark of special recognition
in the form of a luncheon at the Association s
offices on Thursday week last. All the other
principal members of the staff were present, and
the proceedings were genial even to enthusiasm;
the esteem in which Mr. Remington is held being
further shown by the heavy fire of congratula-
tory letters and telegrams to which he was sub-
jected. He will, however, it is expected, survive
for the admiring contemplation of his entire staff,
pietorially assembled in the handsome album with
which they presented him.
“ Ricardo ” : Gladstone leaves an estate
of about iJ1.0,000 a year, through his careful
management. It is entailed on his eldest grand-
son. Here is Gladstone’s Toryism, as pronounced
as that of Scott in trying to found his family at
Abbotsford. Viceroy Dalhousie said he reckoned
his brilliant Governorship of India of less honor
than belonging to the family of Ramsay. 1 itt, as
Premier of England, was not considered a good
enough mark as husband of Lady Lleanor Lden,
who refused him, on the instruction of her father,
Lord Auckland. All this shows the hollowness,
the windbaggery of political achievement, the
neglect of the Substance for the Shadow. Your
Dibbses and Berrys can lecture on it.
By the death of 0. H. Croaker, Wagga loses
a white man, and its poor an unobtrusive friend.
“Charlie” bossed Dibbs’s committee when that
long politician pervaded the Murrumbidgee. In
>B9 when Dibbs took office and went to the coun-
try, only to be defeated by Parkes, he returned
to Wagga on a Saturday afternoon, dead beat
from a week’s travelling and spouting. The elec-
tion was on the following Monday, and, as he was
sitting at lunch in that sweet calm engendered
by thinking the campaign over, Croaker came m,
and, after the usual inquiries, said, “ Look here,
Dibbs, you’ve to deliver an address here to-night.
“ Damifido. Why, man, my throat’s like a brick-
kiln ; I can hardly whisper.” “ Can’t help it, old
boy, it’s announced ; here it is—” putting before
him a copy of the local Advertiser. L>ibbs
wearily reached his off his forehead, and
read “Mr. Dibbs will address the electors of
the 'Murrumbidgee from the balcony of the
Criterion Hotel to-night, at 8 o clock. At the
conclusion of his address, the Premier will sing
that well-known song ‘Just Before the Battle,
Mother.’ ” [The Bulletin had recently published
a picture of Uncle Abigail doing that same.] Dibbs
tore the paper to shreds, and fairly lost himself m
a maze of heartfelt curses, beside which his after-
efforts at Chicago were not a circumstance and
when he dropped back into his chair, still ( b' a ®"
phemous but exhausted, Croaker said coolly. It s
all right, old man. I went down to the IISER
office with Coleman, and, when they finished
printing, got them to lift out a paragraph, insert
this, and strike off a couple of copies. So, don t
worry —you won’t have to tell any more lies to-
night.

Veteran White, of Bathurst Free Press,
now rapidly nearing his end, arrived at Adelaide
exactly GO years ago, and built with his own hand
the first Wesleyan church there. Mr. White was
at one time a great believer in Henry Parkes—
raking in by his personal exertions over <£looo for
the “ Empire fund,” but was afterwards so treated
by Parkes that, when a subsequent fund was
started. White “ wasn’t taking any.”
Randell, the present alleged Minister for
Education in the great and glorious West, is the
same bright spirit who recently lamented in pub-
lic the “immorality” of English literature,
winding up with the astounding hope that ‘ one
day Shakespeare would be re-written.” A Ran-
dellised edition of the works of William the
Wonder re-written by, say, the President of the
W.C.T.U., is now within the range of preposter-
ous possibilities.
Percy Spence, the well-known Bulletin
artist, has, on arrival in London, found plenty of
work at his hand. Already he is doing black-and-
white for Pearson’s, Blackie & Sons, the Gentle-
woman, and the Naval and Military Gazette.
His painting, “The Buster,” has been sold; also
a number of his small pictures. The Fine Art
Society of Bond-street is going to engrave his
cow picture, “Sundown,” one of his best and
most distinctly Australian efforts.

Toowoomba possessed till lately a School
of Arts encumbered with an old debt and some
very old literature. T’other day, a providential
fire swept the old shop out of existence, and as it
was pretty well insured nobody wept. Of tie
2500 vols. in the library only one was found un-
damaired —and that was a Bible. Fifty years ago,
parsondom would have preached on such an oc-
currence till all was blue—but now never a sky-
pilot thinks the circumstance worthy of notice.

sends us his portrait which shows a smooth
and healthy condition of the skin. But he
has had the following experience :
9
“ For the past three years I have been suf-
fering from an eruption of the body. I had
medical attendance and had medicine to take
and a lotion for outward application, but they
did not do me the slightest good. In fact I
tried all sorts of medicine and nothing seemed
to relieve me. My life was a burden. I had
no appetite and I could not sleep. I was
strongly advised to try .

A young man, tried for assaulting the
“ conshtable ” in the execution of his duty in
Melbourne the other clay, pleaded that he was
subject to “fighting fits,” and had been seized
with one when the policeman arrrived. Possibly
the defence was a true bill. Writer once knew
an amiable little Irishman who was subject to
fighting fits, in which he would attack anything,
from a man to a brick house. The fact that he
always got woefully licked was proof of the
genuineness of his fits.
The drolleries of municipal government.
At a recent meeting of Preston (Melb.) Shire
Council it was moved- “ That this council having
lost confidence in its representative on the Met.
! Board of Works, Cr. Howden be requested to
'send his resignation to the chairman.” But the
’) motion was lost by 5 votes to 4,. “ Cr. Howden
j himself voting with the majority,” His own con-
i fidence in Howden had not been shaken by
• adverse comment.

i She was young and rather pretty, and her
pet amusement was replying to matrimonial advts.
There were four girls in the family, and the pa
thereof was a very sanctimonious and respectable
old gentleman. The other three sisters, being
decidedly plain, didn’t take kindly to their dear
Evelyn’s adventures with strange men in search of
a wife; but the climax came one morning when
she burst into their room and showed them an
advt. : —“Gentleman, middle-aged, with means,
desires to make the acquaintance of a young lady
who will not be averse to living in a country town.
One who can play and sing preferred. Reply to
Confidential, G.P.0.” “There, girls,” exclaimed
Evelyn, “what do you think of that ? It’s in-
serted specially for me and I’m going to reply to it
right away ; but, of course, as you all seem so
scared, I’ll sign another name, and if anything
comes of it, I can tell him the reason after-
wards. ’ The three scraggy virgins remonstrated
in vain. Evelyn, wrote her letter, and received
a very nice typed note in reply, saying he
would be delighted to see her, and if she i
could meet him at the Manly ferry wharf at a I
certain time on Thursday night, they might be :
able to come to some satisfactory arrangement. ;
“ Wear a white rose in your dress,” he added in a j
postscript, “and I will wear a geranium to enable '
us to recognise each other.” The poor girl was in a .
flutter of excitement as she stepped out of the ’bus •
at Circular Quay at the appointed time, and when i
she saw a man with a red flower the size of a
dinner-plate in his button-hole coming towards
her, cast down her eyes, and only raised them
when he was bowing and scraping before her. It
was her own dear papa. Then she remembered
that the saintly old gent did own a house in the
country.

How to excel at Billiards.
Only a few days ago a wonderful game of
billiards was witnessed in one of the principal
billiard saloons in this city, which fairly astonished
everyone present, inasmuch as one of the players
was known to suffer from a long-standing nervous
affection, so much so that he could scarcely hold
the cue in his hand ; but notwithstanding this he
offered his opponent (who was a professional) 50
in 100, and beat him, by 20. And he gave Herr
Rassmussen’s Alfaline Vitality Pills the
credit for his wonderful improvement.

AREA.:
125 Acres, held under Application Certificate, 783 Mineral Lands Act, Queensland.
Mount Mollov, 22 miles from Port Douglas, Queensland, which is an important shipping port. The mine is about
half-a-mile from the Port Douglas and Mareeba coach road, and 13 miles from Biboohra railway station.
Cost of carriage to Port Douglas by road, £1 per ton. ~ ...,
An abundance of water is obtainable all the year round, with a very small expense for dams, and timber suitable
for mining purposes abounds in the immediate vicinity.
Operations at the Mine.
Work has been carried on at the mine in a desultory way for about seven years by small parties of men.

Fraulein Fusch, though still a mere “ flappet (latest
naval slang for maidens of tender years with flowing
locks), plays with expression and finish, and displays a
wonderful amount of aplomb for one so young—far more
than Jakoff Hambourg did, for instance. The violin is
truly compared to the human voice, inasmuch as it can
afford the most exquisite pleasure or the most hideous
torture. Fraulein Fusch handles the instrument with
the loving touch that seems to come naturally to all
genuine violinists.
“Haresfoot”: Rickards says that Ugo Biondi, his
Proteus, to open at Mell). Opera House in about a fort-
night, beats a whole company. Single-handed enter-
tainers seemed all at once to have gone out when
Fregoli and Biondi sprung up, carrying the business
to unheard-of lengths. Sam Foote, of the last cen-
tury, would have been struck dumb, with all his clever-
ness ; but yet, I fancy, nothing comes up to the elder
Mathews, in his own peculiar field of changing his per-
sonality without any aids whatever. Once, when acting
at a London theatre, he personated an interloper so
thoroughly that he was turned out from behind the
scenes. He and his comrade Yates, father of Edmund,
used to personate each other before an audience at once
with an excruciating result.
“ Melb.” : While Titheradge was acting Wilfred Denver
last night, in the first act there was a curious mirror of
the Denver of real life in the crowded upper circle.
Because his wife would not change her seat, he tried to
drag her out, greatly to the scandal of the people, while
their little boy cried bitterly.
“Snell”: Comparisons are odious, but inevitable. _ Yet
there should be no offence, because really there is no
comparison, speaking 1 of Titheradge’s and Barrett’s Silver
King. Titheradge is a true artist; Barrett, to my nnnd,
more a mechanic —a good, earnest, hard-working mechanic,
respecting himself and his materials, but only a trades-
man. His tricks are sometimes excellent, almost de-
ceiving the judgment, but he wrestles openly and
obviously with a part into which Titheradge glides
instantly. Barrett works the part, the part works
Titheradge. There is scarcely a line or a thought
in it that is not more lustrous for the latter
medium. Barrett is false from the jump, most false at
the jump ; his Wilfred Denver in the first scene is a
smug gent of the master-barber type, not above a sus-
picion of Wesleyan saintliness, and mysteriously betrayed
into drink. By the way, is there an actor who portrays a
drunk as wretchedly as Barrett does ? I have seen him
make two attempts, “ Ben-my-Chree” and Wilfred Den-
ver, and in each the effect is weakly amateurish, sugges-
tive of a highly moral jamboree due to an over-indulgence
in picnic tea. Compare Titheradge and Barrett in the
description of Denver’s dream. Barrett is declamatory,
convulsive ; Titheradge is close, metallic, intense, with a
feverish eye, and a terrible belief in the long, skinny arm
and taloned hand reaching out of heaven. Barrett gener-
ally accents the weak spot of the pla3' in the scene in
Coombe’s den, Titheradge almost reverses it. But, pick
’em where you please, the ineffectual fires of the newer
importation pale in the radiance of the local light—the
eclipse of a star.
“S.S.”: Melb. amateurs, who lost themselves in “La
Perichole ” at the Bijou last week, addressed a line to the
public : “Do not buy tickets just for the sake of charity,
but to see good opera.” Having discarded the cloak of
charity, their sins were naked and—ashamed.
At a recent Australian metropolitan first-night per-
formance the representative of a great daily sat in
a conspicuous position throughout the acts and inter-
vals, with his chin planted in his bosom, sleeping the
weary hours away, and a drone as of innumerable bees
percolated through the house, swelling to a clarion blast
at pathetic moments. It is only fair to say that the press-
man never exhibited higher critical judgment.
How these Christian newspapers love one another !
Last Friday night’s profits of “ Perichole,” at Melb.
Bijou, were to be handed, said the managerial advt., to
“ Rita,” of the local ’Erald, on behalf of certain destitute
musicians for whom she had pleaded in the evening jour-
nal. Friday morning’s Age was willing to aid the cause
of the destitute ones, without mentioning the ’Erald.
Thus The proceeds of to-night’s performance will be
distributed as announced in our amusements column.”
Which plainly meant: “On page 8, under the heading
* Bijou Theatre,’ will be found a reference to one of our
beastly contemporaries.”
N. : ‘“LaPerichole,’by the Melb. amateurs, has pre-
sented the objectionable feature of a handsome and in-
nocent young lady shamming intoxication, as La Peri-
chole, at her bridal, singing ‘ I’m only a little tight,.’ A
thing like this would have been hissed off in the most
degraded time of the drama, in the hands of such as
Etheredge and Wycherley. It pained me, on the premifere
of this revival of ‘ Perichole,’ to see a monster wreath
handed up to the heroine, in the midst of this scene, as a
testimony of the delight of a section of the audience.”
“The kindest, warmest-hearted people in the world 1 ”
That’s what Mrs. Brown-Potter has been calling us to a
Welsh interviewer. Christie Murray, she says, simply
didn’t know what he was talking about when he slated
Australia. But the matter with Murray was that Aus-
tralia never even tried to swallow’ him, whereas it made
heroic efforts to assimilate the beauteous Cora, who, by
the way—fact not generally known—is partly of Spanish
blood.
Liverpool Courier shovels on the jam “ Many people
are talking of this beautiful woman at the Prince of
Wales Theatre. No portraits that I have seen of her do
Mrs. Brown-Potter justice. She is quite a unique type,
not English certainly, nor yet American, nor indeed
modern. Rather she seems to have come by a straight
road from the earliest Eastern races. VVhen you see her
you get to thinking of Cleopatra, of Yishka, of all the
lovely heroines of the ancients. Her hair, which she
wears quite short, and parted at one side in a style pecu-
liar to herself, is the true red-gold, that rarest color, and
shines like burnished copper. She makes pictures with
every movement. I have seen nothing so superb as her
first entrance in ‘ Charlotte Corda.v,’ in a grev-blue gown
and an immense black hat with black plumes, and with
great clusters of crimson roses in her hair and in her
hands. Then as the model for the picture of Judith,
with clinging draperies of purple and scarlet and white,
she is not less a marvel and a surprise. She is more beau-
tiful off the stage, as you find when you go round to the
green-room to welcome her to Liverpool, and the simplest
and most natural of women.” Not the simplest—but
very natural.
Wilson Barrett has at last met with his deserts. The
Mayor and councillors of Perth (W.A.) entertained him at
the Tow’n Hall, and Premier Forrest proposed his health,
referring to his visit as another proof that Westralia is
“fast becoming one of the greatest provinces of the
group.” That ought to make Barrett happy.
Sims Reeves, in his 76th year, is to get a national sub-
scription benefit, which, considering that W. G. Grace
was rewarded in the same way for his artistic services to
cricket, is not an unfair thing. The money that Sims
Reeves ought to have held was probablv put into cronk
companies at the instigation of cronk friends. Anyhow,
he is a needy man now, and his old-time greatness as an
interpreter of ballads that still live on the strength of his
interpretation should preserve him from poverty. A
good many trivial English songs remain popular and
“ give pleasure to all,” only because the Sims Reeves way
of singing them has survived.
Charles Holloway's venture at Melb. Royal was lucky
from start to finish. “ When London Sleeps,” a really
outrageous melodramatic joke, showed a profit of nearly
£2OOO, and the season finished up with a week of packed
houses for “ Silver King.” Money turned away every
night.
What a hold poor little Sadie Macdonald had on the
affections of her confreres ! At Sydney Tivoli, the other
night, Coleman explained to a sympathising audience
that he could not dance for them, as, on returning from
a.visit, on the previous Sunday, to his dear little friend’s
grave, he had strained his ankle. The house nearly
wept. Pathos was rampant in the theatre that night. It
was the last occasion but one of Mr. Michael Nolan’s
appearance, and Michael took the opportunity of speechi-
fying. When one had cut through the brogue and got
down to the words, one could hear him saying that
“ Never in my life have I and my wife been so happy as
here. Everyone has been good to us; everyone has been
kind ; and if we should never meet again this side of the
grave, good-bye, and God bless you all.”
Manager Charles Holloway’s little daughter Beatrice,
who played Cissy Denver in the recent “Silver King’*
season at Melb. Royal, was born during the original pro-
duction 13 years ago, when dad played Jaikes after Phil
Day. Accordingly christened Beatrice Denver, and will
probably be playing Denver’s wife in another five years,
judging by her progress in her art.
“We speak of going to England as ‘ going home,’ but
to me home will always be in Australia, where I have
spent the greater portion of my life, and where I have
made my reputation as an actor.”—Titheradge’s farewell
on Saturday night at Melb. Royal. Tith sailed on Tues-
day, but his family, including the sprightly Lily, remains
behind.

“ Quick study ! The most astonishing I ever heard of
was Madame Viardot, with my Azucena. On the morn-
ing of the first performance of ‘ II Trovatore,' in Paris,
I was distracted to find that Mine. Alboni’s throat pre-
cluded her appearing. Viardot had never seen the part,
but made it the hit of the opera that night.”—Verdi.
“ Who were the first to encourage me ? Why, the
working men. At the rehearsal of my first opera,
1 Nabuco,’ in La Scala Theatre, Milan, I went humbly
and was treated as a poor young composer. Workmen
were hammering all over the house in repairs. The
orchestra, principals and chorus skulked and slummed.
I was in despair. But, at the Pensiero chorus, the ham-
mering stopped in a moment. The workmen cried, * Viva
for the Maestro ! ’ and clapped their hands. Then began
my success." —Verdi.

Re M.L. flv-pest. A circus proprietor in the West keeps
no elephant outside his posters. When he wants to fill
the bill he ties a three-foot rope to the nose of one of his
horses, washes the whole turn-out with honey, and lets
the flies do the rest. He soon has a huge black elephant,
trunk and all, made up of millions of flies and a horse
and a yard of rope.
“ Al.” writes of a Maoriland incident:—The hate that
mummer bears to mummer passeth understanding. Last
Dec., the Gidyea comedy co. put up at our hash-tower at
Whaukoro. My room separated the leading lady’s from
her jealous satellite’s ; cause of jealousy—a very tall man
with a sounding “h’m,” a captain of soldiers, clanks here,
clanks there, winks once and the girls are dead. A man,
sir, for a billet-doux or a sharp bit of work. At break-
fast the satellite sat between him and the leading lady,
intercepted glances and foot-communication, kept the
toast from her enemy, hoping to starve her out : utter
futility—the 1.1. has devilish long arms. Bicker-bicker
when Captain Moltke looks either way, both watching
each other like Dewey and Blanco. One night I was
lying heels up, head down, fighting insomnia. Clock
struck three. Something tripped past my door ; then a
sharp breath and a click ; peeped, and saw the 1.1.’s door
swing back ; three seconds later the satellite slipped out
and passed into her own room. Back to bed, wondering
what business could take her into her enemy’s quarters.
Then I began to breathe gas in thick streams; got up,
and chased the smell to 1.1.’s door ; opened it (rash act!)
and got nearly suffocated on the threshold. Shook 1.1.
until she sat up and requested me to quit at once. I ex-
plained that if she lay in bed a minute longer she’d be-
come a gaseous body. A loud singing in the near jet
located the trouble. I casually mentioned the satellite’s
visit; the 1.1. thanked me coldly, and begged that I would
not mention the circumstance. A strange hilarity per-
vaded the breakfast-table ; the 1.1. passed toast to her
rival with alarming politeness. When Moltke left the
room the 1.1. collared the satellite by the scruff and
rubbed her pearly cheek in the fireplace. I locked the
door sympathetically, and hinted through the key-hole
that cold ashes and hot bacon-fat would meet the case.
All is well. We are living in the next terrace, where the
pale heroine meddleth not with gas-taps.
In an amateur performance of the comedietta “Withered
Leaves,” an engaged couple once appeared. During the
piece, the lady was to be asked by her lover whether she
had ruined her hat in a romantic adventure on a moun-
tain, to which she should have replied, “ I hope you didn’t
spoil your trousers.” The unfortunate lover forgot his
own line, however, and spoke the lady’s, with the result
that the engagement was broken off in the interval.

Pointed out by a Correspondent of the
Australasian Medical Gazette that there isn’t
a word about “conscientious objections ” or “re-
ligious scruples ” in the section (343) of the Crimi-
nal Law Amendment Act which allows any person
who “ objects to take an oath ” to make a declara-
tion instead. Surprising how few people are aware
that no Judge has power to make them slobber the
filthy Court Bible.

Consulting Mechanical Engineer, the firm is now
in a position to furnish inventors with expert
engineering assistance free of charge.*
Few realise how much the food supplies of
Great Britain depend upon other countries. If
Britain went to war with France, or in any way
was cut off from that country, it would be de-
prived of comestibles annually imported such as
butter, 200,000 cwt. ; margarine, 41,963 cwt.; eggs
to the value of .£893,133 or 35,725,320d0z., and
i poultry, 71,747 cwt. The increasing demand for
margarine is causing trouble. To meet it, France
I has had to import tons of fat from other countries.
: It was then found that much of the stuff was
mixed with butter and sold as pure butter. Now,
severe penalties are imposed if anything not made
exclusively from milk or cream is sold as butter.
Margarine was tried in Australia years ago, but
did not catch on. The poor found ordinary
dripping cheaper, cleaner, and more palatable.
Sir Joseph Banks, the large head of the
Botany Bay discovery party, had, like English
exploiters of to-day, a pretty scheme of his own
for utilising the vast areas of inland Australia.
Syndicates at that date were called “ parties of
adventurers,” and Banks proposed that such par-
ties should be allowed from 100,000 to 1,000,000
acres each, on lease, that they should be em-
powered to make and enforce their own code
of laws, and pay tribute out of the profits. State
law was not to interfere between employes
and employers, except to see that employes were
to be paid a percentage of the profits, “so as to
encourage them to put forth their best energies in
the interest of their employers.” Banks, to do
him justice, spoke and wrote with ability against
the State selling or granting in perpetuity any
part of Australia to individual or company.

“ Von Kotze J. A, Philp goes to con-
siderable trouble in his defence of Chinese immi-
gration and racial inter-marriages. But, though
he may have proved the case entirely to his own
satisfaction, I, and thousands besides me, hold
opposite opinions, and can bring quite as many
proofs for our contention that the Chow is an
evil, as the rather meagre statistics of Mr. Philp.
Anyhow—that isn’t the point at all! The Mon-
golians are fundamentally different from our race,
like the opposite pole of a magnet, and into the
bargain they are phenomenally frugal, adaptable
and thrifty, and, as such, a grave economical dan-
ger to the Caucasian. It simply comes to this:
Shall Australia be a white or a yellow country?
And, quite apart from the notorious immorality of
the lower classes from “far Kathay,” their lia-
bility to disease, their opium habit, a thousand
other objectionable and perhaps contagious traits,
I prefer, as a white man, to see this country a
white man’s land. I’d rather have Australia
peopled by my own kin, though they be an
awfully bad lot, and tolerably drunk, and pain-
fully prone to illegitimacy and strong language
and sweep-tickets, than the most God-fearing and
puritanical hybrid population on this planet. And
in this light the vitality of the Chinese and the
fertility of their half-castes are only so many more
reasons for the strictest exclusion of the Asiatic.

The Man, the Woman, and the
Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had been waiting more than an hour outside
the School of Arts; waiting with what sickening
anxiety only her own heart knew. The ’buses
plied in monotonous regularity on their way to
Paddington and Woollahra, and as each one
passed she bent forward to read its destination, in
the hope that the little group of men smoking on
the pavement would think her an intending
passenger. The Post-office chime rang out the
quarter. Seven o’clock, h.e had said. It was now
a quarter past eight. What could have kept him ?
He had changed lately. Did he care less? No,
surely not; his letters breathed the same love and
devotion; yet this was the third time he had
failed in his appointment. Her mind reviewed
with startling clearness every detail of their
friendship during the past year. It had been
such an ideal comradeship. They had passed
hours together discussing art and books; they
had frequented debates and lectures on social and
intellectual problems, drifting along the platonic
stream, unconscious of the ocean of passion to
which it led —till one day.
One day, when a trivial circumstance had
broken down the walls of self-repression, and the
man had poured' out passionate words of love, of
loneliness and despair, to which the tide of love,
welling up in the woman’s heart, had responded.
Even in the midst of the happiness the know-
ledge of his love brought her, her heart had
ached, as she listened to the broken sentences
telling of the man’s wretchedness, his craving for
sympathy, his lost ideals, his wasted past.
“ I will make it all up to you,” she had whis-
pered ; “ we will be strong together, to live and
work for what we know to be true. ” Her thoughts
had been so busy with the past that a touch on
the arm made her turn with a start to find the
man at her side.
“ I am sorry, dear, to have kept you waiting.
I could not come Sooner, I am in great trouble. I
was afraid you would have gone, but I wanted to
see you so desperately that I came on the chance
of finding you still here. Let us walk on and find
a seat somewhere. You must be tired, and I
have so much to say to you.” He placed the
hand he still held on his arm, and they walked
away together.
“ I am not tired now,” the woman said ; “ we
can walk where the streets are quieter, and you
can tell rue the trouble.”
The man was silent as they turned into Market-
street and crossed the tram-line into the Park,
then he said : “ Let us walk to our favorite spot,
Gratia, and sit where we sat that night when I
told you I loved you. I think there I could have
courage to tell you my wretched story.”
“ Very well, Paul. But, dear, why should you
mind telling me? I love you. No matter how
dreadful, how hideous, how humiliating it may
be, tell me all. I am strong enough to bear it.”
They walked on for some time in silence through
the gates, down the avenue, till they reached
a seat at the end of a path overlooking the water.
Then the man said: “ Have you noticed any
difference in me lately, Gratia ? ”
“ Yea, I have thought at times you were sorry
for having spoken ; for having told me that you
loved me; that perhaps you had begun to realise
you had made a mistake.”
The man looked at her. “ I could never realise
that, Gratia. It sounds like the usual thing to say
I never knew what love was till I met you ; but
it is true —I never did.”
The girl stroked his hand gently. “ What is
the change then, Paul ? ”
“ There is no change in my affection, dear ; it
is simply that I am anxious and wretched.
Gratia, I am married.”
For a few seconds the woman’s heart seemed to
stop beating, then she said, “ When were you
married ?”
“ Seven years ago.”
“ Are there any children ? ”
“Yes, there are two.”
A pause, in which the sound of happy laughter
floated up from the waterside. The woman looked
at the man. “ Why did you make me love you,
Paul ? Do you remember, long ago, I told you 1
was afraid to lovfe, because I was not like other
women—l should care very badly. You knew
me ; you knew this was true; why did you do it?”
“Gratia,” the man said, passionately, “listen
to me. lam not all bad. I have been to blame,
but not wilfully. When I first met you at the
Debating Club, I had no thought of our ever fal-
ling in love with one another. It was your mind
I admired ; an intellectual friendship that I
sought. Then, as I got to see more of you—to
know you better —I began to realise that it was
you yourself that I wanted. I struggled to do
what was right; I tried to avoid you, to keep
from the club. I could not. I am not a weak
man, but my love was too strong for me, and—
you know the rest.”
Another pause ; then the woman spoke. - “Do
you wish to tell mo any more ? Your wife ?
Where is she ? Are you separated ? Do you see
her ? ”
“ No. We are not legally separated. She has
been living in the country with her people for
some years. We never got on together. Our
tastes and interests were quite opposed. Our mar-
riage was a mistake ; I never loved her; it was
merely passion, and was soon over.”
“ Why have you told me of her to-night, Paul ? ”
“ Because she has heard ot you, and is jealous.”
“ Then she loves you ? ”
“ She is not capable of love as we understand it.”
“As we understand it ? How do we under-
stand it ? ”
“ That is what we have to decide to-night,
Gratia. My wife is returning to Sydney, and
either our friendship must end, and our lives drift
apart, or we must enter upon another relationship.
I cannot divorce my wife, and I am bound to sup-
port her. But my marriage with her is merely
legal; my relationship with you would be marriage
in the highest and most moral sense. She has
brought the vilest accusations against me, she
threatens to sue for a judicial separation ; it is the
knowledge of how she would ruin your career, at
present so bright and promising, which troubles
me. ”
“ And in what way, Paul, does the plan you
suggest minimise the danger? You fear for my
career, and yet you propose that I should take a
step which would give color to your wife’s accusa-
tions, and which would ruin my career, as you
understand ruin, most effectually.”
“ But, Gratia, we need not stay here. The world
is wide. We could begin our new life in another
country.”
The woman looked into the man’s eyes.
“ Paul,” she said, “ the old life would follow us;
I should be a woman with a past, and without a
future.”
“Your future would be mine, Gratia.”
“For how long, Paul?”
“ Gratia,” the man said, “don’t you love me
enough to make some little sacrifice ? ”
The woman gazed out upon the moonlit water
without speaking.
“ Little one,” he went on softly, “ you have
always said you would defy conventionality for
love’s sake. We need each other, darling ; we
could not face life with no hope of meeting
again.”
The woman shivered as the soft tones continued.
“ Our love belongs to the highest part of us, dear ;
only sordid, vulgar minds could associate it with
shame. Think of Shelley, of George Eliot, of
other cultured minds, whose love was spiritual
and ideal, and who dared to live in defiance of
social laws.” The woman bent her head in her
hands. When she looked up her face seemed
altered, as if all joy had passed from her life.
Then she said, “ I will not go with you.”
The man started as though he would speak, but
the woman went on unheedingly, “ I will not go
with you. Not because I fear social ostracism,
but because I fear you. I cannot feel sure of you.
To take up life with you as you suggest would be
the most solemn act of my life. I could not try
it as an experiment; I should have to feel certain
I had made no mistake, in linking my life with
yours. Is it one of the essential differences of our
natures that what to me would be a sacrament,
might come to be regarded by you as an episode.”
The man spoke angrily. “So you doubt me?
I might have guessed that when it came to the
test your love would not be strong enough to
fight conventionality. The new woman, after all,
is very much like the old ; she has theories enough,
but hasn’t the courage to carry them out.”
A change passed over Gratia’s face as the man
spoke. Was it possible he could so misunder-
stand her? She rose from the seat shivering.
“ It is getting late, I must catch my tram.”
Together they retraced their steps along the
path fraught with so many memories, the man
still pleading, the woman silently listening. At
the corner of Bent-street they parted, the man to
his chambers, the woman to her lodgings.
All through that night she lay, with eyes wide
with misery, thinking.
* * * •
A week later, at dusk, Gratia entered her study
to find a woman waiting. The woman looked at
her—“ I am Paul Grayson’s wife. I received this
letter from him a few days ago ; I want you to
read it.”
Gratia read it. The eyes of the two women
met.
“You love your husband, Mrs. Grayson?”
Gratia said slowly.
“ Yes, I idolise him. I know his weaknesses,
and yet I love him. My married life has been a
martyrdom. Time after time he has left me for a
newer and more attractive face; but he has
always come back to me and I have forgiven. I
have lived for the hope that one day I might win
him back altogether. Two years ago he failed in
business, and we were terribly poor. I went to
the country with my two little children, and
worked and slaved and sacrificed, not only to
keep them, but to keep him. I have managed to
do it. A fortnight ago the management of a city
business was offered me. My husband asked me
to defer my decision for a few days Then he
wrote to me and I came to town.”
Gratia looked on the letter lying on the table
between them. “ When I came down,” the wife
continued, “I had only bitterness in my heart
towards you. Last night, I saw you were adver-
tised to speak at a meeting on the ‘ Woman Ques-
tion.’ I went to hear you. I thought, if I could
see you, I would know better what to do. As you
spoke, I felt you were sincere in all you said ; you
did not seem a heartless woman, so I determined
to come and speak to you frankly, to ask you not
to ruin my poor chance of ultimate happiness.”
Gratia listened intently, her face in the shadow.
“Then,” she said, “don’t you think a woman’s
chance of happiness very uncertain when she
stakes it on the love of a liar and a coward ? ”
The wife’s face flushed. “He is not that,” she
replied. “He may be weak, selfish, fickle ; but
not a liar, not a coward ; and, even if he were?
Oh ! can’t you see I love him ? lam his wife. I
could not leave off loving him, whatever he did.”
Again Gratia looked at the letter. “In this,”
she went on, touching it lightly, “he tells you
that, though he admired me, he had no thought
of love till I told him I loved him. That, by
reason of my magnetic personality, I had influence
over him which he could not resist. That he
wished to be true to you, that his feeling for me
was merely a mood—a passing mood ; and he asks
you to come to Sydney to save him from himself—
and me. A brave man’s letter, surely ? ”
The sarcasm fell unheeded on the Wife’s ears.
“ Yes,” she said, “ it was brave of him to confess
so freely. I feel he really means to keep the fresh
page turned this time ; if only you will leave him
to me,” the wife continued, eagerly, passionately.
“ You do not love him ; you cannot love him as I
do. Clever women like you, whose interests are
wholly given to public affairs, cannot realise the
part love plays in the ordinary woman’s life. You
have a great future, people say ; as for me, with-
out my husband, I have no future ! ”
Gratia bent forward, speaking slowly, “There
is something worse in life than losing the one we
love. To set up an ideal and worship it; and
then suddenly, without warning, to see it shat-
tered at your feet—so that you lose hope, and
trust, and belief in men and women, is a greater
tragedy. ”
She spoke as though she were thinking aloud;
as though she had forgotten the presence of the
wife. Only for a moment, however. Then she
rose —
“ Mrs. Grayson,” she said, “ I think we under-
stand each other as far as this matter is con-
cerned. You have my assurance that in no way
will I come between you and your husband.”
The wife broke in eagerly : “ If he does not see
you, he will soon forget; will you promise me not
to write, not to see him, not to offer any explana-
tion ? ”
A quiver passed over Gratia’s face. “ I pro-
mise. From this moment he will be dead to me.
I think there is no more to say.”
The wife passed out of the room into the street,
with a great load off her heart, and her mind
busy with plans for the home she had already
pictured her husband and children sharing.
The woman sat alone in the darkness till far
into the night; then she lit the gas, seated herself
at her desk, and went on with her article for the
“Pioneer journal” on “The Relations of the
Sexes. ”

Hardly a reigning dynasty in Europe is
properly of the people it governs. In Britain—
Germans ; Russia, Denmark, and . Greece—llol-
steiners ; Spain—Bourbons ; Sweden—French ;
Austria —Lorrainers ; Germany—Suabians ; Italy
—Savoyards; Belgium—Coburgers.
Out of a N.S.W. dairy herd ,of 15, eight
apparently healthy cows singled out by the tuber-
culin-test were' discovered, upon slaughter, to be
in an advanced stage of consumption ! The
pleasing little fact that the milk of these animals
was being unsuspectingly consumed as wholesome
opens up a cheerful vista of disease-possibility,
and indicates the crying necessity for having the
tuberculin-test made legally imperative. '
Lady Elizabeth Biddulph, a prominent
English Cold-Tea apostle, lately told an audience
that she had learnt that Sirdar Kitchener' had sent
back all the beer that had been ordered to the
front with the soldiers. “ Therefore,” continued
her ladyship in a triumphant tone, “they had
fought and had achieved a great temperance as
well as a great and brilliant military victory.”
Wild applause. Considering that the Dervishes
conquered in the great temperance victory referred
to were teetotallers by religion, and had never
touched strong drink in all their lives, Lady Bid-
dulph’s rejoiceful whoop sounds funny. It was
really the Dervishes who should have won, accord-
ing to temperance logic. But one forgets : there
is no temperance logic.

A CONSTANT SUFFERER.
Mrs. THOMAS BISHOP Tells of Her Illness.—Serious Consequences Feared.
Many Doctors Fail, but BILE BEANS Eifect a Speedy and Thorough Cure.
If any one thought has been in my mind more than another for the past few months, it has been one
of gratitude to the makers of Bile Beans, and I am only too delighted to have an opportunity of
publicly stating this.” Thus replied Mrs. Thomas Bishop, of James-street, Hamilton, when a re-
porter waited upon her the other day in reference to the remarkable recovery. Mrs. Bishop keeps a
store m Hamilton, and is the wife of Mr. Thomas Bishop, the well-known blacksmith of that district.
at 6 1 -n- i a ' Wlde l y res P e °ted in the Newcastle district, having resided there for about nine years.
Mrs. Bishop had, up to a short time ago, suffered so much from various disorders that she was begin-
i liig to fear serious consequences. To day, however, she is bright and lively, without a pain or an
i.che. this wonderful change in her system has, she says, all been brought about by the use of Bile
eans for Biliousness. The reporter was astonished to hear that a lady looking the picture of health
and happiness could have been so great a sufferer, and he ventured to inquire into the case. “Though
not at death s door exactly, I have been so bad,” said Mrs, Bishop, “ that nothing I could do or get
seemed to give me any relief. It was a misery for me to walk about. Night after night I passed
a.most without any sleep, to rise in the morning utterly unrefreshed. Terrible pains assailed me in
the back and between the shoulders, causing me intense suffering. I think it was my liver that caused
this. As tune went on the weary and languid feeling grew more acute, headaches were frequent, and
1
it was a trouble for me to take my meals. I was beginning to
think the illness would be my last, because I had lost 141 b. in
weight in three months, and was very weak.”
“ Had you consulted doctors as to your condition ?” suggested
the reporter.
“Yes; they said it was indigestion. They prescribed for me,
and, when I got no permanent relief, I was advised to go away
for a change of air.”
“ How long had you been suffering? ”
“The pain in my back I first severely felt twelve months ago
last Easter; but the breakdown of my physical vitality, loss of
appetite, and general weakness only came on about eight months
ago. Prom that time forward I always wanted to be lying down,
only, however, to feel more tired when I got up again. Then,
when I would try to walk about, the pains in the middle of my
back would be something unbearable, and latterly I had developed
a soreness in the abdomen, which caused me some concern. I in-
tended to have the change of air the doctor spoke about, and went
to Lake Macquarie. I took bad in the house we had taken, how-
ever, and was glad to get back to Hamilton. At this time every-
thing was a trouble to me—eating, drinking, walking, talking, and
even thinking being equally burdensome. I was at my wits’ end
to know what to do to get relief.”
<t at^ 0 recomrnpn ded the Bile Beans, then? ” queried the newspaper man.

How soon did you experience relief ? ”
Practically at once. The first dose had rather a powerful effect ; but I realised that this might

INFLUENZA AND COLDS.
Nothing so suddenly obstructs the perspiration as sudden transitions from heat to cold. Heat
ranrj e s trie blood,.quickens the circulation and increases the perspiration, but when these are suddenly
checked, the consequences must be bad. The most common cause of disease is obstructed perspiration
or what commonly goes by the name of catching cold. Coughs, colds, sore throat, etc., if attended to
m time are easily subdued, but if allowed to run their own course, generally prove the forerunner of
more dangerous diseases. Nine-tenths of the consumptives date their affliction from a neglected cold,
and the diseases that are caused by wet feet, damp clothes, or exposure are more numerous than are
generally supposed. One of the most efficacious medicines for the prevention of diseases of the throat
and lungs is Bile Beans.

The Tugs of Simpsonville.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was dirty, dark, and artful and they called
him “ Saltbush Bill,”
But we didn’t recognise him when he came to
Simpsonville ;
It’s a sort of one-horse township out beyond the
Cobar track,
Where the sun’s a perfect scorcher and the dust
would choke a black !
Hot ? Great Scot !
It was Hell, with some improvements, worse than
Booligal, a lot!
Saltbush Bill arrived at sundown ; called for
“ Hennessy’s three-star,”
And he shouted for the jackeroos a-standingin the
bar,
And he introduced the subject when he’d liquored
up. ' Says he :
4“l’m no English duke or nobleman a-tracking
round ; not me !
Shout ? No doubt!
But I ain’t a bloomin' squatter nor a shearer just
cut out!
“ I’m in Simpsonville on business and I claim to
represent,
The most wonderful neuralgia cure that any could
invent,
And it’s known as ‘ Brown’s Neuralgia Dice’;
the price a bob a die
And you rub it where the pain is and the pain is
bound to fly !
Sell ? oh well,
Just you wait till I have finished and youll have
a chance to tell ! ”
Then he brings a pickle-bottle and he puts it on
the bar ;
(It was full of peas and fastened down) and says :
“Now, there you are !
I’m the liberalest bagman that was ever on the
rounds;
If you guess how many peas is there you get five
blanky pounds !
Fair ? and square !
And the nearest guess will get the gonce as sure as
you are there ! ”
Well of course we goes to rush it, but he says :
“ One moment there !
I am no escaped loo natie nor eccentric million-
aire !
I’m no travelling convalescent and I ain’t been
very ill,
Nor come to view the scenery surrounding Simp-
sonville !
Yes ! you guess,
But you have to buy a bob’s worth of the cure !
Well here’s success ! ”
“ Now I want a hundred guesses, which will make
the fiver sure,
And the landlord holds the money just to see you
all secure,
And I leaves a hundred samples of the cure inside
the bar
Which he sells, and pays the money to the winner.
There you are !
Me ? I’ll gee ! 1
I must introduce the remedy in other towns vou
see ! ” ’ J
In the morning came a swaggie with “Matilda”
’cross the flat,
Whom we recognised immediate as a bloke called
Jack the Rat;
And he listened to the story, then went over to
the store
And he bought dry peas in bagfuls till there
wasn’t any more.
Rot ? ’Twas not!
Why you haven’t got a notion what a head that
bloke had got!
Now it first struck Joe the Spieler it would be as
good as gold,
For to get a pickle-bottle and see just what it
would hold.
He was always on for pointing, and as artful as
you please ;
But he went all round the township and he
couldn’t get no peas !
See? Not'a pea !
It was just the same with Jackson and with
Dogherty and me !
And the bobs they kept on coming in ; the time
was drawing nigh;
Joe was savage, so was Dogherty and Jackson, so
was I!
Spare me days, I think the lot of us was looking
after peas !
When one day I meets that Jack the Rat as simple
i as you please.
ft “Me? Got peas ? ”
Yes, 111 sell you, at two bob a pint, as many as
you please ! ” (
It was something like six times the price, but what
was I to do ?
So I bought ’em, and found afterwards that others
bought’em, too.
Jack the Rat was so delighted with his honest
trade’s success
That he shouted for a dozen, and he also took a
guess.. .
Swear? Well, there,
It would simply freeze the marrow in a bullock-
driver’s hair !
When we had the bottle opened, it was not half-
full of peas,
For a corncob in the centre took the space up, if you
please i
And the clever blokes who measured, they were
out by half-a-mile;
It was Jack the Rat who won it, and he wore a
peaceful smile !
Toast ? Great Ghost !•
In about a week the landlord got the letter by the
post.
“We had things to do in Melbourne, so we
thought we’d get away,
But desire, as we are leaving, most respectfully to
say
That we’re thankful for the kindness of the tugs
of Simpsonville,
And remain, yours most respectful, Jack the Rat
and Saltbush Bill.”
Catch ’em ? No hope !
And the “ remedy ” was little squares cut out of
bars of soap //

W. T. GOODGE.
A Woman of Conscience.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Sunday morning at a South African sea-side re-
sort. One of the occupants of the hotel balcony
was a slender, soulful-looking woman, with lovely
eyes, and that restful, saintly look which comes of
black hair smoothly parted on a head arched over
the region where benevolence and ideality are sup-
posed to dwell. Her companion was a dark,
handsome Afrikander, and I, a perfect stranger,
jumped naturally enough to the conclusion that I
was looking at a newly - wedded pair. . The
woman’s dark eyes gazed long and lovingly
straight into the man’s, and her delicate hand
rested in his, as he talked softly to her in Dutch,
with that gentle tolerance which one uses to a
loved but foolish child. To my surprise, she ever
and anon cast looks of concern at me, and would
say something in an expostulatory way, whereat
her companion would laugh and reply as one
who says, “ Oh, never mind; it doesn’t matter.”
At last, however, at the lady’s request, the gentle-
man came over to me and with profuse apologies
explained that his fair companion was very much
concerned to know what I was reading. I said it
was “ Trilby,” the latest boom in silliness. “ Ah;
Dreelpee ? Eet ees a book of de religious ? No ! ”
Then, looking at the cover—“ She haf de Zal vation
Army coat, is it not ? ” I explained that the coat
was an old military one, and added that the lady
had had numerous admirers, any one of whom
would have bought her a sealskin jacket, had the
author deemed it advisable. “ Tank you ver’
mooch,” he said. “My vife she say to me, ‘I
vonder vat dat zhentleman ees reading on Zunday.
I hope eet ees hees Bybel.’ I say ‘I dink it some
Enklesh religious book.’ But she say ‘ No, Henri,
eet ees some vicked novel; do go and schpeak to
heem.’ Ye mens of de voridt,”he added, apologeti-
cally, “ ve don’t care for zomedings like dat, but de
lady, brought oop in de high Detch family, it is
ver particular.” I said, if it would ease her mind,
I would put away the book; whereupon he took
me over and introduced me, at the same time con-
veying the news of my self-denial. Her beautiful
saintly eyes gleamed gratefully when I announced
that, for her sake, I would read no more that day,
but she would not even touch “Trilby” when I
offered the volume for inspection. Then talk
turned to novelists in general, and I instanced
Olive Schreiner as a writer of whom her country
should be proud.
My fair friend gave a shudder of disgust. “ Oh,
dat dreadful voman; she write dat disgusting
book, de ‘ African Farm.’” “ Why disgusting ? ”
I asked, in surprise. “Oh, it is a indecent book ;
vere de single girl haf a baby,” she replied. “ No
respectable high Detch lady haf dat book in de
house ; it corrupt de mind ; I vould not read it
for de vorldt.”
“Ah, well,” I said, “then you mustn’t read
‘ Trilby,’ for she is a single girl of the same sort,
only more so.” This and other talk filled up the
time till dinner. I got much good advice about
keeping my thoughts on God during the holy Sab-
bath-day, and accompanied by such tender looks
from the saintly eyes that I was “almost per-
suaded” to become a Sabbatarian.
She and Henri passed the afternoon in their
room—rather to my surprise—but, being new t o
the ways of piety, I came to the conclusion that
anything she did must be right.
They were Saturday-to-Monday guests, and
when we parted next day I took' the incident to
be closed; but about three months later I was
visiting an inland town hundreds of miles away,
and was asked by some of the farmers of the
neighborhood to address a meeting and explain
why Australian farmers could sell wheat in Africa
at 20s. a bag, while Dutch farmers could not niake
it pay at 30s. One of them, a burly, bandy-
legged man of about 40, gave me a hearty
invitation to stay with him at his farm
for the intervening week. And when an hour’s
swift drive had brought us to his house,
a lady came out to meet us. “ Dese is my vife,”
said Van Kopje, “yust oop from Captown, vere
I seend her every zuinmer from de hot vedder
avay.”
I was astounded. It was she of the de-
mure countenance and saintly eyes! Later,
when Oom Piet went out for the afternoon to
dicker with an Israelite for ostrich-feathers, she
said : “ I am zo tankful you did not make sign of
knowing me before —my husband is so jealous.”
“ What, Henri ? ”
“No! don’t be cruel.” Tears started to her
eyes. It was really most unbecoming conduct.
“Oh, don’t! ” Her hands flashed out, and mine
found them like magnets.
“For Sunday,” I said; “to-day it is Wednes-
day!”
P. ABBOTT.

East London, Cape Colony, So. Africa,
July 11, 1896.
“Shortly after leaving .Johannesburg,.* where wo had resided several
years, 1 cont ract' ! a fever, which left me in a very weak state. I was a
long - time without any improvement, and none ot the various remedies 1
tried, under the doctor’s direction, did me any good. My husband finally
persuaded me to try Ayer’s -Sarsaviri! hi, which cured several acquaintances
of ours who had suffered from impoverished blood and general debility. I
therefore began to lake’ the Sarsaparilla, and its good influence was soon
apparent. Within less than a month I was as well as ever, and could eat
and sleep like a child. Gratefully yours,
Lily Fokbes.”
Mr. Henry Forbes writes in confirmation of his wife’s testimony, and
adds that he had suffered from dyspepsia and loss of appetite, and that he
was cured by
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla.
Beware of worthless substitutes. See that the name— Ayer’s Sarsapa-
rilla-is on the wrapper, and blown in the glass of each bottle.
